No Motion in Remaining Categories, Especially Substance
Author denies motion in six categories, explains substance case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that motion is not proven in substance and the other remaining categories, and why substance's lack of intensity makes its change instantaneous rather than gradual.

Core Idea
Substance cannot change gradually, so it has no motion.

The author continues his analysis of motion by addressing the remaining Aristotelian categories. He asserts that no evidence supports motion in substance, when, relation, possession, action, and passion. Focusing on substance, he explains that because substance cannot admit gradual increase or decrease (intensity), any change in substance occurs all at once, not gradually. This passage clarifies a key limitation on the concept of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the categories
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the six categories listed where motion is not proven.
2
Understand the reasoning for substance
Focus on the explanation for substance. Why does the author say substance's transition is instantaneous? Connect this to the lack of intensity (ishtidad).
Think about what 'intensity' means: gradual increase or decrease.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider the broader implication: motion (gradual change) is not universal. Some categories of being, like substance, change in an instant.
This challenges the idea that all change is gradual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولا ثبت للحركة في باقي المقولات . يعني لا دليل على ثبوت الحركة في الجوهر والمتى والإضافة والملك وأن يفعل وأن ينفعل، بل ربما يقام الدليل على نفيها . أما الجوهر فلأنه بعد ثبوت الكون وتوارد الصور على المادة الواحدة، فالانتقال إلى كل منها دفعى، لأن الجوهر لا يقبل الاشتداد فلا يكون حدوثه على التدريج
He said: And motion is not proven in the remaining categories. Meaning, there is no proof for the existence of motion in substance, when, relation, possession, action, and passion; rather, proof may be established for its negation. As for substance, because after the establishment of contingency and the succession of forms upon one matter, the transition to each of them is instantaneous, because substance does not admit intensity, so its occurrence is not gradual.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof regarding motion in the remaining Aristotelian categories. The author, following the statement 'He said', declares that motion is not proven in the remaining categories. He then explains that this means there is no proof (daleel) for motion in substance (al-jawhar), when (mata), relation (al-idafa), possession (al-milk), action (an yaf'al), and passion (an yanfa'il), and in fact proof may be given to negate it. He specifically addresses substance: after the establishment of contingency (al-kawn) and the succession of forms (tawarrud al-suwar) upon one matter (al-madda al-wahida), the transition from one form to the next is instantaneous (daf'i), because substance does not admit intensity (ishtidad) — it cannot change gradually; its occurrence is all at once. Therefore, there is no motion in substance.

Key Terms
الجوهر
المتى
الإضافة
الملك
أن يفعل وأن ينفعل
الاشتداد
الكون
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that substance changes gradually like other things.
Correction: The author argues that substance cannot admit intensity, so any change in substance is instantaneous, not gradual, and therefore not motion.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that there is no motion in substance?
A) Because substance is eternal and does not change.
B) Because substance does not admit intensity, so its transformations are instantaneous.
C) Because substance belongs to a different category than motion.
D) Because the proof for motion in substance is inconclusive.

Key Takeaway
Substance's lack of intensity means its changes are instantaneous, not gradual, so motion does not apply to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If substance, the very core of your being, changes not gradually but instantly by God's command, what does that mean for your spiritual stagnation? Are you waiting for a slow improvement when an immediate turn to Allah is possible right now? What is holding you back from that instantaneous repentance?
?
You believe that change must be slow—habits, character, faith. But this lesson shows that substance cannot change gradually; it is either one form or another in an instant. Where in your life have you made gradual change an idol, assuming God cannot transform you in a moment? Will you ask Him tonight for an instantaneous shift in your heart?
?
The author proves that substance lacks intensity, so its change is sudden. Do you treat your own soul as if it intensifies and weakens gradually, making excuses for sin? Could it be that you are underestimating God's power to uproot a deep sin instantly? What would it look like for you to plead for that today?

The Non-Independence of Relation: Objection and Answer
Objection and refutation on the non-independence of relative attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of the non-independence of relation (al-idāfa) and why this distinction is crucial for the category of relation in Islamic philosophical theology.

Core Idea
Relation's non-independence means dependence on its subject, not just being a relative accident.

This passage presents a critical objection and its answer regarding the concept of relation (al-idāfa). The objector argues that stating relation lacks independence is sufficient, but the answer refines the meaning by distinguishing between mere relativity and genuine dependence on the subject. This distinction has important consequences for whether contrariety can occur in relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage along with the translation. Notice the structure: the objector makes a claim, then the answer refines the definition of non-independence.
The objector's point is about sufficiency of explanation.
2
Identify the key refinement
The answer states that non-independence does not simply mean being relative, but rather being dependent on the subject in its rulings. Understand why this matters.
The counterexample of space (al-ayn) and position (al-waḍʿ) is used to illustrate.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's support
Ibn Sina is cited to show that contrariety does not apply to relations because relations are not self-subsistent. This reinforces the answer's point about dependence.
Note the categories where contrariety does exist: space, time, position, action, passion.
4
Analyze the hot and cold example
The answer uses the example of hot and cold to show that contrariety in the underlying subject is what matters, not the relation itself.
If relation were independent, it would have its own contrariety, which it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما ذكر في الإضافة من عدم استقلالها لكونها من الأعراض النسبية كاف في الجميع على ما أشار إليه الإمام ولاحاجة إلى ما ذكروا من التطويل والتفصيل . قلنا : ليس معنى عدم استقلال الإضافة مجرد كونها نسبية، وإلا انتقض بالأين والوضع بل معناه كونها تابعة لمعروضها في الأحكام، ولهذا قال ابن سينا بعد إثبات التضاد في الأين ،والمتى ،والوضع، وأن يفعل، وأن ينفعل أن التضاد لا يعرض الإضافة، لأن الإضافات طبايع غير مستقلة بأنفسها، فيمتنع أن يعرض لها التضاد، لأن أقل درجات المعروض أن يكون مستقلاً بتلك المعروضية، وأما كون الأجر [؟] ضداً للأبرد كالحار للبارد فلأن الإضافة لما كانت طبيعة غير مستقلة، بل تابعة لمعروضها وجب أن يكون في هذا الحكم أيضاً ،تابعة وإلا لكانت مستقلة فيه .
For it is said: What was mentioned regarding relation (al-idāfa) – that its non-independence is because it is a relative accident – suffices for all, as the Imām indicated, and there is no need for the length and detail they mentioned. We say: The meaning of the non-independence of relation is not merely that it is relative; otherwise, it would be subverted by space (al-ayn) and position (al-waḍʿ). Rather, its meaning is that it is dependent on its subject in its rulings. And for this reason, Ibn Sīnā said, after establishing that there is contrariety in space, time, position, acting, and being acted upon, that contrariety does not occur in relation, because relations are natures that are not independent in themselves, so it is impossible for contrariety to occur in them, because the least degree of a subject is that it be independent in that subjecthood. And as for the fact that the hotter [؟] is contrary to the colder, like the hot [being contrary] to the cold, it is because relation, since it is a nature that is not independent but rather dependent on its subject, must also be dependent in this ruling; otherwise, it would be independent in it.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the doctrine of relation (al-idāfa). The objector argues that the simple statement that relation lacks independence because it is a relative accident is sufficient, rendering the lengthy discussions of the author unnecessary. The answer refines this: the meaning of non-independence is not merely being relative—if that were the case, other relative accidents like space (al-ayn) and position (al-waḍʿ) would also be considered non-independent, but they are not. Rather, non-independence means that relation follows its subject in its rulings (aḥkām). To support this, the answer cites Ibn Sīnā, who after proving that contrariety (taḍādd) exists in categories like space, time, position, action, and passion, holds that contrariety does not apply to relation because relations are not self-subsistent natures. For contrariety to occur, the subject must be independent, but relations lack that independence. The example of the hot and cold is addressed: their contrariety is not due to the relation itself but because the relation follows the subject in this ruling; otherwise, the relation would be independent in that judgment, which is false.

Key Terms

الأعراض النسبية
التضاد
الاستقلال
الأين
الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the non-independence of relation simply means it is a relative accident, so any relative accident is non-independent.
Correction: The correct meaning is that relation is dependent on its subject in its rulings; otherwise, other relative accidents like space and position would also be non-independent, but they are not.

Checkpoint
What is the correct meaning of the non-independence of relation (al-idāfa) according to the answer?
A) It is a relative accident.
B) It is dependent on its subject in its rulings.
C) It is neither relative nor dependent.
D) It is independent in its rulings.

Key Takeaway
The passage teaches that relation's lack of independence is not merely its being relative, but its dependence on the subject in rulings, which prevents contrariety from occurring directly in relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector in this passage thought a simple explanation was sufficient, but the answer corrected him. Are there Islamic concepts you have accepted without deep understanding, assuming you already know enough? What step will you take today to study one such concept more thoroughly using the Quran and Sunnah?
?
Relation's non-independence means it follows its subject completely. In your life, what is the 'subject' you follow most—your desires, culture, or Allah's commands? If you realized you are following the wrong subject, what concrete change would you make starting this week?
?
Ibn Sīnā argued that contrariety cannot occur in relation because it lacks independence. In your own heart, do you find contradictory intentions—wanting both the world and the Hereafter—because you treat your desires as independent of divine guidance? How can you make your inner intentions dependent on the Quran's priorities?

Classification of Motion: Essential and Accidental Types
The author classifies motion into essential and accidental types and refutes the objection about pulse motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complete classification of motion into essential and accidental categories, and how the pulse motion is accounted for within this scheme.

Core Idea
All motion is either essential or accidental; the pulse is compound compulsory, not a standalone category.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection against the completeness of the threefold division of motion. He presents a detailed taxonomy showing that motion can be exhaustively categorized, and that problematic motions like the pulse fit neatly as compound compulsory motion. By following the classification, you will see how every motion has a defined place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms for motion types.
The passage packs dense information; reading both helps.
2
Identify the two main categories
Find the first division: essential (ذاتية) and accidental (عارضة). Understand that essential motion originates from within the object, accidental from outside.
3
Trace the subdivisions of essential motion
Essential motion is divided into simple (بسيطة) and compound (مركبة). Simple is either voluntary or natural; compound is either vegetative or animal-based (voluntary or compulsory).
The pulse appears as an example of compulsory motion.
4
Understand accidental motion subdivisions
Accidental motion is categorized based on the mover's relation: if part of the moved (accidental voluntary), if natural place (accidental natural), otherwise forced.
5
See how the objection is resolved
The pulse motion is not an extra category but falls under compound compulsory motion, showing the threefold division (essential/accidental, voluntary/natural/forced) is sufficient.
This addresses the claim that pulse motion required a fourth type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من جعل مثل النبض قسماً آخر سماها تسخيرية) . يعني هرب بعضهم عن الإشكال المذكور بمنع انحصار الحركة بالذات في الأقسام الثلاثة، وجعل طريق القسمة أن الحركة إما ذاتية أو عـارضـة والذاتية إن كانت على نهج واحد فبسيطة ، وإلا) ،فمركبة والبسيطة إن كانت تابعة لإرادة، فإرادية (كحركة الفلك) (۱) وإلا فطبيعية كالحركة الهابطة للحجر النازل من الهواء والمركبة إن لم يكن من خواص الحيوانات فنباتية كالنمو، وإن كانت فإما أن تكون تابعة للإرادة وهي الإرادية كالمشي أو لا وهي التسخيرية كالنبض والعارضة إن كان المحرك كجزء من المتحرك فعرضية إرادية أو مكاناً له بالطبع فعرضية طبيعية وإلا فقسرية.
One who makes [a motion] like the pulse another division calls it 'compulsory' (tashkhīriyya). That is, some fled from the mentioned problem by denying the confinement of essential motion to three divisions, and made the method of division that motion is either essential or accidental. The essential, if on a single pattern, is simple; otherwise compound. The simple, if dependent on will, is voluntary (like the motion of the sphere); otherwise natural, like the downward motion of a stone falling from air. The compound, if it is not among the properties of animals, is vegetative (like growth); and if it is [among animal properties], then it is either dependent on will and it is voluntary (like walking) or not, and it is the compulsory (like the pulse). The accidental, if the mover is like a part of the moved, is accidental voluntary; or if it is its place by nature, is accidental natural; otherwise forced.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of motion and addresses an objection. The author begins by mentioning that some scholars proposed a fourth type of motion for the pulse to avoid a perceived incompleteness in the threefold division. He then provides an exhaustive division: motion is either essential (ذاتية) or accidental (عارضة). Essential motion is either simple (بسيطة) – if uniform – or compound (مركبة). Simple motion is either voluntary (إرادية) like celestial motion, or natural (طبيعية) like a stone falling. Compound motion, if not specific to animals, is vegetative (نباتية) like growth; if animal-specific, it is either voluntary (like walking) or 'compulsory' (تسخيرية) like the pulse. Accidental motion is subdivided based on the mover's relation to the moved: if the mover is part of the moved, it is accidental voluntary; if the mover is the natural place, accidental natural; otherwise forced (قسرية). The pulse motion is thus classified under compound compulsory motion, showing the threefold division is sufficient.

Key Terms
حركة
ذاتية
عارضة
بسيطة
مركبة
إرادية
طبيعية
نباتية
تسخيرية
قسرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pulse motion is a distinct fourth category of motion outside the three essential types (voluntary, natural, forced).
Correction: The pulse is classified as compound compulsory essential motion, which falls under the broader categories; the threefold division remains complete.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the motion of the pulse (النبض) is classified under which type?
A) Voluntary motion (إرادية)
B) Natural motion (طبيعية)
C) Compulsory motion (تسخيرية)
D) Forced motion (قسرية)

Key Takeaway
The pulse motion is not a separate category but falls under compound compulsory motion, demonstrating the completeness of the threefold division of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a phenomenon in your own life that seems to defy easy categorization—like a struggle you cannot explain as purely voluntary, natural, or forced—are you quick to invent a new label rather than seeking a deeper understanding? This lesson shows that the pulse, though unique, fits within an existing framework. What hidden assumption might you be holding that prevents you from seeing the completeness of the categories derived from revelation and reason? Write down one area of your aqeedah where you are tempted to add an extra category, and commit to studying it further before concluding it is separate.
?
The author refutes those who fled from the problem by denying the confinement of essential motion to three divisions. In your study of Islam, have you ever avoided a difficult question by creating an easy way out instead of grappling with the established principles? How can you cultivate the intellectual honesty to face such problems directly, trusting that the tradition has answers even if they require deep reflection? This week, identify one difficult theological question you have set aside and resolve to take the first step toward addressing it using the classical sources.
?
Compulsory motion (like the pulse) is a reminder that not all actions are under our will, yet they are still part of a divine order. How does this shape your understanding of your own limitations and dependence on Allah? If even your heartbeat is beyond your direct control, what does that say about your need to rely on Him for every moment? Take a minute to silently acknowledge one aspect of your life where you try to maintain the illusion of total control, and ask Allah to help you surrender that to Him.

Essential and Accidental Attachments of Motion
Taftazani defines essential and accidental attachments of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential and accidental attachments of motion and how they affect the essence or identity of motion.

Core Idea
Three attachments define motion's essence; three others only affect its identity.

In this passage, al-Taftazani lays out a key principle in kalam physics: motion attaches to six things, but not all are equal. The first three—location, beginning, end—are like essential attributes; changing them changes the nature of motion itself. The last three—moving object, mover, time—only affect its identity. This distinction is crucial for understanding when motions are considered the same or different.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the technical terms: ما فيه, ما منه, ما إليه, المتحرك, المحرك, الزمان.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the six attachments
List the six things motion attaches to. Three are called essential, three accidental. Find the sentence that states this division.
The author says 'attachment to three of them... is in the position of the essential'.
3
Understand the essential vs accidental distinction
Understand that changing an essential attachment changes the essence of motion; changing an accidental attachment changes only the identity (هوية). The moving object is an exception: its difference does not harm the continuous identity.
Notice the exception: 'سوى المتحرك'.
4
Reflect on the meaning of specific and individual unity
The passage says specific unity requires unity of the three essential attachments, individual unity requires unity of everything except the mover. Think about why the mover is excluded.
This is a key point for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
اختلاف الحركات بالماهية والعوارض واتحادها بالشخص
قال: المبحث الرابع:
تعلق الحركة بما فيه، وما منه وما إليه يكاد يكون ذاتياً يوجب الاختلاف فيه. الاختلاف في الماهية ، وبما عداها عرضياً يوجب الاختلاف فيه. الاختلاف في الهوية فقط، سوى المتحرك، فإن اختلافه لا يقدح في هويتها الاتصالية الواحدة بالذات، وإن كانت بتوهم فيها كثرة باعتبار النسب إلى المحركات فلذا كانت وحدتها النوعية بوحدة الأمور الثلاثة والشخصية بوحدة ما سوى المحرك).
اختلاف الحركات قد يكون بالماهية، وقد يكون بالعوارض، واتحادها قد يكون بالشخص، وقد يكون بالنوع ، وقد يكون بالجنس، ثم قد يوصف بالتضاد (۱)، وقد يوصف بالانقسام، فيشير في هذا المبحث إلى بيان ماهية ذلك (٢) الحركة . وقد سبق أن الحركة تتعلق بأمور ستة .
فاتفقوا على أن تعلقها بثلاثة منها وهي ما فيه وما منه وما إليه بمنزلة الذاتي يختلف باختلاف ماهية الحركة ، وتعلقها بالثلاثة الباقية بمنزلة العرضى لا يختلف باختلاف ماهية الحركة، بل باختلاف المحرك لا يختلف هويتها أيضاً،
(۱) التضاد هو التباين والتقابل التام، وضد الشيء خلافه، ومن شرط الضدين أن يكونا من جنس واحد كالبياض والسواد . (۲) في (ب) بزيادة لفظ (ذلك ) .
٤٣٢
فبنوا على ذلك، أنه إذا اتحد المبدأ والمنتهى وما فيه الحركة لتحدث الحركة بالنوع ، وإن اختلف المتحرك أو المحرك أو الزمان، لأن تنوع المعروضات أو الأسباب لا يوجب تنوع العوارض والمسببات لجواز قيام نوع منها كالحرارة بموضوعين مختلفي الماهية كالإنسان والفرس وحصوله المؤثرين مختلفين كالنار والشمس، وبهذا يظهر أن لا أثر للاختلاف بالقسر والطبع والإرادة. فالحركة الصاعدة للنار طبعاً، وللحجر قسراً وللطير إرادة، لا تختلف نوعاً . وأما الأزمنة فلا يتصور فيها اختلاف الماهية ولو فرض فلا خفاء في جواز إحاطتها بحقيقة واحدة والتمسك بأنها عارضة للحركة واختلاف العارض لا اختلاف المعروض ضعيف لما سبق من أن هذا التعلق بالزمان غير تعلق الحركة التي جعل الزمان عارضاً لها (۱) ، فإنها إنما هي حركة الفلك الأعظم، وإذا اختلفت المبدأ والمنتهى اختلفت الحركة، وإن كان مـا فيـه واحداً أما في الأين فكالحركة الصاعدة مع الهابطة وأما في الكيف فكالحركة من البياض إلى السواد على طريق التصفر ثم التحمر ثم التسود مع الحركة من السواد إلى البياض على طريق التحمر ثم التصفر ثم التبيض . وكذا إذا اختلف ما فيه وإن اتحد المبدأ والمنتهى كالحركة من نقطة إلى نقطة على الاستقامة معها على الانحناء وكالحركة من البياض إلى السواد على طريق الأخذ في الصفرة ثم الحمرة ثم السواد معها على طريق الأخذ في الخضرة ثم النيلية ثم السواد وما ذكر في المواقف من أنه لا بد من وحدة ما فيه وما منه وما إليه، إذ لو اختلف ما فيه اختلف النوع كالتسخن والتسود ليس على ما ينبغي لأن هذا إنما يصح للتمثيل (۲) دون التعليل (۳)، وكأنه أراد أنه يختلف النوع عند اختلاف مجرد ما فيه كما يختلف عند اختلاف الأمور الثلاثة مثل التسخن والتسود أو كان الأصل كالتسخن والتبرد فصحف إلى
(۱) في (أ) بزيادة لفظ (لها ) .
(۲) التمثيل : مثل الشيء بالشيء سواه وشبهه به وجعله على مثاله فالتمثيل هو التصوير والتشبيه . (۳) علل الشيء بين علته وأثبته بالدليل وأعله سقاه ثانية، وأعله الله ، أصابه بعلة والتعليل : تبين علة الشيء
٤٣٣
The Fourth Investigation: The difference of motions in essence and accidents and their unity in individual. He said: The Fourth Investigation: The attachment of motion to what it is in, what it is from, and what it is to is almost essential, necessitating difference in it: difference in essence; and to what is other than them is accidental, necessitating difference in it: difference only in identity, except the moving thing, for its difference does not detract from its continuous identity that is one in essence, even if there is imagined multiplicity in it in consideration of relations to movers. Thus its specific unity is by the unity of the three things and its individual unity by the unity of what is other than the mover. Differences of motions may be in essence, and may be in accidents; and their unity may be in individual, and may be in species, and may be in genus; then they may be described by opposition (1), and may be described by division. So he indicates in this investigation to the clarification of the essence of that motion. And it has preceded that motion attaches to six things. So they agreed that its attachment to three of them—namely what it is in, what it is from, and what it is to—is in the position of the essential, differing with the difference of the essence of motion; and its attachment to the remaining three is in the position of the accidental, not differing with the difference of the essence of motion, but with the difference of the mover; its identity also does not differ. (1) Opposition is complete separation and complete contrast; the opposite of a thing is its contrary; among the conditions of two opposites is that they be of one genus, like whiteness and blackness. (2) In (B) an addition of the word 'that'. So they built upon that that if the beginning and the end and what the motion is in are united, then the motion occurs in species, even if the moving thing or the mover or the time differs, because the diversification of substrates or causes does not necessitate the diversification of accidents and effects, due to the permissibility of one type of them—like heat—subsisting in two subjects differing in essence, like man and horse, and its occurrence from two differing effects, like fire and sun. By this it appears that there is no effect of difference by compulsion, nature, and will. So the ascending motion of fire by nature, and of stone by compulsion, and of bird by will, do not differ in species. As for times, difference in essence is not conceivable; and even if assumed, there is no concealment of the permissibility of encompassing them by one reality. And the holding fast that it is accidental to motion and that difference of accident does not necessitate difference of the subject is weak, because of what preceded that this attachment to time is not the attachment of motion which made time an accident to it (1); for it is only the motion of the outermost sphere. And if the beginning and end differ, the motion differs, even if what it is in is one. As for in 'where', like the ascending motion with the descending; and as for in 'quality', like the motion from whiteness to blackness via the path of becoming yellow, then red, then black, along with the motion from blackness to whiteness via the path of becoming red, then yellow, then white. Likewise if what it is in differs, even if the beginning and end are united, like the motion from a point to a point on a straight line along with it on a curve, and like the motion from whiteness to blackness via the path of taking on yellowness, then redness, then blackness, along with it via the path of taking on greenness, then blueness, then blackness. And what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif that it is necessary to have unity of what it is in, what it is from, and what it is to, for if what it is in differs, the species differs—like heating and blackening—is not as it should be, because this is only valid for exemplification (2) not for demonstration (3). And it is as if he intended that the species differs upon the difference of merely what it is in just as it differs upon the difference of the three things, like heating and blackening; or the original was like heating and cooling, and it was corrupted to... (1) In (A) an addition of the word 'to it'. (2) Exemplification is likening one thing to another and making it similar to it; so exemplification is depiction and analogy. (3) Explanation of a thing is to make its cause clear and prove it by evidence; and to 'illa' is to water it a second time; and 'a'illahu' means He afflicted him with an illness. And explanation is making clear the cause of something.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion in kalam about motion and its differentiation and unity. The author begins by stating the topic: difference of motions in essence and accidents, and their unity in individual. He then explains that motion attaches to six things: what it is in (المحل), what it is from (المبدأ), what it is to (المنتهى), the moving thing (المتحرك), the mover (المحرك), and time (الزمان). The attachment to the first three is like an essential attribute: if any of them changes, the essence of the motion changes. The attachment to the last three is accidental: changing them only affects the identity (الهوية) of the motion, not its essence. The only exception is the moving thing (المتحرك) — its difference does not harm the continuous identity of motion because the motion is one in essence even if imagined multiple due to relations to movers. Thus, specific (نوعية) unity of motion requires unity of the three essential relatives (المبدأ والمنتهى وما فيه), while individual (شخصية) unity requires unity of everything except the mover (المحرك). He notes that differences in motion can be in essence or accidents, and unity can be in individual, species, or genus, and motions can also be described as opposites or divisible. The author then confirms the agreement that the three essential attachments are like essential attributes, whereas the remaining three are accidental; the identity (هوية) of motion does not differ with the mover's difference. Building on this, he explains that if the beginning, end, and location are united, the motion is the same in species even if the moving object, mover, or time differ, because the diversity of substrates or causes does not necessitate diversity of accidents and effects — just as heat can be found in different subjects (man/horse) and produced by different sources (fire/sun). Therefore, compulsion, nature, or will do not affect the species of motion: ascending motion by nature (fire), compulsion (stone), or will (bird) are all the same species. Times, he argues, do not differ in essence, so they can be encompassed by one reality. He rebuts the objection that time is accidental to motion and that difference in accident should not affect the subject, saying that this time-attachment is not the same as the motion that makes time an accident (i.e., the motion of the outermost sphere). He then gives examples: in the category of ‘where’ (الأين), ascending and descending motions differ because they have different beginning and end, even if the same location; in quality (الكيف), motion from white to black via yellow-red differs from motion from black to white via red-yellow. Similarly, if the location differs but the beginning and end are the same, motions differ, e.g., straight vs. curved path, or different paths of color change. Finally, he criticizes a claim from al-Mawaqif that unity of location, beginning, and end is necessary for species unity, giving examples like heating and blackening which differ in species even if the beginning and end (e.g., from cold to hot vs. from white to black) differ in location? He argues that such examples are only for illustration, not for proof, suggesting that the original example might have been heating vs. cooling.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if the moving object (e.g., a stone vs. a bird) is different, the motion's essence changes.
Correction: The passage states that the difference of the moving object does not detract from the motion's continuous identity; only the three essential attachments change the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which three attachments of motion are considered essential (بمنزلة الذاتي) and necessitate difference in the essence of motion?
A) The moving thing, the mover, and time
B) What the motion is in, from, and to
C) The mover, what it is from, and time
D) The moving thing, what it is in, and the mover

Key Takeaway
The essence of motion is determined by its location, beginning, and end; other factors only affect its identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual journey toward God, do you treat the beginning (intention) and end (goal) as essential, or do you let accidents like time and external movers define your worship? Where is your focus slipping?
?
If the mover changes — teachers, circumstances, influences — your motion's essence should remain. Is your devotion to God constant regardless of the worldly agents around you, or do you let them alter the very nature of your faith?
?
The subject of motion is essential — the place where change occurs. Is your heart (the subject of spiritual motion) being purified, or are you merely changing accidental attachments without transforming your core?

Unity and Diversity of Motion in Species and Individual
Taftazani explains species unity of motion and critiques al-Mawaqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the essential attachments determine species unity of motion and why differences in mover, time, or compulsion/nature/will do not affect the species.

Core Idea
Unity of location, start, and end makes motions the same species, regardless of mover or time.

Building on the previous principle, al-Taftazani now explains the consequences: if the three essential attachments are the same, the motion is the same in species even if the moving object, mover, or time differ. He gives examples like ascending motion by nature/compulsion/will, and clarifies that times cannot differ in essence. He then addresses a possible objection and critiques a statement from al-Mawaqif.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage from 'فبنوا على ذلك' onwards. Notice the logical flow: rule, examples, objection, rebuttal.
This passage builds on the previous lesson.
2
Understand the rule for species unity
The rule: if the beginning, end, and location are united, the motion is the same in species. Identify this sentence.
The author says 'إذا اتحد المبدأ والمنتهى وما فيه الحركة لتحدث الحركة بالنوع'.
3
Examine the examples
Consider the examples: ascending motion of fire (nature), stone (compulsion), bird (will) are all the same species. Why? Because the essential attachments are the same (upward).
Also note the example of heating and blackening to illustrate difference in species when location differs.
4
Analyze the critique of al-Mawaqif
The author criticizes a claim that unity of location, beginning, and end is always necessary for species unity. He argues that some examples are only for illustration, not proof.
This shows the author's attention to precise argumentation.

المبحث الرابع
اختلاف الحركات بالماهية والعوارض واتحادها بالشخص
قال: المبحث الرابع:
تعلق الحركة بما فيه، وما منه وما إليه يكاد يكون ذاتياً يوجب الاختلاف فيه. الاختلاف في الماهية ، وبما عداها عرضياً يوجب الاختلاف فيه. الاختلاف في الهوية فقط، سوى المتحرك، فإن اختلافه لا يقدح في هويتها الاتصالية الواحدة بالذات، وإن كانت بتوهم فيها كثرة باعتبار النسب إلى المحركات فلذا كانت وحدتها النوعية بوحدة الأمور الثلاثة والشخصية بوحدة ما سوى المحرك).
اختلاف الحركات قد يكون بالماهية، وقد يكون بالعوارض، واتحادها قد يكون بالشخص، وقد يكون بالنوع ، وقد يكون بالجنس، ثم قد يوصف بالتضاد (۱)، وقد يوصف بالانقسام، فيشير في هذا المبحث إلى بيان ماهية ذلك (٢) الحركة . وقد سبق أن الحركة تتعلق بأمور ستة .
فاتفقوا على أن تعلقها بثلاثة منها وهي ما فيه وما منه وما إليه بمنزلة الذاتي يختلف باختلاف ماهية الحركة ، وتعلقها بالثلاثة الباقية بمنزلة العرضى لا يختلف باختلاف ماهية الحركة، بل باختلاف المحرك لا يختلف هويتها أيضاً،
(۱) التضاد هو التباين والتقابل التام، وضد الشيء خلافه، ومن شرط الضدين أن يكونا من جنس واحد كالبياض والسواد . (۲) في (ب) بزيادة لفظ (ذلك ) .

فبنوا على ذلك، أنه إذا اتحد المبدأ والمنتهى وما فيه الحركة لتحدث الحركة بالنوع ، وإن اختلف المتحرك أو المحرك أو الزمان، لأن تنوع المعروضات أو الأسباب لا يوجب تنوع العوارض والمسببات لجواز قيام نوع منها كالحرارة بموضوعين مختلفي الماهية كالإنسان والفرس وحصوله المؤثرين مختلفين كالنار والشمس، وبهذا يظهر أن لا أثر للاختلاف بالقسر والطبع والإرادة. فالحركة الصاعدة للنار طبعاً، وللحجر قسراً وللطير إرادة، لا تختلف نوعاً . وأما الأزمنة فلا يتصور فيها اختلاف الماهية ولو فرض فلا خفاء في جواز إحاطتها بحقيقة واحدة والتمسك بأنها عارضة للحركة واختلاف العارض لا اختلاف المعروض ضعيف لما سبق من أن هذا التعلق بالزمان غير تعلق الحركة التي جعل الزمان عارضاً لها (۱) ، فإنها إنما هي حركة الفلك الأعظم، وإذا اختلفت المبدأ والمنتهى اختلفت الحركة، وإن كان مـا فيـه واحداً أما في الأين فكالحركة الصاعدة مع الهابطة وأما في الكيف فكالحركة من البياض إلى السواد على طريق التصفر ثم التحمر ثم التسود مع الحركة من السواد إلى البياض على طريق التحمر ثم التصفر ثم التبيض . وكذا إذا اختلف ما فيه وإن اتحد المبدأ والمنتهى كالحركة من نقطة إلى نقطة على الاستقامة معها على الانحناء وكالحركة من البياض إلى السواد على طريق الأخذ في الصفرة ثم الحمرة ثم السواد معها على طريق الأخذ في الخضرة ثم النيلية ثم السواد وما ذكر في المواقف من أنه لا بد من وحدة ما فيه وما منه وما إليه، إذ لو اختلف ما فيه اختلف النوع كالتسخن والتسود ليس على ما ينبغي لأن هذا إنما يصح للتمثيل (۲) دون التعليل (۳)، وكأنه أراد أنه يختلف النوع عند اختلاف مجرد ما فيه كما يختلف عند اختلاف الأمور الثلاثة مثل التسخن والتسود أو كان الأصل كالتسخن والتبرد فصحف إلى

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion in kalam about motion and its differentiation and unity. The author begins by stating the topic: difference of motions in essence and accidents, and their unity in individual. He then explains that motion attaches to six things: what it is in (المحل), what it is from (المبدأ), what it is to (المنتهى), the moving thing (المتحرك), the mover (المحرك), and time (الزمان). The attachment to the first three is like an essential attribute: if any of them changes, the essence of the motion changes. The attachment to the last three is accidental: changing them only affects the identity (الهوية) of the motion, not its essence. The only exception is the moving thing (المتحرك) — its difference does not harm the continuous identity of motion because the motion is one in essence even if imagined multiple due to relations to movers. Thus, specific (نوعية) unity of motion requires unity of the three essential relatives (المبدأ والمنتهى وما فيه), while individual (شخصية) unity requires unity of everything except the mover (المحرك). He notes that differences in motion can be in essence or accidents, and unity can be in individual, species, or genus, and motions can also be described as opposites or divisible. The author then confirms the agreement that the three essential attachments are like essential attributes, whereas the remaining three are accidental; the identity (هوية) of motion does not differ with the mover's difference. He then gives examples: in the category of ‘where’ (الأين), ascending and descending motions differ because they have different beginning and end, even if the same location; in quality (الكيف), motion from white to black via yellow-red differs from motion from black to white via red-yellow. Similarly, if the location differs but the beginning and end are the same, motions differ, e.g., straight vs. curved path, or different paths of color change. Finally, he criticizes a claim from al-Mawaqif that unity of location, beginning, and end is necessary for species unity, giving examples like heating and blackening which differ in species even if the beginning and end (e.g., from cold to hot vs. from white to black) differ in location? He argues that such examples are only for illustration, not for proof, suggesting that the original example might have been heating vs. cooling.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if a motion is by compulsion vs. by nature, it becomes a different species.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that ascending motion by nature, compulsion, and will do not differ in species; only the essential attachments matter.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following motions would be considered the same species?
A) A stone falling by nature and a stone thrown upward by force
B) A bird flying upward by will and a stone falling downward by gravity
C) Fire rising by nature, a stone rising by compulsion, and a bird rising by will
D) A stone moving from white to black and a stone moving from black to white

Key Takeaway
Species unity of motion depends solely on the unity of its location, beginning, and end, regardless of mover, time, or external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look back at your past actions, do you tend to judge them by their external circumstances (who you were, the time period) rather than by their moral beginning and end? How does this lesson challenge you to re-evaluate your own life's movements?
?
If times do not differ in essence, why do you allow past failures or future worries to define the species of your current actions? Will you commit to seeing each moment as equally capable of the same species of virtue?
?
Consider a 'motion' in your life that you thought was unique because of its cause (e.g., a heartfelt repentance vs a forced one). Are you willing to accept that the reality of the motion itself may be one species regardless of its origin? What would that demand of you today?

Logic and Technical Terms

The Category-Based Unity of Motion
Sanusi explains that the generic unity of motion is by the category, with a hierarchy within categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the generic unity of motion depends on the category in which it occurs, and that absolute motion is not a single genus covering all motions.

Core Idea
Motion's unity comes from its category, not from a universal genus.

In this passage, the author establishes that motion's generic unity is determined by the category (quantity, quality, etc.) in which the motion takes place. For example, motion in quantity and motion in quality are different high genera, while motions within quality (such as growth and decay) share one genus. The author also notes that absolute motion is not a genus; rather, the term 'motion' is applied to different categories by tashkik or pure equivocation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Focus on the main claim about generic unity.
Notice the examples given.
2
Identify the basis of generic unity
The passage states that generic unity is 'by the unity of what it is in' – meaning the category. Confirm this.
Also note the examples of different genera.
3
Understand the hierarchy within a category
Imam al-Razi described a descending order of genera within one category, e.g., motion in quality → motion in sensible quality → etc. Visualize the hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإما أن وحدتها الجنسية : بوحدة ما فيه حتى أن الواقع في كل مقولة جنس عال من الحركة، ثم يتنازل عن ترتيب أجناسها فمبناه على أن مطلق الحركة ليس جنساً لما يقع في كل مقولة، بل إنما يقال عليها بالتشكيك أو الاشتراك). ذكروا أن الوحدة الجنسية للحركة إنما تكون بوحدة ما فيه جنساً أعني أن المقولة. حتى إن الحركة في الكم مع الحركة في الكيف والأين والوضع أجناس مختلفة، وحركة النمو والذبول والتخلخل والتكاثف جنس واحد، وكذا في الكيف وغيره. وصرح الإمام بأن اتحاد حركات المقولة الواحدة اتحاد في الجنس العالي، ثم يتناول على ترتيب أجناس المقولة مثلا الحركة في الكيف جنس عال وتحته الحركة في الكيفية المحسوسة، وتحتها الحركة في المبصرات، وتحتها الحركة في الألوان وعلى هذا القياس، ولا خفاء في هذا إنما يصح إذا لم يكن مطلق الحركة جنساً لما تحته، بل تكون مقولية الحركة على الأربع، بالاشتراك اللفظي، فلا يتحقق مطلق شامل أو بالتشكيك، فيكون المطلق عرضياً للأقسام لا ذاتياً، والأول باطل لمثل ما مر في الوجود كيف والتغير التدريجي الذي هو حاصل قولهم كمال أول لما هو بالقوة من حيث هو بالقوة مفهوم واحد يشمل الكل، وأما الثاني أعني التشكيك فذهب إليه الكثيرون تمسكاً بأن الحركة كمال أي وجود الشيء لشيء من ذلك الوجود مقول بالتشكيك . ورد أن الكبرى طبيعية لا كلية لأن المقول بالتشكيك مفهوم الوجود لا ما صدق هو عليه من الأفراد ومنعـه آخرون لأنه لا يتصور كون بعض أقسام الحركة أولى أو أقدم أو أشد في كونها حركة ، بل لو أمكن نفي الاتصاف بالوجود كالعدد يكون لبعض أقسامه تقدم على البعض في الوجود لا في العددية فيكون التشكيك عائداً إلى الوجود. فإن قيل : على تقدير التواطؤ لا يثبت الجنسية لجواز أن يكون عارضا كالماشي . قلنا : هذا مع أنه بعد غير مفيد أما البعد فلأنه لا يعقل من الحركة في الكيف مثلا إلا تغير على التدريج من كيفية إلى كيفية وإما عدم الإفادة فلأن القول بأن الوحدة الجنسية يتوقف على وحدة ما فيه إنما يتم إذا ثبت عدم جنسية مطلق الحركة ولا يكفي عدم ثبوت الجنسية.
He said: As for its generic unity: it is by the unity of what it is in, such that what occurs in each category is a high genus of motion, then it descends in the order of its genera. Its basis is that absolute motion is not a genus for what occurs in every category; rather it is only said of them by tashkik (equivocity with priority) or common equivocation. They mentioned that the generic unity of motion is only by the unity of what it is in as a genus, i.e., the category. So motion in quantity together with motion in quality, position, and posture are different genera; and the motion of growth, decay, rarefaction, and condensation is one genus, and similarly in quality and other categories. The Imam explicitly stated that the unity of motions of one category is unity in the high genus; then it descends in the order of the genera of the category. For example, motion in quality is a high genus; under it is motion in sensible quality; under it is motion in visible things; under it is motion in colors; and so on. There is no ambiguity about this; it is only correct if absolute motion is not a genus for what is under it, but rather the predication of motion on the four categories is by pure equivocation (al-ishtirak al-lafẓī), so no comprehensive absolute is realized, or by tashkik, so the absolute is accidental to the species, not essential. The first is invalid due to what was mentioned regarding existence; how about gradual change which is the content of their statement 'first perfection of what is in potency insofar as it is in potency' is one concept encompassing all? As for the second, i.e., tashkik, many held it, holding that motion is a perfection, i.e., the existence of a thing for something, and that existence is predicated by tashkik. It was refuted that the major premise is natural, not universal, because what is predicated by tashkik is the concept of existence, not the individuals of which it is true. Others denied it because it is not conceivable that some divisions of motion are more deserving, prior, or more intense in being motion; rather, if it were possible to negate the attribution of existence, like number, then some of its divisions would have priority over others in existence, not in number-ness, so tashkik would revert to existence. If it is said: On the assumption of univocity, the generic property is not established, because it is possible that it is an accident like 'walking'. We say: This, besides being remote, is not useful. As for being remote, because nothing is understood from motion in quality, for example, except gradual change from one quality to another. As for lack of usefulness, because the statement that generic unity depends on the unity of what it is in is only complete if the non-generic nature of absolute motion is established; it is not sufficient to lack proof of genericness.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about whether motion constitutes a single genus or is predicated by tashkik (equivocity with priority) or common equivocation. The author begins by stating that the generic unity of motion depends on the unity of its category: motion in each category (quality, quantity, etc.) is a distinct high genus. Absolute motion is not a genus covering all; rather it is said by tashkik or equivocation. The author then explains that scholars say the generic unity of motion is only due to the unity of the category: motion in quantity is different from motion in quality, position, and posture—these are different genera. However, within one category, e.g., quality, motions like growth, decay, rarefaction, condensation are one genus. Imam al-Razi stated that the unity of motions in one category is a unity in the high genus, and then there is a descending order of subgenera. This holds only if absolute motion is not a genus for what is under it; rather the predication on the four categories is either pure equivocation (so no comprehensive absolute motion exists) or tashkik (so the absolute is accidental, not essential). The first option (equivocation) is invalid because, as argued earlier regarding existence, the concept of gradual change as 'first perfection of what is in potency insofar as it is in potency' is one concept covering all motions. The second option (tashkik) was held by many, arguing that motion is a perfection, i.e., the existence of a thing, and existence is predicated by tashkik. This was refuted by saying that the major premise is natural, not universal, because tashkik applies to the concept of existence, not to the individuals. Others denied tashkik because it is inconceivable that one division of motion is more deserving, prior, or more intense in being motion; if negation of attribution were possible (like number), then priority would be in existence, not in the essence of motion. Then an objection is raised: even on the assumption of univocity, genericness is not established because motion could be an accident like 'walking'. The response: this is both remote and not useful. Remote because nothing else is understood from motion in quality but gradual change; not useful because the claim that generic unity depends on unity of the category is only valid if the non-generic nature of absolute motion is proven, and lack of proof is insufficient.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion is one genus covering all categories.
Correction: Motion in different categories (quantity, quality, etc.) are different high genera; absolute motion is not a genus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the generic unity of motion?
A) The unity of the concept of motion.
B) The unity of what motion is in (the category).
C) The unity of time.
D) The unity of distance.

Key Takeaway
The generic unity of motion is determined by its category, not by a universal concept of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you tend to treat all forms of change in your life — physical, emotional, spiritual — as if they were the same kind of 'motion'? How does this lesson's insistence on category-based unity challenge you to examine the specific nature of each change? What would it look like to address each with its own appropriate 'genus'?
?
Reflect on a time when you grouped people or experiences under a single label, ignoring the deeper differences that make each unique. How does the principle that 'absolute motion is not a genus' apply to your tendency to oversimplify? What concrete step can you take today to honor the distinct category of something you've been lumping together?
?
If motion in quality, quantity, position, and posture are distinct genera, then spiritual transformation and physical aging are not the same kind of change. How does this distinction affect the way you measure progress in your relationship with Allah? Are you applying the wrong metric to the wrong category?

The Debate on Absolute Motion: Equivocation or Tashkik?
Sanusi debates whether absolute motion is a genus, concluding that it is not, and discusses the implications of tashkik vs. equivocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why absolute motion cannot be a genus and the arguments for and against the views that motion is predicated by pure equivocation or by tashkik.

Core Idea
Absolute motion is not a genus; the two alternative views each face serious objections.

This lesson continues the discussion from the previous one, diving into the two possibilities for the predication of 'motion' across categories: pure equivocation (common equivocal term with no shared meaning) or tashkik (a term with priority and posteriority). The author rejects pure equivocation because the concept of gradual change is common to all motions. He then presents the tashkik view and its refutation, followed by an objection and response about univocity. This is a dense argument typical of kalam debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the logical steps: the two options, the rejection of equivocation, the tashkik view, its refutation, and the objection/response.
This is a complex argument; take your time.
2
Identify the two options
The passage says motion is predicated either by pure equivocation or by tashkik. Mark these in the text.
3
Understand why pure equivocation is rejected
The author argues that gradual change is a common concept (first perfection of what is in potency) that encompasses all motions, so pure equivocation fails.
This is a key argument.
4
Analyze the tashkik argument and its refutation
Many scholars held tashkik, but it is refuted because tashkik applies to the concept of existence, not to the individuals, and because it is not plausible that some motions are more 'motion' than others.
Notice the two different refutations.
5
Examine the objection and response
An objector says that even with univocity, genericness is not proven. The author responds that the objection is remote and not useful because the claim rests on proving non-genericness, not lacking proof.

Is Motion a Genus?
Debate on whether motion is a genus for its divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for and against motion being a genus for its various species, including the roles of homonymy and gradation.

Core Idea
Whether motion is a genus depends on how it is predicated across categories.

This passage tackles a nuanced logical and ontological question: Is the concept of 'motion' broad enough to be a genus that includes all its specific forms? The author presents different views, including those who say motion is predicated by homonymy or gradation, and addresses objections about the categories. Let's explore the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation to get the overall flow.
This passage is dense; don't worry about every detail on first read.
2
Identify the main positions
Look for the three stances: motion as a true genus (univocal), motion as homonymous, and motion as gradational. Note how the author supports or refutes each.
3
Examine the categories objection
Focus on the objection that if motion were a genus, it would either increase the number of categories or be above them. Understand how the author rebuts this.
This is a key logical move.
4
Reflect on the final subtle point
Consider the author's closing remark about generic unity residing in the substrate, not motion itself. How does this save the possibility of motion being a genus?

ثبت عدم جنسية مطلق الحركة ولا يكفي عدم ثبوت الجنسية، وقد يقال لو كانت الحركة جنساً لأقسامها لزادت المقولات على العشر لأنها لا محالة يكون جنساً عالياً، بل ربما يكون فوق المقولات الأربع فيبطل كونها أجناساً عالياً، ويجاب بالمنع لجواز أن يكون من مقولة أن ينفعل على ما سبق مع وقوعها في المقولات الأربع بالمعنى الذي ذكرنا، وإنما يلزم ما ذكر لو كان الحركة الواقعة في الكم من الكم وفي الكيف من الكيف، وفي الأين من الأين وفي الوضع من الوضع فإنه يمتنع حينئذ كون مطلق الحركة مندرجة تحت شيء من المقولات العشر لامتناع تداخلها نعم لو أريد أن الوحدة الجنسية لا يصدق عليها أنها بعض أقسام الحركة، إنما يكون بالوحدة الجنسية لما فيه الحركة لكان وجهاً ولا ينافيه كون مطلق الحركة جنساً.
That the category. Even motion in quantity with motion in quality, location, and posture are different genera, and the motion of growth, decay, rarefaction, and condensation is one genus, and likewise in quality and others. The Imam stated that the unity of motions of one category is unity in the higher genus, then it proceeds according to the order of the genera of the category; for example, motion in quality is a higher genus, under it motion in sensible quality, under it motion in visible things, under it motion in colors, and so on. This is clear only if absolute motion is not a genus for what is under it, but rather the categoricity of motion over the four is by verbal homonymy, so no inclusive absolute is realized, or by gradation (tashkik), so the absolute is accidental to the divisions, not essential. The first is false for the same reason as in existence: how can gradual change, which is what they mean by 'the first perfection of what is in potentiality insofar as it is in potentiality', be a single concept encompassing all? As for the second, namely gradation, many have held it, clinging to the fact that motion is a perfection, i.e., the existence of a thing for something, and that existence is predicated by gradation. It was refuted that the major premise is natural, not universal, because what is predicated by gradation is the concept of existence, not the individuals of which it is true. Others denied it because it is inconceivable that some divisions of motion are more deserving or prior or stronger in being motion. Even if it were possible to deny the characterization by existence—like number—some divisions have priority over others in existence, not in number-ness, so the gradation reverts to existence. If it is said: On the assumption of univocity, genus is not established because it could be accidental, like 'walking'. We say: This, besides being later, is unhelpful. As for 'later', it is because motion in quality, for example, is only understood as gradual change from quality to quality. As for 'unhelpful', it is because the statement that generic unity depends on the unity of that in which it is [motion] is only valid if the non-genus-ness of absolute motion is established, and it is not sufficient to merely lack proof of genus-ness. It may be said: If motion were a genus for its divisions, the categories would increase beyond ten, because it would inevitably be a higher genus; rather, it might be above the four categories, thus invalidating them as higher genera. The answer is by denial: it is possible that it belongs to the category of being affected (an yanfa'il) as previously mentioned, even though it occurs in the four categories in the sense we mentioned. The objection would only follow if motion occurring in quantity were from quantity, in quality from quality, in location from location, and in posture from posture; then it would be impossible for absolute motion to be subsumed under any of the ten categories due to the impossibility of their overlapping. However, if it is meant that generic unity is not predicated of it as being some parts of motion, but rather is due to the generic unity of that in which motion occurs, that would be a valid point, and it does not contradict absolute motion being a genus.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage discusses whether motion (al-haraka) constitutes a genus (jins) for its various species (e.g., motion in quantity, quality, location, posture). The author first notes that motions in different categories are considered different genera, while motions within the same category (like growth, decay) are one genus. The Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) stated that the unity of motions within a single category is a unity in the higher genus. The author then clarifies that this holds only if absolute motion is not a genus for its species; otherwise, the predication of motion across the four categories would be by homonymy (ishtirak lafzi) or gradation (tashkik). The first option is rejected using a parallel argument from existence (al-wujud). The second option (gradation) is examined: many hold it because motion is a perfection and existence is predicated by gradation. However, this is refuted because the major premise is natural (tabi'iyya), not universal (kulliyya): what is predicated by gradation is the concept of existence, not the individuals. Others deny it because no species of motion is more deserving of being motion. An objection is then raised: if motion is univocal (tawatu'), it might be accidental and not a true genus. The author responds that this objection is unhelpful because motion in quality is essentially gradual change. Furthermore, if motion were a genus, the number of categories would increase beyond ten or it would be above the four categories, but the author denies this by suggesting that motion could belong to the category of 'being affected' (an yanfa'il), given its occurrence in the four categories in a certain sense. The passage concludes with a subtle point: generic unity might not be predicated of motion itself but of the substrate of motion, which would not contradict absolute motion being a genus.

مقولة
تشكيك
تواطؤ
اشتراك لفظي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if motion is a genus, it must be a single univocal concept applying equally to all species.
Correction: The passage shows that motion could be predicated by gradation (tashkik) or even accidentally, so genus does not require univocity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason against the view that motion is predicated by gradation (tashkik)?
A) Because gradation only applies to existence, not motion.
B) Because no species of motion is more deserving of being called motion.
C) Because the major premise is natural, not universal.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The debate on whether motion is a genus hinges on how motion is predicated across categories, with homonymy, gradation, and univocity each facing objections, but the author leaves open the possibility of motion being a genus if generic unity is understood in terms of substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage challenges whether all changes can be grouped under a single category. In your own life, do you treat spiritual, intellectual, and physical changes as fundamentally the same type of 'motion'? Or do you recognize distinct genera? How might misclassifying them lead to misdirected effort? Pause and name one area where you have assumed uniformity of change—what would it mean to treat it as a different genus?
?
The debate hinges on whether any species of motion is more deserving of the name. Think of a change you consider 'more real' or significant—perhaps a spiritual transformation versus a habit change. Does the concept of gradation resonate with you? How does your ranking of changes affect your commitment to them? In light of this lesson, will you reconsider which changes you prioritize and why?

Essential and Accidental Opposition of Motions
Sanusi defines the two types of opposition in motions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how opposition between motions is defined through the opposition of their starting and ending points, and how this opposition can be essential or accidental.

Core Idea
Opposition of motions depends on the opposition of what they come from and go to.

This passage introduces the foundational principle for determining when two motions are opposite. Sanusi explains that opposition arises from the termini of motion—what it begins from and what it ends at—and distinguishes between essential and accidental opposition. Keep this criterion in mind as you read the examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms for 'from' and 'to' (ma minhu, ma ilayhi).
The examples of whitening/blackening and ascent/descent will clarify the two types.
2
Identify essential opposition
Look at the example 'whitening of black and blackening of white'. Why are these essentially opposite? Because the qualities (white and black) are intrinsically opposite.
Essential opposition arises when the termini are contrary by nature.
3
Identify accidental opposition
Now consider 'ascent and descent'. The opposition here comes from accidental relations like 'above' and 'below' applying to two points. Why is this accidental?
Accidental opposition depends on external relations, not the motion's intrinsic nature.
4
Reflect on the criterion
Notice that both types rely on the opposition of the motion's starting and ending points. This is the universal criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما تضادها : فلتضاد ما منه وما إليه بالذات كتبيض الأسود، وتسود الأبيض، أو بالعرض كالصعود والهبوط بحسب ما عرض لنقطتين من الفوقية، والتحتية وقبل من المبدئية والمنتهية، ويلزمه التضاد بين كل حركة وعكسها ولو على الاستدارة، وقد ذكروا أن لا تضاد بين الحركات الوضعية حتى الشرقية والغربية، لأن كلا تفعل مثل ما تفعله الأخرى، لكن في النصفين على التبادل، ولعله يلتزم لو ثبت اختلاف الماهية وغاية التخالف) .
He said: As for their opposition: it is due to the opposition of what the motion is from and what it is to, essentially, like the whitening of black and the blackening of white, or accidentally, like ascent and descent according to what accidentally occurs to two points of 'above' and 'below' and prior from 'starting point' and 'end point'. It follows that there is opposition between every motion and its opposite, even in circular motion. They have mentioned that there is no opposition between positional motions, even eastern and western, because each does the same as the other, but in two halves alternately. Perhaps it is asserted that it is established by difference in essence and extreme dissimilarity.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage explains the opposition (tadhad) of motions. The author states that opposition between motions arises from the opposition of their starting point (ma minhu) and endpoint (ma ilayhi). This opposition can be essential (bi al-dhat), such as the motion of whitening of a black object and blackening of a white object—these are essentially opposite because the termini are opposite qualities. Or it can be accidental (bi al-'arad), such as ascent and descent, where the opposition is due to accidental relations like 'above' and 'below' or 'starting point' and 'end point' that apply to two points. The author then notes that this implies opposition between every motion and its reverse, even in circular motion (e.g., clockwise vs. counterclockwise). However, scholars have stated that there is no opposition between positional motions (al-harakat al-wadi'iyya), even between eastward and westward motion, because each motion does the same thing as the other, just in two halves alternately (i.e., moving from east to west is essentially the same as from west to east in terms of the motion's nature). The passage ends by suggesting that perhaps opposition would be asserted if there were a difference in essence (māhiyya) and extreme dissimilarity (ghāyat al-takhāluf).

حركة

ما إليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All opposite motions are essentially opposite.
Correction: The passage shows that some opposition is accidental, depending on relative attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for opposition between motions?
A) The speed of the motion
B) The opposition of the motion's starting and ending points
C) The direction of the motion
D) The substance of the moving object

Key Takeaway
Opposition of motions is defined by the opposition of their termini, which can be either essential or accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you confused essential opposition (deep, intrinsic conflict) with accidental opposition (surface-level disagreement) in a relationship? What would change if you consistently applied Sanusi's distinction?
?
Consider a habit you are trying to change. Is the opposition between your old habit and new habit essential (opposite in nature) or accidental (just different circumstances)? How does that affect your strategy?
?
If the true opposition of motions is in their termini, then what are the starting points and endpoints of your daily actions? Are they essentially opposed to your ultimate goal, or only accidentally so?

Opposition of Reverse and Positional Motions
Sanusi derives the general rule and notes an exception for positional motions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of opposition applies to every motion and its reverse, and why positional motions are an exception to this rule.

Core Idea
Every motion is opposite to its reverse, but positional motions lack true opposition.

Having established the criterion for opposition, the author now derives a general implication: every motion is opposite to its reverse, even circular motion. He then introduces a notable exception: positional motions—such as eastward and westward—are not truly opposite because they perform the same action in alternate halves. This passage invites us to think about when opposition is genuine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English together. Note the phrases 'يلزمه التضاد' (it follows that there is opposition) and 'قد ذكروا' (they have mentioned).
2
Understand the implication
Why does the criterion imply opposition between every motion and its reverse? Because the starting and ending points are swapped, causing opposition. Even circular motion has a direction and its reverse.
Think of clockwise vs. counterclockwise.
3
Examine the exception
Why are positional motions like eastward and westward not really opposite? Because each does the same thing as the other, only in two halves alternately. They are essentially identical.
Positional motions involve change in position, but without a change in the intrinsic nature of the motion.
4
Reflect on the concluding remark
The author hints that opposition might be asserted if there were a difference in essence (māhiyya) and extreme dissimilarity. What does this add?
This suggests that the absence of opposition depends on the sameness of essence.

Source Text

It follows that there is opposition between every motion and its opposite, even in circular motion. They have mentioned that there is no opposition between positional motions, even eastern and western, because each does the same as the other, but in two halves alternately. Perhaps it is asserted that it is established by difference in essence and extreme dissimilarity.

Commentary

This opposition can be essential (bi al-dhat), such as the motion of whitening of a black object and blackening of a white object—these are essentially opposite because the termini are opposite qualities. Or it can be accidental (bi al-'arad), such as ascent and descent, where the opposition is due to accidental relations like 'above' and 'below' or 'starting point' and 'end point' that apply to two points. Motion as a Genus: Objection and Reply
Argument against motion as a genus and its reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion cannot be a genus for its species according to the classical category system, and how the reply defends the absolute motion as a genus.

Core Idea
Motion is not a genus for its species; it belongs to the category of being acted upon.

In this passage, the author engages in a logical disputation about the nature of motion and its relation to the Aristotelian categories. The objection claims that if motion were a genus, it would disrupt the ten-category system. The reply clarifies that motion belongs to the category of being acted upon, thus preserving the categorical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of objection and reply.
Pay attention to the conditional structure of the argument.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: if motion were a genus for its species, it would increase the categories beyond ten.
The objection uses 'لزادت المقولات على العشر'.
3
Understand the reply
The reply denies that motion is a genus; it is from the category of being acted upon (انفعال).
The phrase 'ويجاب بالمنع' indicates the reply.
4
Note the conditional nuance
The author adds that if generic unity is meant differently, absolute motion could be a genus.
This is the 'نعم لو' construction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جنساً لأقسامها لزادت المقولات على العشر لأنها لا محالة يكون جنساً عالياً، بل ربما يكون فوق المقولات الأربع (۱) فيبطل كونها أجناساً عالياً، ويجاب بالمنع لجواز أن يكون من مقولة أن ينفعل على ما سبق مع وقوعها في المقولات الأربع بالمعنى الذي ذكرنا، وإنما يلزم ما ذكر لو كان الحركة الواقعة في الكم من الكم وفي الكيف من الكيف، وفي الأين من الأين وفي الوضع من الوضع (۲) فإنه يمتنع حينئذ كون مطلق الحركة مندرجة تحت شيء من المقولات العشر لامتناع تداخلها نعم لو (۳) أريد أن الوحدة الجنسية لا يصدق عليها أنها بعض أقسام الحركة، إنما يكون بالوحدة الجنسية لما فيه الحركة لكان وجهاً ولا ينافيه كون مطلق الحركة جنساً .
as a genus for its species, it would increase the categories beyond ten because it would necessarily be a higher genus, perhaps even above the four categories (1) so it would invalidate their being higher genera. It is answered by denial, because it is possible that it belongs to the category of being acted upon as mentioned before, while it occurs in the four categories in the sense we stated. What was mentioned would only follow if the motion occurring in quantity is from quantity, and in quality from quality, and in where from where, and in posture from posture (2) for then it would be impossible for absolute motion to be included under any of the ten categories due to their mutual exclusion. Yes, if (3) it is meant that generic unity is not truly predicated as being some parts of motion, but rather by generic unity of that in which motion exists, that would be a valid point, and it does not contradict that absolute motion is a genus.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a logical disputation about whether motion can be considered a genus for its species. The author presents an objection: if motion were a genus for its species, it would increase the number of categories beyond ten because motion would be a higher genus, possibly above the four categories (quantity, quality, relation, and position). That would invalidate the established list of ten categories. Then the reply is given: we deny that motion is a genus; rather, it belongs to the category of being acted upon (انفعال) as previously stated, even though it appears in the four categories in a different sense. The objection would only hold if the motion in quantity were from quantity, in quality from quality, etc., which would cause overlap; but that is not the case. The author then adds that if one means by generic unity not that the genus itself is a part of motion, but that the thing having motion has a generic unity, then that is compatible with absolute motion being a genus.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion as a genus would necessarily increase the number of categories beyond ten.
Correction: The reply shows that motion belongs to the category of being acted upon, so it does not create a new category; it is already within the ten.

Checkpoint

B) Motion would cause the categories to overlap and increase beyond ten.
C) Motion cannot be a genus because it is not a substance.
D) The four categories are not genera at all.

Key Takeaway
Motion can be considered a genus only if we mean generic unity of the thing in which motion occurs, not motion itself.
