The Principle of Opposition: Accident vs. Subject
Objection and answer: the rule of accident opposition and its qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle 'opposition of an accident does not necessitate opposition of the subject' is qualified when the accident's opposition makes the definition of opposites apply to the subject itself.

Core Idea
Opposition of an accident only necessitates subject opposition when it directly makes the subject an opposite.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection to his earlier argument that the opposition of motion's endpoints implies opposition of motion itself. The objector appeals to a known principle: the opposition of an accident does not necessitate opposition of its subject. The author responds by clarifying that the principle holds only in an absolute sense, but when the accident's opposition is so specific that it fulfills the definition of opposites for the subject, the principle does not apply. This lesson unpacks that qualification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection carefully. The objector cites a general rule about accidents and subjects and questions why the author's previous claim about motion doesn't violate it.
Notice the objector uses 'qīla' (it was said), a common formula.
2
Understand the author's qualification
The author distinguishes between the absolute sense of the rule and a specific case. Focus on the words 'bi-mujarradihi' (by itself) and 'ʿalā iṭlāqihi' (absolutely).
The key is that the rule is not universal; it depends on the specificity of the accident.
3
Apply to the motion case
The author argues that the opposition of the two endpoints (starting and ending points) is so specific that it makes the definition of opposites true of motion itself. Think about why that is plausible.
The passage cuts off, but the reasoning is that motion's identity is tied to its endpoints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد ذكروا أن تضاد العارض لا يوجب تضاد المعروض فكيف أوجب تضاد عارض بعض ما يتعلق به الحركة تضاد الحركة مع أن هذا أبعد . . ؟ قلنا مرادهم أن ذلك بمجرده وعلى إطلاقه لا يوجب تضاد المعروض، وأما إذا كان بخصوصه بحيث يوجب صدق حد الضدين على المعروض، أو على ما يتعلق به فلا استبعاد وههنا قد صدق بتضاد الطرفين حد الضدين على
For it might be said: They have mentioned that opposition of an accident does not necessitate opposition of the subject; so how did opposition of an accident of some of that with which motion is related necessitate opposition of motion, when this is even more unlikely? We say: Their meaning is that this, by itself and absolutely, does not necessitate opposition of the subject. But when it is specific in such a way that it necessitates the truth of the definition of opposites on the subject, or on that which is related to it, then there is no improbability. And here, by the opposition of the two endpoints, the definition of opposites is true of
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that it is commonly accepted that opposition of an accident does not necessitate opposition of the subject (the thing that bears the accident). So how can the author claim that opposition of the endpoints (which are accidents of motion) necessitates opposition of motion itself? This seems even more far-fetched. The answer clarifies that the general principle applies when the opposition of the accident is considered in an absolute sense. However, when the accident is such that its specific presence causes the definition of opposites (i.e., the criteria for being opposite) to apply to the subject or to what is related to it, then it is valid. In this case, the opposition of the two endpoints (the starting and ending points) is so specific that it makes the definition of opposites true of the motion itself. The answer is incomplete in the given text but the core reasoning is clear.

Key Terms
عارض
معروض
تضاد
حد الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the principle 'opposition of an accident does not entail opposition of the subject' is absolute and applies in all cases.
Correction: The author clarifies that the principle is only general; if the accident's opposition is specific enough to make the subject itself fulfill the definition of opposites, then it does necessitate subject opposition.

Checkpoint
What condition does the author give for opposition of an accident to necessitate opposition of the subject?
A) When the accident is an essential property of the subject.
B) When the accident's opposition is specific enough to make the definition of opposites apply to the subject or its related thing.
C) When the accident is a substance.
D) When the subject is a body.

Key Takeaway
Opposition of an accident can necessitate opposition of the subject if the accident's opposition is specific enough to make the definition of opposites true for the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that the principle 'opposition of accident does not necessitate opposition of subject' is qualified. Where in your own life do you hold onto absolutes that actually require specific conditions? What would change if you examined those conditions?
?
The objector in this passage used a common principle to challenge a specific case. When have you used a general Islamic principle to dismiss a particular situation, only to later realize the principle needed qualification? What did you learn from that?

Speed and Slowness as Qualities of Motion
Al-Taftazani defines speed and slowness as qualitative consequences of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that speed and slowness are not motions themselves but relative qualities of motion, defined through time and distance.

Core Idea
Speed and slowness are relative qualities of motion, not motion itself.

In this passage, al-Taftazani clarifies a subtle point in the philosophy of motion: speed and slowness are not motions but qualities that attach to motion. He explains that motion inherently involves time and distance, and these qualities are defined by the ratio of distance covered to time. This lesson unpacks his precise definition and the relational nature of speed and slowness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of al-Taftazani's argument. Notice that he starts by stating that speed and slowness are qualities (kayfiyya) consequent to motion.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Taftazani says that speed and slowness are a quality that admits intensity and weakness, differing by relation. This is the core definition. Understand that they are not motions but attributes of motion.
This refutes the common misconception that speed and slowness are motions themselves.
3
Understand the role of time and distance
The passage explains that motion requires time and extension (distance). Both time and distance are divisible. The quality of speed or slowness manifests when covering the same distance in different times or different distances in the same time.
4
Grasp the relational definition
Speed and slowness are defined relationally: a motion is fast relative to something that takes more time to cover the same distance or covers less distance in the same time. This makes them relative, not absolute.
This is why the same motion can be both fast and slow relative to different references.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الخامس :
من لوازم الحركة كيفية قابلة للشدة والضعف مختلفة بحسب الإضافة يسمى باعتبار الشدة سرعة ، وباعتبار الضعف بطئاً ) .
لا بد للحركة من زمان ومن امتداد في الأيون أو المقادير أو الكيفيات أو الأوضاع ولا بأس بتسميته مسافة وإن كان الاسم بإطلاقه لمــا في الأيون وهما أعني الزمان والمسافة يقبلان القسمة فإذا فرضنا قطع مسافة في زمان، فقد يقطع تلك المسافة في زمان أقل، أو بقطع في ذلك الزمان مسافة أقل مـع أن حقيقة الحركة بحالها فلا محالة يكون ذلك بصفة في الحركة يشتد فيقطع المسافة الأطول وتسمى سرعة ، وتضعف فتقطع المسافة الأقصر وتسمى بطئاً، ولا تقدر على التعبير عنهما إلا بما يلزمهما من قطع المسافة الأطول في زمان مساو أو المسافة المساوية في زمان أقل، وقطع المسافة الأقصر في زمان مساو أو المسافة المساوية في زمان أكثر ، ويختلفان بحسب الاعتبار فتكون الحركة الواحدة سريعة بالنسبة إلى ما يقطع مسافتها في زمان أكثر أو يقطع في زمانها مسافة أقل وبطيئة بالنسبة إلى عكس ذلك .
He said: The fifth section: Among the consequences of motion is a quality susceptible to intensity and weakness, differing according to relation, called in respect of intensity speed, and in respect of weakness slowness. Motion necessarily requires time and extension in space (al-ayn) or quantities or qualities or positions, and there is no harm in calling it distance (masafa), even if the name in its absolute sense is for what is in space. Both of them, namely time and distance, are divisible. So if we suppose covering a distance in a time, it may be that that distance is covered in less time, or that less distance is covered in that time, while the reality of motion remains as it is. Therefore necessarily there is a quality in motion: it intensifies so it covers the longer distance and is called speed, and it weakens so it covers the shorter distance and is called slowness. And one cannot express them except by what necessarily accompanies them: covering the longer distance in equal time or equal distance in less time, and covering the shorter distance in equal time or equal distance in more time. And they differ according to consideration, so one and the same motion is fast relative to something that covers its distance in more time or covers in its time less distance, and slow relative to the opposite of that.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage defines speed and slowness. It first states that one of the consequences (lawaazim) of motion is a quality (kayfiyya) that admits intensity and weakness, varying by relation: called speed by intensity, slowness by weakness. Then it explains that motion requires time and extension in space (al-ayn) or quantities or qualities or positions, called distance (masafa). Both time and distance are divisible. If we assume covering a distance in a time, it is possible to cover that same distance in less time, or to cover a shorter distance in the same time, while the essence of motion remains. Thus there must be a quality in motion: it intensifies to cover the longer distance (speed) or weakens to cover the shorter distance (slowness). These are only expressed by their necessary consequences: covering a longer distance in equal time or equal distance in less time (speed), and covering a shorter distance in equal time or equal distance in more time (slowness). They vary by relation: one motion can be fast relative to something that covers its distance in more time, and slow relative to the opposite.

Correction: They are qualities (kayfiyya) that inhere in motion, not the motion itself.

Checkpoint

A) They are two different types of motion.
B) They are two qualities of motion that vary by intensity and relation.

Key Takeaway
Speed and slowness are relative qualities of motion, defined by the ratio of distance to time.

Are you paying attention to these signs, or are you ignoring them?
?
Motion necessarily requires time and distance. Your life has a fixed amount of time. Are you covering the 'distance' towards your purpose, or are you stationary? If you were to measure your life today by the same ratio the passage uses (distance covered per time), would you be moving fast or slow? What needs to change?

First Proof: Impossibility of Two Motions with Different Distances
Al-Taftazani presents the first proof against motion composed of rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against the analysis of motion into rests, which shows that it would prevent two motions from coexisting when they cover different distances.

Core Idea
If motion were made of rests, two motions with different distances couldn't happen together.

Al-Taftazani begins the refutation of the atomistic theory of motion by showing a logical contradiction. The first argument targets the idea that motion is a series of rests. He argues that if motion were composed of discrete rests, then two motions traversing different distances in the same time would have different numbers of rests, making it impossible for them to actually be simultaneous. This proof uses examples from everyday observation like the sun, shadow, and millstone.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its examples
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the reasoning: if motion = rests, then distances differ => rests differ => motions cannot be simultaneous.
The proof is a reduction to absurdity (luzum).
2
Understand the logical structure
The proof is: if motion is composed of rests, then two motions with different distances would have different rests, which contradicts their simultaneity. But simultaneity occurs (e.g., sun and shadow), so the premise is false.
3
Reflect on the examples
The sun moves across the sky, the shadow moves along the ground - they occur together but cover different distances. The millstone's ends move differently. These are concrete counterexamples.
This shows the argument is not merely theoretical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تحلل السكنات لوجوه :
الأول : أنه يستلزم امتناع تلازم الحركتين مع تفاوت المسافتين لاستلزامه تفاوت السكنات المنافي لتلازم الحركات واللازم منتف كما في حركة الشمس، وما يتخيل من حركة الظل، وفي حركة طرفي الرحى ونحو ذلك .
الثاني : إن انتفاء الحركة مع تحقق المقتضى وعدم المانع ضروري البطلان .
الثالث : إن فضل سكنات الفرس الشديد العدو حينئذ على حركاته، كفضل حركات الفلك الأعظم عليها، فيلزم أن يرى ساكناً على الدوام، لكون الحركات مغمورة أو في زمان هو أضعاف آلاف زمان الحركة لا أقل للقطع بأن الجسم حال السكون يرى ساكناً، وإن كان السكون عدمياً .
And [he said:] do not analyze the rests [i.e., motion is not composed of rests] for [the following] reasons: First: that it entails the impossibility of two motions coexisting with difference of the two distances, because it entails difference of rests which prevents the coexistence of motions, and the necessary consequence is false, as in the motion of the sun, and what is imagined of the motion of the shadow, and in the motion of the two ends of a millstone and the like. Second: that the absence of motion while the requisite is present and the obstacle absent is necessarily false. Third: that the excess of the rests of a horse that is very fast then over its motions, like the excess of the motions of the uppermost sphere over them, would necessitate that it be seen as continuously at rest, because the motions are overwhelmed or in a time that is thousands of times the time of motion, at least, since it is certain that a body in a state of rest is seen as at rest, even if rest is non-existent.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments against the theory that motion is composed of discrete rests (tahlil al-sakanat). First proof: If motion were composed of rests, then it would be impossible for two motions to coexist (talazum al-harakatayn) while covering different distances, because the rests would differ and prevent the simultaneous occurrence of the motions. But we observe coexisting motions like the sun's apparent motion, shadow motion, and the motion of both ends of a millstone. Second proof: It is necessarily false that motion would cease while its requisite conditions exist and no obstacle is present. Third proof: If a fast horse's motion is composed of rests, then its number of rests would exceed its motions to an enormous degree, so the horse would appear stationary because motions are overwhelmed by rests or the time of motion is insignificantly small compared to the time of rest. But we see the horse moving.

Key Terms
تحليل السكنات
تلازم الحركتين
تفاوت المسافتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two motions with different distances can coexist even if motion is composed of rests.
Correction: The rests would differ and prevent simultaneity, as shown by the proof.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the first proof?
A) Motion cannot be analyzed into rests because it would prevent two motions with different distances from coexisting.
B) Motion is composed of rests because we see simultaneous motions.
C) Rests are not real.
D) The sun's motion is composed of rests.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that the theory of motion as composed of rests contradicts observed simultaneity of motions with different distances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that seemed intuitively true, only to realize it contradicts what you can plainly observe? How did you respond? This proof shows that even our understanding of motion can be mistaken. What assumptions in your life might be similarly flawed, and are you willing to test them against reality?
?
The proof uses the sun's motion and the shadow's motion — one real, one perceived. Are there aspects of your faith or practice that you dismiss as 'just perception' while treating others as 'real'? How does the unity of all motions under Allah's creation challenge you to see His hand in everything, not just what you consider significant?
?
If motion were composed of rests, simultaneous motions would be impossible, yet you experience them daily. What does this coherence in creation tell you about the Creator? When you observe order in the world, do you pause to thank Allah for His perfect design, or do you take it for granted? What will you do today to cultivate that awareness?

Second Proof: No Motion without Obstacle When Conditions Are Met
Al-Taftazani presents the second proof based on causal necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof, which argues that it is impossible for motion to cease while its requisite conditions exist and no obstacle is present.

Core Idea
If motion were rests, it could stop even when conditions for motion are met—which is absurd.

The second proof is concise but powerful. Al-Taftazani asserts that if motion were composed of rests, then it would be possible for motion to be absent even when all the conditions for motion are satisfied and there is no obstacle. This, he states, is 'necessarily false' (daruri al-batlan). The proof relies on a basic causal principle: when a cause is fully present and no impediment exists, the effect must occur.

Guided Path
1
Read the short proof
Read the Arabic and translation. The proof is a single statement: absence of motion while requisite is present and obstacle absent is necessarily false.
It is a direct application of causal necessity.
2
Understand the causal principle
In kalam and philosophy, when all conditions for a thing are met and no barrier exists, that thing must occur. Here, the 'requisite' (muqtada) for motion is present.
This is a self-evident principle.
3
Connect to motion analysis
If motion were composed of rests, then between two rests there is a state of rest. But if the conditions for motion are continually present, motion should be continuous, not interrupted by rests. So the existence of rests contradicts the causal principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint

The second proof establishes that motion cannot be composed of rests because that would violate the causal principle of necessary effect when conditions are met.

?

?
Have you ever assumed that something 'just happened' without a cause, like a sudden change in your heart? How does this lesson's proof that every effect requires a complete cause challenge you to take more responsibility for the conditions you set in your spiritual life?

Third Proof: The Fast Horse Would Appear Stationary
Al-Taftazani presents the third proof using the analogy of a fast horse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof, which uses the analogy of a fast horse to show that if motion were composed of rests, the horse would appear stationary.

Core Idea
If motion were rests, a fast horse would have more rests than motions, making it look still.

The third proof is the most vivid and imaginative. Al-Taftazani asks us to consider a horse running at full speed. If its motion is composed of rests, then the number of rests exceeds the number of motions to an enormous degree, similar to the vast difference between the motions of the highest sphere and earthly motions. As a result, the horse's motion would be overwhelmed by rests, and we would perceive it as stationary. But we see the horse moving, so the premise must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its reasoning
Read the Arabic passage. Notice the comparison: the excess of rests over motions in the horse is compared to the excess of celestial motions over earthly ones.
2
Understand the analogy
If rests are many and motions are few, the object appears at rest because motions are 'overwhelmed' (maghmura) or occupy a negligible time.
This uses our experience that a body at rest is seen as stationary.
3
Apply to the horse
A fast horse should appear stationary if its motion is composed of rests, because rests dominate. But we see it moving, so the theory is false.
This is a reduction to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Limits of Speed and Slowness
Al-Taftazani examines whether speed and slowness have absolute limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speed and slowness are necessarily relative and cannot reach absolute limits according to al-Taftazani's analysis.

Core Idea
Speed and slowness are always relative—no motion is absolutely fast or slow.

This passage from al-Taftazani's commentary on al-Maqasid tackles a subtle question: can a motion be purely fast without any slowness, or purely slow without any speed? The discussion reveals that speed and slowness are inherently relational, depending on a standard of comparison. As you read, pay attention to how the author uses the infinite divisibility of time and distance to argue against absolute limits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Try to grasp the main issue and the flow of argument before analyzing details.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Notice that al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) inclines toward there being a limit, while the philosophers' view (more in line with their principles) denies absolute limits. Understand why each side holds its position.
The commentator presents both sides but ultimately favors relativity.
3
Analyze the key argument from divisibility
The passage states that motion requires time and distance, both divisible ad infinitum. This implies that any given motion is slow relative to faster motions and fast relative to slower ones. See how this undermines the claim of absolute speed or slowness.
This is the core logical move.
4
Examine the refutation of al-Imam's argument
Al-Imam argued that without ultimate extremes, contrariety would be lost. The commentator shows this is weak because even if division reaches a minimum, that minimum motion is still relative. Understand the example of the outermost sphere's motion being only relatively fastest.
This reinforces the relational nature of speed and slowness.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ثم كل من السرعة والبطء قابل للشدة والضعف : وهل ينتهي ذلك إلى حد أم لا؟ فيه تردد وميل الإمام إلى الأول، وإن كان الثاني أشبه بأصولهم) .
لا خفاء في ذلك لكن هل ينتهيان إلى حد حتى تتحقق حركة سريعة لاحظ لها من البطء وبطيئة لا حظ لها من السرعة أم لا بد لكل حركة خط من السرعة بالنسبة إلى ما هو أبطأ، ومن البطء بالنسبة إلى ما هو أسرع فيه تردد، وإلا شبه بأصولهم هو.
الثاني : لأن الحركة لا تكون بدون زمان ومسافة أي امتداد. إحدى المقولات الأربع وكل منهما ينقسم لا إلى نهاية، وكل حركة تعرض فهي بالنسبة إلى ما يقطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان بطيئة وبالنسبة إلى ما يقطع في ذلك الزمان نصف تلك المسافة سريعة ، لكن ميل الإمام إلى الأول تمسكاً بأنهما لو لم ينتهيا الشدة إلى حد لما كان بينهما غاية الخلاف فلم يتحقق التضاد، فلم تتصور والضعف لكونه انتقالاً من ضد إلى ضد وضعفه ظاهر، وقد يتمسك بأن انقسام الزمان والمسافة قد ينتهي إلى ما لا تمكن الحركة في أقل منه، وإن كان قابلاً بحسب الفرض. وحينئذ تتحقق بحسب ذلك الزمان سرعة بلا بطء، وبحسب تلك المسافة بطء بلا سرعة وهو أيضاً ضعيف، لأن تلك السريعة بطيئة بالنسبة إلى ما يقطع في ذلك الزمان ضعف تلك المسافة، وتلك البطيئة سريعة بالنسبة إلى ما يقطع تلك المسافة في ضعف ذلك الزمان نعم، لما كانت الأبعاد متناهية، فقطع أطول مسافة في أقصر زمان، ربما تخلو عن البطء، وأما كون حركة الفلك الأعظم أسرع الحركات، فإنما هو النسبة إلى ما هو في الوجود دون ما في الإمكان. إذ لا يمتنع أن يقع في أقل من ذلك القدر من الزمان.
He said: Then each of speed and slowness is susceptible to intensity and weakness: and does it reach a limit or not? There is hesitation and the inclination of al-Imam is to the first, although the second is more in line with their principles. There is no concealment in that, but do they reach a limit such that a fast motion comes to have no share of slowness, and a slow motion no share of speed? Or must every linear motion have speed relative to what is slower, and slowness relative to what is faster? There is hesitation regarding this, otherwise it would resemble their principles. Second: because motion cannot be without time and distance, i.e., extension. One of the four categories, and each of them is divisible ad infinitum. And every motion that occurs is, relative to what covers that distance in half that time, slow, and relative to what covers half that distance in that time, fast. But al-Imam inclined to the first, holding that if they did not reach an extreme of intensity, there would be no ultimate difference between them, so no contrariety would be realized, and hence it would not be conceived. And weakness, because it is a transition from one contrary to another, its weakness is apparent. And it may be argued that the division of time and distance may terminate at something in which motion is not possible in less than it, although it is divisible hypothetically. Then according to that time, a speed without slowness is realized, and according to that distance, a slowness without speed. But this is also weak, because that fast motion is slow relative to what covers twice that distance in that time, and that slow motion is fast relative to what covers that distance in twice that time. Yes, since dimensions are finite, covering the longest distance in the shortest time may be devoid of slowness. As for the motion of the outermost sphere being the fastest of motions, it is only relative to what exists, not to what is possible. For it is not impossible that something occur in less than that measure of time.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the nature of speed and slowness and whether they can reach absolute limits. It begins with a statement from the matn (al-Maqasid) noting that speed and slowness are subject to intensity and weakness, and raising the question of whether they reach a limit. There is hesitation, but al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) inclines to the view that they do reach a limit, though the opposite is more in line with the principles of the philosophers. The commentator then elaborates. He first considers whether a motion can be purely fast without any slowness or purely slow without any speed. The opposite view holds that every linear motion necessarily has speed relative to what is slower and slowness relative to what is faster. The commentator then presents a second argument: motion requires time and distance, both of which are infinitely divisible. Consequently, any given motion is slow relative to a motion that covers the same distance in half the time, and fast relative to a motion that covers half the distance in the same time. Al-Imam's argument for a limit is that if speed and slowness did not reach an extreme, there would be no ultimate contrariety between them. But the commentator counters by showing that even if division terminates at a minimum, that minimum motion would still be relative: a fast motion in a minimal time is slow relative to one covering twice the distance in that time, and a slow motion in a minimal distance is fast relative to one covering that distance in twice the time. The only exception is that finite distances allow a motion covering the longest distance in the shortest time to be devoid of slowness relative to existing things. But the motion of the outermost sphere, though fastest in existence, is only relatively fast; faster motions are possible in principle. Thus the passage refutes the claim of absolute limits by emphasizing the relativity of speed and slowness.

Rest Between Rectilinear Motions: Refutation
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim of necessary rest between rectilinear motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalām scholars refute the claim that rest must exist between two rectilinear motions, and why the division of time into distinct instants is considered estimative rather than actual.

Core Idea
Rest between motions is not necessary; distinct instants are only estimative.

This lesson examines a subtle kalām discussion about whether a moving body must rest between two successive rectilinear motions. The claim is based on the idea that the instants of arrival and departure are distinct, which if not separated by time would entail a succession of instants and thus atomic time. The response shows that time is continuous and its division into instants is purely mental. We will follow the argument and its rebuttal step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a claim, an answer, an objection, and a counter. Also note the later discussion about turn vs. return.
2
Identify the claim and its reasoning
The claim states that between every two rectilinear motions there must be rest because the instant of arrival is different from the instant of return, and without an intervening time, a succession of instants would follow, implying the existence of the atom (atomic time).
This reasoning relies on the idea of distinct instants.
3
Understand the answer
The answer denies the necessity of rest by arguing that time is not actually divided into distinct instants; any division is only estimative. Therefore, we do not concede that the instants are distinct, nor that a succession is impossible.
The key point: time is continuous, not composed of discrete instants.
4
Examine the objection and counter
An objection is raised using a rotating sphere: at each point, the instant of arrival differs from the instant of departure, suggesting a succession of instants or rest. The counter is that the division of the sphere's path into points is purely estimative.
This reinforces the estimative nature of spatial division as well.
5
Consider the turn vs. return distinction
The passage ends with a discussion about whether the motion changes direction (turn) or reverses (return). The occurrence of an angle only happens in a turn, not a return, because the line remains the same in a return. The commentator criticizes the expression 'abstraction' used here.
This distinction is less central but shows the depth of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس: السكون بين الحركتين المستقيمتين عند بعض الفلاسفة والمتكلمين
قال : المبحث السادس: [زعم بعضهم أن بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين سكوناً لأن أن الوصول غير أن الرجوع ضرورة فلو لم يتخللهما زمان لزم تتالي الأنين المستلزم لوجود الجزء، وحيث لا حركة بين الوصول والرجوع تعين السكون .
والجواب : بعد تسليم امتناع الجزء أنه بالفعل ما لم ينقطع الزمان . اللهم إلا أن يراد به زمان لا ينقسم إلا بالوهم وحينئذ لا نسلم تغاير آني الوصول والرجوع ولا إستحالة تتالي الآنين، وأما النقض بكل حركة مستقيمة سيما إذا أدرنا كرة على سطح، فإن أن الوصول إلى كل نقطة يغاير أن الانفصال عنها، ويلزم تتالي الآنين أو تخلل زمان السكون. فقد يرد بأن انقسام المسافة هنا محض توهم).
ذهب بعض الفلاسفة والمتكلمين إلى أن بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين زماناً يسكن فيه المتحرك سواء كانت الثانية رجوعاً إلى الصوب الأول أو انعطافاً إلى صوب آخر، ولا خفاء في أن حصول الزاوية إنما يكون على تقدير الانعطاف دون الرجوع ، لأن الخط واحد فعبارة التجريد وهي أنه لا اتصال لذوات الزوايا ولا انعطاف ليست على ما ينبغي، وقد فسرت بأنه لا اتصال للحركات الأينية التي
Sixth Investigation: Rest between two rectilinear motions according to some philosophers and theologians.
He said: Sixth Investigation: [Some claimed that between every two rectilinear motions there is a rest, because the instant of arrival is different from the instant of return necessarily. For if no time intervenes between them, then a succession of instants would follow, which entails the existence of the atom. And since there is no motion between arrival and return, rest is determined.
The answer: After conceding the impossibility of the atom, it is [that] it is in act only if time is not interrupted. Unless what is meant by it is a time that is divisible only by estimation. Then we do not concede the difference between the instants of arrival and return, nor the impossibility of the succession of instants. As for the objection by every rectilinear motion, especially if we rotate a sphere on a surface, the instant of arrival at each point differs from the instant of separation from it, and a succession of instants or the interposition of a time of rest would follow. It may be countered that the division of distance here is purely estimative.
Some philosophers and theologians went that between every two rectilinear motions there is a time in which the moving body rests, whether the second motion is a return to the first direction or a turn to another direction. There is no concealment that the occurrence of an angle is only on the assumption of a turn, not a return, because the line is one. So the expression of abstraction, namely that angular things have no connection and no turn, is not as it should be. And it has been interpreted as meaning that there is no connection of the 'where' motions that...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a kalam discussion about whether rest must exist between two rectilinear motions. It begins with a claim from some philosophers and theologians that between every two rectilinear motions there is a period of rest, because the instant of arrival at a point is distinct from the instant of departure from that point. If no time intervened, there would be a succession of instants, which (in their view) implies the existence of indivisible atomic parts, which the theologians deny. Hence rest must fill the gap. The commentator gives the answer: even if we concede the impossibility of atomic parts, the intervening time is not actually divisible into distinct instants; rather, time is continuous and its division is only mental or estimative. Therefore, we do not accept that the instants are distinct or that a succession of instants is impossible. A further objection is raised: consider a sphere rotating on a surface; at each point, the instant of arrival differs from the instant of departure, which seems to force either a succession of instants or a rest. The counter is that the division of the distance into points is purely estimative. Then another opinion is mentioned: some hold that there is a real time of rest between two rectilinear motions, whether the motion changes direction (turn) or reverses (return). The commentator notes that an angle occurs only in a turn, not a return, because the line remains the same in a return. He criticizes the expression 'abstraction' used in this context, saying it is not appropriate. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing the interpretation of that expression. Overall, the commentator refutes the necessity of rest by denying the reality of distinct instants and emphasizing the estimative nature of spatial division.

Key Takeaway

The Instantaneous Nature of Reaching the Limit
Sanusi presents the philosophers' core argument for instantaneous reaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that reaching the endpoint must be instantaneous, based on the impossibility of temporal division.

Core Idea
Reaching the limit cannot be temporal because half the time would contradict the assumption.

The philosophers begin their proof by asserting that reaching a limit is an instantaneous event. They argue that if it were spread over time, a contradiction would arise. This lesson unpacks that key argument and shows why it leads to the conclusion that reaching is instantaneous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the half-time argument
Focus on the dichotomy: if reaching were temporal, then in half that time either reaching occurs (contradiction) or it does not (contradiction). This forces the conclusion that reaching is instantaneous.
Apply the same reasoning to return.
3
Note the objection and response
An objection says reaching and return are motions and thus temporal. The response clarifies that non-reaching is not a motion, so the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعمدة في احتجاج الفلاسفة أن الوصول إلى النهاية آني إذ لو كان زمانياً ففي نصف ذلك الزمان إما أن يحصل الوصول فلا يكون في ذلك الزمان بل في نصفه، أو لا يحصل فلا يكون المفروض زمان وكذا الرجوع . أعني ابتداءه الذي قد يعبر عنه باللاوصول واللامماسة، والمباينة والمفارقة فلا يرد ما قيل إن كلاً من ذلك حركة وهي زمانية لا آنية ، ثم الآنات متغاير إن ضرورة فإن لم يكن بينهما زمان لزم تتالي الآنات، فيكون الامتداد الزماني الذي هو مقدار الحركة متألفاً من الآنات وهو منطبق على الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة فيلزم وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وإن كان بينهما زمان ولا حركة فيه تعين السكون ، ولما كان منع ضرورة تغاير الآنين ظاهر بناء على جواز أن يقع الوصول واللاوصول. أعني نهاية حركة الذهاب وبداية حركة الرجوع في آن واحد هو حد مشترك بين زمانيهما، كالنقطة الواحدة التي تكون بداية خط ونهاية خط آخر، وليس هذا من اجتماع النقيضين . أعني الوصول واللاوصول في شيء لأن معناه أن يصدق على الشيء أنه واصل وليس بواصل ، لا أن يحصل له الوصول، وابتداء الرجوع الذي هو لا وصول كما يحصل للجسم الحركة والسواد الذي هو لا حركة . قرر هذه الحجة بوجه آخر وهو أن الحركة إنما تصدر عن علة موجودة تسمى باعتبار كونها مزيلة للمتحرك من حد ما مقربه له إلى حد آخر ميلاً وهي العلة للوصول إلى الحد، وإن لم يسم باعتبار الاتصال ميلاً فتكون موجودة في آن الوصول، إذ ليس الميل من الأمور التي لا توجد إلا في الزمان كالحركة ثم اللاوصول أعني المباينة عن ذلك الحد لا تحدث إلا بعد حدوث ميل ثان في آن شان ضرورة امتناع اجتماع الميل إلى الشيء مع الميل عنه في آن واحد ولاستحالة تتالي الآنين يكون بينهما زمان يكون الجسم فيه عديم الميل فيكون عديم الحركة، وهو معنى السكون .
The main point in the philosophers' argument is that reaching the end is instantaneous, for if it were temporal, then in half of that time, either reaching occurs, so it would not be in that time but in its half, or it does not occur, so the assumed time would not be [the time of reaching]. Similarly for return, meaning its beginning, which may be expressed as non-reaching, non-tangency, separation, and disjunction. So what was said—that each of these is motion and motion is temporal, not instantaneous—does not apply. Then the instants are necessarily distinct. If there is no time between them, successive instants follow, so the temporal extension which is the measure of motion would be composed of instants, and it corresponds to the motion which corresponds to the distance, so the existence of the indivisible part follows. But if there is time between them with no motion, rest is determined. Since the denial of the necessity of the two instants being distinct is apparent, based on the permissibility that reaching and non-reaching—i.e., the end of the going motion and the beginning of the returning motion—occur in a single instant, which is a common boundary between their times, like a single point that is the beginning of one line and the end of another line, and this is not a case of the conjunction of opposites—I mean reaching and non-reaching—because its meaning is that something is described as reaching and not reaching, not that it obtains reaching and the beginning of return which is non-reaching, just as a body obtains motion and blackness which is not motion. He formulated this argument in another way: motion arises from an existing cause called, in consideration of its being what moves the mover from one limit and brings it near to another limit, an inclination (mayl), and it is the cause of reaching the limit, even if it is not called an inclination due to continuity. So it exists at the instant of reaching, since inclination is not among things that exist only in time, like motion. Then non-reaching—i.e., separation from that limit—does not occur except after the occurrence of a second inclination in a second instant, due to the impossibility of the conjunction of inclination toward something and inclination away from it in one instant. And due to the impossibility of successive instants, there is between them a time in which the body is devoid of inclination, so it is devoid of motion, and this is the meaning of rest.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument of the philosophers regarding the instantaneous nature of reaching a limit (al-wusul). The author begins by stating that reaching the end must be instantaneous (ani). If it were temporal, then in half that time either reaching occurs (making the whole time unnecessary) or it does not (contradicting the assumption). The same applies to the beginning of return. The author then addresses a potential objection that both reaching and return are motions and thus temporal. He responds that they are not motions in the strict sense; the beginning of return is 'non-reaching' (la wusul). Next, he discusses instants (anat): if two consecutive instants have no time between them, they are successive (tatali al-anat), implying time is composed of instants, which leads to the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza). If there is time between them with no motion, that is rest. The author then allows for the possibility that reaching and non-reaching could occur in the same instant if that instant is a common boundary between two times, like a point shared by two lines. This does not entail a conjunction of opposites, because it is a description of the same thing as reaching and not reaching simultaneously is impossible, but here we have two different aspects: the end of going and the beginning of return. Finally, the author presents another formulation using inclination (mayl). Motion arises from an inclination that is the cause of reaching. This inclination exists at the instant of reaching, as it is not a temporal entity like motion. Non-reaching (separation) requires a second inclination in another instant. Since two opposite inclinations cannot coexist in one instant, and instants cannot be successive without time, there must be a period of time between them when the body has no inclination, i.e., rest. Thus, the argument establishes the necessity of rest between opposite motions.

Key Terms
آن
الوصول
اللاوصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reaching the limit is a motion and therefore temporal.
Correction: Reaching is instantaneous; the beginning of return is non-reaching, not a motion.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why must reaching the end be instantaneous?
A) Because motion is inherently instantaneous.
B) Because if it were temporal, half the time would either contain it or not, leading to a contradiction.
C) Because the philosophers deny the existence of time.
D) Because reaching is a simple act that cannot be divided.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that reaching the limit must be instantaneous because a temporal duration would lead to a contradiction in half the time.

Instants, Succession, and the Indivisible Part
Sanusi discusses the nature of instants and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the implications of the distinctness of instants and how it leads to either successive instants composing time or rest.

Core Idea
If instants are distinct, time must be composed of indivisible instants or there must be rest between them.

Sanusi now examines the nature of instants (ānāt). He argues that if two consecutive instants are distinct, there are two possibilities: either no time between them (leading to composition of time from instants and existence of the indivisible part) or there is time with no motion (which is rest). He also addresses a potential objection involving a common boundary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two possibilities
If instants are distinct and no time between them, you get successive instants and the indivisible part. If there is time without motion, you get rest.
These are the only logical outcomes.
2
Analyze the objection
An objector says that reaching and non-reaching can occur in the same instant as a common boundary, like a point shared by two lines. This would make instants not necessarily distinct.
Sanusi uses this to show the necessity of distinctness is not absolute.
3
Grasp the response
Sanusi responds that this does not entail a conjunction of opposites, because the same instant has two aspects: end of going and beginning of return. It is not that something is both reaching and not reaching in the same respect.
Analogous to a body having both motion and blackness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعمدة في احتجاج الفلاسفة أن الوصول إلى النهاية آني إذ لو كان زمانياً ففي نصف ذلك الزمان إما أن يحصل الوصول فلا يكون في ذلك الزمان بل في نصفه، أو لا يحصل فلا يكون المفروض زمان وكذا الرجوع . أعني ابتداءه الذي قد يعبر عنه باللاوصول واللامماسة، والمباينة والمفارقة فلا يرد ما قيل إن كلاً من ذلك حركة وهي زمانية لا آنية ، ثم الآنات متغاير إن ضرورة فإن لم يكن بينهما زمان لزم تتالي الآنات، فيكون الامتداد الزماني الذي هو مقدار الحركة متألفاً من الآنات وهو منطبق على الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة فيلزم وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وإن كان بينهما زمان ولا حركة فيه تعين السكون ، ولما كان منع ضرورة تغاير الآنين ظاهر بناء على جواز أن يقع الوصول واللاوصول. أعني نهاية حركة الذهاب وبداية حركة الرجوع في آن واحد هو حد مشترك بين زمانيهما، كالنقطة الواحدة التي تكون بداية خط ونهاية خط آخر، وليس هذا من اجتماع النقيضين . أعني الوصول واللاوصول في شيء لأن معناه أن يصدق على الشيء أنه واصل وليس بواصل ، لا أن يحصل له الوصول، وابتداء الرجوع الذي هو لا وصول كما يحصل للجسم الحركة والسواد الذي هو لا حركة . قرر هذه الحجة بوجه آخر وهو أن الحركة إنما تصدر عن علة موجودة تسمى باعتبار كونها مزيلة للمتحرك من حد ما مقربه له إلى حد آخر ميلاً وهي العلة للوصول إلى الحد، وإن لم يسم باعتبار الاتصال ميلاً فتكون موجودة في آن الوصول، إذ ليس الميل من الأمور التي لا توجد إلا في الزمان كالحركة ثم اللاوصول أعني المباينة عن ذلك الحد لا تحدث إلا بعد حدوث ميل ثان في آن شان ضرورة امتناع اجتماع الميل إلى الشيء مع الميل عنه في آن واحد ولاستحالة تتالي الآنين يكون بينهما زمان يكون الجسم فيه عديم الميل فيكون عديم الحركة، وهو معنى السكون .
The main point in the philosophers' argument is that reaching the end is instantaneous, for if it were temporal, then in half of that time, either reaching occurs, so it would not be in that time but in its half, or it does not occur, so the assumed time would not be [the time of reaching]. Similarly for return, meaning its beginning, which may be expressed as non-reaching, non-tangency, separation, and disjunction. So what was said—that each of these is motion and motion is temporal, not instantaneous—does not apply. Then the instants are necessarily distinct. If there is no time between them, successive instants follow, so the temporal extension which is the measure of motion would be composed of instants, and it corresponds to the motion which corresponds to the distance, so the existence of the indivisible part follows. But if there is time between them with no motion, rest is determined. Since the denial of the necessity of the two instants being distinct is apparent, based on the permissibility that reaching and non-reaching—i.e., the end of the going motion and the beginning of the returning motion—occur in a single instant, which is a common boundary between their times, like a single point that is the beginning of one line and the end of another line, and this is not a case of the conjunction of opposites—I mean reaching and non-reaching—because its meaning is that something is described as reaching and not reaching, not that it obtains reaching and the beginning of return which is non-reaching, just as a body obtains motion and blackness which is not motion. He formulated this argument in another way: motion arises from an existing cause called, in consideration of its being what moves the mover from one limit and brings it near to another limit, an inclination (mayl), and it is the cause of reaching the limit, even if it is not called an inclination due to continuity. So it exists at the instant of reaching, since inclination is not among things that exist only in time, like motion. Then non-reaching—i.e., separation from that limit—does not occur except after the occurrence of a second inclination in a second instant, due to the impossibility of the conjunction of inclination toward something and inclination away from it in one instant. And due to the impossibility of successive instants, there is between them a time in which the body is devoid of inclination, so it is devoid of motion, and this is the meaning of rest.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument of the philosophers regarding the instantaneous nature of reaching a limit (al-wusul). The author begins by stating that reaching the end must be instantaneous (ani). If it were temporal, then in half that time either reaching occurs (making the whole time unnecessary) or it does not (contradicting the assumption). The same applies to the beginning of return. The author then addresses a potential objection that both reaching and return are motions and thus temporal. He responds that they are not motions in the strict sense; the beginning of return is 'non-reaching' (la wusul). Next, he discusses instants (anat): if two consecutive instants have no time between them, they are successive (tatali al-anat), implying time is composed of instants, which leads to the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza). If there is time between them with no motion, that is rest. The author then allows for the possibility that reaching and non-reaching could occur in the same instant if that instant is a common boundary between two times, like a point shared by two lines. This does not entail a conjunction of opposites, because it is a description of the same thing as reaching and not reaching simultaneously is impossible, but here we have two different aspects: the end of going and the beginning of return. Finally, the author presents another formulation using inclination (mayl). Motion arises from an inclination that is the cause of reaching. This inclination exists at the instant of reaching, as it is not a temporal entity like motion. Non-reaching (separation) requires a second inclination in another instant. Since two opposite inclinations cannot coexist in one instant, and instants cannot be successive without time, there must be a period of time between them when the body has no inclination, i.e., rest. Thus, the argument establishes the necessity of rest between opposite motions.

Key Terms
الآنات
تتالي الآنات
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two opposite states like reaching and non-reaching occur in the same instant, it is a logical contradiction.
Correction: There is no contradiction if they are aspects of different motions (end and beginning) and not simultaneous descriptions of the same state.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if there is no time between two distinct instants?
A) Rest is determined.
B) Motion is impossible.
C) Successive instants and the existence of the indivisible part.
D) The instants are actually the same.

Key Takeaway
The distinctness of instants forces either the composition of time from indivisible instants or the existence of periods of rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the concept of an indivisible instant. In your daily life, you experience time as a continuous flow. How does this philosophical analysis challenge your intuitive understanding of time? Are you willing to reexamine your assumption that time is infinitely divisible, even if it leads to a necessary conclusion about the existence of indivisible parts?
?
Sanusi uses an analogy of a point common to two lines. Think about a moment in your life when you faced a transition, perhaps ending something and starting something else. Do you consider that moment as a single instant or as two distinct instants? How does your answer affect your understanding of responsibility and change in your actions?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'reaching' and 'non-reaching' in different respects. Is there an area of your life where you have mistakenly thought that contradictory states must be happening at the same time, when in fact they apply in different respects? How can this insight help you resolve inner conflicts?

The Inclination Argument for Rest Between Opposite Motions
Sanusi presents the inclination-based proof for rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second formulation using inclination (mayl) to prove the necessity of rest between opposite motions.

Core Idea
Opposite inclinations cannot coexist, so between them there must be a period of rest.

Sanusi presents an alternative formulation of the argument based on the concept of inclination (mayl). He argues that motion arises from an inclination, which exists instantaneously at the moment of reaching. A new inclination in a different direction must occur in a separate instant, and since instants cannot be successive, a period of time devoid of inclination (i.e., rest) must intervene.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of inclination
Inclination (mayl) is the cause that moves a body from one limit to another. It exists at the instant of reaching because it is not temporal like motion.
Inclination is instantaneous.
2
Follow the necessity of a second inclination
Non-reaching (separation) requires a new, opposite inclination. This cannot happen in the same instant as the first inclination.
Opposite inclinations cannot coexist.
3
Conclude the existence of rest
Since instants cannot be successive, there must be a time between them with no inclination, meaning no motion – that is rest.
Rest is the absence of motion due to absence of inclination.

Objection on the Reality of the Instant and the Response
Objection and response on the reality of the instant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the instant is a mere mental construct and how the author refutes it by clarifying the philosophers' true position.

Core Idea
The instant is a real boundary of time, not a mere illusion.

After conceding the denial of partless atoms and that inclination is an efficient cause, a critic raises a problem: if the instant is just a limit, like a point, then it has no external reality and cannot host events like reaching or return. In this passage, the author presents this objection and then offers a response, clarifying what philosophers truly mean by 'instant'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is a back-and-forth argument: objection then response.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the critic states that the instant is a pure illusion (mawhum mahd) and argues that therefore reaching or return cannot occur in it.
The critic also argues that if the instant is indivisible only by illusion, then two inclinations could occur in the same instant.
3
Understand the response
The author first says the objection is weak. Then he clarifies that philosophers mean by 'instant' something absolutely indivisible, not just imaginary. They consider the division of time into past and future sufficient for the instant's reality as a boundary.
The impossibility of successive instants only follows if it leads to a partless atom indivisible even by illusion.
4
Reflect on the definition of 'instant'
In kalam, the instant is a real limit (taraf) of time, like a point of a line. It is not a temporal duration but a boundary that exists when time is conceived continuously.
This distinction is crucial for understanding events like motion and rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعضهم يرد عليه بعد تسليم نفي الجزء وثبوت كون الميل علة موجبة للوصول لا معدة ليلزم بقاؤه معه أن الآن عندكم طرف للزمان بمنزلة النقطة للخط فلا تحقق له في الخارج ما لم ينقطع الزمان، وإنما هو موهوم محض بما يفرض للزمان من الانقسام، فكيف يقع فيه الوصول أو الرجوع، وإن أردتم به زماناً لا ينقسم إلا بمجرد الوهم ، فلا ثم تغاير آني الميلين لجواز أن يقعا في آن واحد بحسب ماله من الانقسام الوهمي . ولو سلم فلا تم استحالة تتالي الأنين بهذا المعنى، وإنما يستحيل لو لزم منه وجود الجزء . أعني ما لا ينقسم بالوهم أيضاً، ولا خفاء في ضعف المنع الأول، وفي أنهم يعنون بالآن ما لا ينقسم أصلاً حيث يعللون استحالة تتالي الآنات باستلزامه وجود الجزء، وكأنهم يجعلون انقسام الزمان إلى الماضي والمستقبل كافياً في تحقق الآن أعني الطرف الذي يكون نهاية الماضي وبداية المستقبل، ويحكمون على كثير من الأشياء بأنها آنية لا زمانية .
Some object to him after conceding the denial of the part and the affirmation that the inclination is an efficient cause of reaching, not a preparatory cause, so that its remaining with it would be necessary, [saying] that the instant, according to you, is a limit of time like a point to a line, so it has no realization in external existence unless time is cut off, and it is a pure illusion by the division imagined for time. So how can reaching or return occur in it? And if you mean by it a time that is indivisible except by mere illusion, then there is no distinction between the two instants of the inclinations, because it is possible for them to occur in a single instant according to its imaginary division. Even if we grant that, the impossibility of successive instants in this sense does not hold; it would be impossible only if it entailed the existence of the part—I mean that which is indivisible even by illusion. And there is no doubt about the weakness of the first objection, and that by 'instant' they mean that which is absolutely indivisible, since they explain the impossibility of successive instants by its entailing the existence of the part. And it is as if they consider the division of time into past and future sufficient for the realization of the instant, I mean the limit which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future, and they judge many things to be instantaneous, not temporal.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents an objection raised by some (ba'duhum) against the philosophers' argument, and the author's response. The objector first concedes the rejection of the partless atom (al-juz') and accepts that inclination (mayl) is a direct cause (illa mujiba) of reaching, not merely a preparatory cause. The objection is that the instant (al-an) according to the philosophers is merely a limit (taraf) of time, like a point to a line, so it has no external reality except when time is cut off; it is purely imaginary (mawhum mahd). Therefore, how can actual reaching or return occur in it? If by 'instant' they mean a time that is indivisible only by illusion (wahm), then two inclinations could occur in the same imaginary instant, so their instants would not be distinct. Even if that is granted, the impossibility of successive instants (tatali al-anat) in this sense does not follow; it would only be impossible if it led to the existence of the partless atom (which is indivisible even by illusion). The author then states that the weakness (da'f) of this first objection is clear. The philosophers mean by 'instant' something that is absolutely indivisible (ma la yanqasim aslan), since they explain the impossibility of successive instants by it entailing the existence of the partless atom. They consider the division of time into past and future sufficient for the realization of the instant as a boundary (taraf) that is the end of the past and beginning of the future. They judge many things (such as instants, points, and perhaps contact) as instantaneous, not temporal. Thus, the objection fails because it misinterprets what philosophers mean by 'instant'.

Key Terms
آن
موهوم
الجزء
الميل
تتالي الآنات
المنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers' instant is merely a mental construct with no reality.
Correction: The passage clarifies that philosophers mean an absolutely indivisible limit of time, which is real as a boundary between past and future.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objector's criticism regarding the instant?
A) The instant is a real entity that can contain motion.
B) The instant is a mere illusion, so it cannot be a locus for reaching or return.
C) The instant is divisible and temporal.
D) The instant is the same as a partless atom.

Key Takeaway
The instant is a real boundary of time, not a mere illusion, and it is the locus for events like reaching and return.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily experience, do you treat moments as real boundaries or just convenient fictions? How might that assumption affect your sense of time and accountability?
?
When you make a decision or take an action, do you consider the instant of that action as a real point of change, or as an illusion? How would viewing it as real change your relationship with those moments?
?
If the instant is a real boundary dividing past and future, what does that mean for your regrets from the past and your hopes for the future? Are you living fully in the boundary of the present, or are you letting it slip away as a mere illusion?

Instant vs. Succession: When Does the Partless Atom Emerge?
A question and answer about why single instant does not imply partless atom but succession does, with analogy and incomplete objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single instant does not entail the partless atom, while successive instants do, and how the analogy with points and lines clarifies this distinction.

Core Idea
A single instant is a boundary; composition demands succession.

This passage tackles a subtle logical point: why does a single moment not prove the existence of the indivisible part, but two consecutive moments do? The answer hinges on the difference between a limit and a component. Just as a point is not a part of a line but a line composed of points implies parts, so too with instants. We'll also encounter an incomplete objection that tests the argument's implications for motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow. The passage begins with a question (qila) and answer (qulna) format.
The last part cuts off mid-sentence; we'll note it as an incomplete objection.
2
Identify the question and answer
The questioner asks why a single instant does not imply the partless atom but two successive instants do. The answer distinguishes between an instant as a limit versus as part of a composed extension.
3
Understand the point-line analogy
The author compares instants to geometric points: a single point does not make a line, but a line composed of points implies the existence of point-like parts. Similarly, a single instant is a boundary, not a building block.
This analogy reinforces the logical rule.
4
Notice the incomplete objection
The passage introduces an objection ('wa qad yuqal') that if the argument were valid, it would force successive instants in all rectilinear motion. The argument is cut off, but you can see the direction: it challenges the original claim.
This shows the dialectical style of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما بال تحقق الآن لم يستلزم وجود الجزء وتتالي الآنين استلزمه قلنا : لأنه على تقدير التتالي يكون الامتداد الذي هو مقدار الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة متألفاً من الآنات بزيادة واحد واحد ، ولا كذلك تحقق طرف للزمان هو عرض قائم به غير حال فيه حلول السرايان، وهذا كما أن ثبوت النقطة لا يستلزم الجزء، وكون الخط متألفاً من نقطة تستلزمه . وقد يقال لو صحت الحجة المذكورة لزم تتالي الآنات أو تخلل السكنات في كل حركة مستقيمة سيما إذا كانت على أجسام منضودة ، أو كان المتحرك لا يماس المسافة إلا بنقطة نقطة على التوالي كما إذا أدرنا كرة على سطح مستو أو ركبناها على دولاب دائر فوقه سطح مستو فإن أن الوصول إلى كل نقطة يغاير أن اللاوصول عنه
For it is said: Why is it that the realization of a single instant does not entail the existence of the part, while the succession of two instants does entail it? We say: Because on the supposition of succession, the extension which is the measure of motion corresponding to the distance becomes composed of instants by adding one by one. But this is not the case for the realization of a limit of time, which is an accident subsisting in it, not pervading it. And this is just as the establishment of a point does not entail the part, but a line being composed of points does entail it. And it may be said: If the aforementioned argument were valid, it would entail successive instants or the interpolation of rests in every rectilinear motion, especially if it occurs upon bodies arranged in a row, or if the moving thing does not touch the distance except point by point successively, as when we roll a sphere on a plane surface or mount it on a rotating wheel above a plane surface, for then the reaching of each point differs from the non-reaching of it.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage begins with a question and answer (qila ... qulna). The questioner asks: Why does the realization of a single instant (tahaqquq al-an) not imply the existence of the partless atom (al-juz'), while the succession of two instants (tatali al-anayn) does imply it? The answer distinguishes between the two cases. In the case of succession, the temporal extension (al-imtidad) that is the measure of motion is composed of instants added one by one. This composition leads to the existence of the partless atom. However, the realization of a single instant as a limit (taraf) of time is not like that; it is an accident (arad) subsisting in time, not pervading it. The author provides an analogy: a point (nuqta) does not entail the partless atom, but a line composed of points does entail it. So a single instant, like a point, is a boundary, not a building block. Then the passage transitions to a new objection introduced by 'wa qad yuqal' (and it may be said). This objection argues that if the philosophers' argument were valid, it would lead to successive instants or the interpolation of rests (sakanat) in every rectilinear motion. This is especially problematic for motion on a surface with bodies arranged in a row, or for a sphere rolling on a plane or a wheel rotating on a plane, where the moving object touches each point sequentially. The objection begins to state that the reaching of each point differs from the non-reaching of it, but the text cuts off here. This objection is incomplete in the provided window.

Key Terms
آن
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
النقطة
الامتداد
تتالي الآنات
السكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single instant, like any instant, implies the existence of the partless atom.
Correction: The author clarifies that a single instant is a boundary (limit) of time, not a constituent of extension. Only when instants are successive do they compose an extension that necessitates the partless atom.

Checkpoint
Why does a single instant not imply the partless atom according to the answer?
A) Because a single instant is a part of time.
B) Because a single instant is a limit, not a component, just as a point is not a part of a line.
C) Because the partless atom only exists in motion.
D) Because the philosophers deny the reality of instants.

Key Takeaway
A single instant is a mere limit of time, whereas successive instants build up extension and thereby entail the partless atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your moments of worship as isolated 'instants' that don't build up, or do you see them as successive steps that compose a continuous movement toward Allah? What would change if you truly believed each moment adds to the whole?
?
When you fall into sin, do you comfort yourself by thinking 'this one moment doesn't define me'? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of isolated 'instants' versus the reality of succession and character formation?
?
The objection mentions motion and rest (sakanat). Where in your spiritual journey are you 'in motion' and where are you 'at rest'? Are you content with periodic acts of obedience or are you striving for a continuous, uninterrupted trajectory?

First Set of Objections and Refutations on the State of Rest
First four objections and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first four objections raised against the necessary state of rest between two opposite motions and the author's refutations.

Core Idea
Each objection to the state of rest is answerable through proper causal analysis.

Here begins the first round of objections from those who deny that a state of rest must occur between two opposite motions. The objector claims that such a rest leads to four impossibilities, including its persistence, lack of cause, indeterminate time, and a paradoxical mountain-mustard seed example. The author then refutes each objection, revealing that the alleged impossibilities are not actual impossibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four objections
Read the passage and list each of the four objections (المحالات) that the objector presents against the state of rest.
2
Notice the refutation structure
Observe that each objection is followed by a concise refutation using the pattern 'ورد الأول...الثاني...الثالث...الرابع'.
The refutations appeal to natural force intensifying, equilibrium as a compeller, indivisible time, and the mustard seed's return.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how the refutations preserve the original claim that a state of rest necessarily occurs, by showing that the objector's assumptions are false or incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (احتج) المانع بأنه لو لزم هذا السكون لزم منه محالات . الأول : بقاؤه لبقاء التعادل على أن القاسرة إنما كانت بضعف ممانعة الهواء المخروق. الثاني : وقوعه لا عن سبب لأنه ليس طبيعياً والتقدير عدم القسر والإرادة . الثالث: وقوعه لا في زمان معين لأن كل زمان يفرض فأقل لمنه كاف . الرابع : وقوف الجبل الهابط لملاقاة الخردلة الصاعدة . ورد الأول بأن الطبيعية تتدرج إلى القوة والقاسرة إلى الضعف بحسب الذات. ولهذا يكون هبوط الحجر عند القرب من الأرض أقوى. والثاني بأن تعادل القوتين قاسر . والثالث بأنه يقع في زمان لا يقبل الانقسام العقلي، الرابع بأن الخردلة ترجع بمصادمة هواء الجبل قبل أن يلاقيها مع أن وقوفه مستبعد مستحيل) . أي القائل بعدم لزوم سكون بين الحركتين بوجوه : لا الأول : أنه لو لزم انتهاء الصاعدة القسرية إلى زمان سكون، لزم بقاؤه من غير تعقب هبوط، لأنه لا سبب لضعف القاسرة إلا مصادمة المخروق، وهي منتفية عند السكون . وأجيب بالمنع ، بل الطبيعة تتدرج إلى القوة، والقاسرة إلى الضعف بحسب الذات، ولهذا تكون حركة الحجر الهابط عند القرب من الأرض أشد ، وما ذكر ابن سينا من أنه لو لا مصادمات الهواء المخروق للقوة القسرية لوصل الحجر المرمي إلى سطح الفلك في حيز المنع . الثاني : أنه لو لزم لكان إما سكوناً طبيعياً وهو ظاهر البطلان، وإما قسرياً، والتقدير عدم القاسر إلى السكون . وأجيب بأن تعادل القوتين قاسر إلى السكون إلى أن تغلب الطبيعة، وفي كلام ابن سينا أن القوة القاسرة مسكنة للجسم في بعض الأحيان، وإلى أحد هذين المعنيين ينظر ما قال الإمام : أن هذا السكون لما كان ضروري الحصول لم يستدع علة كسائر اللوازم . الثالث : أنه لو لزم لضرورة تعادل القوتين أو استحالة تتالي الآنين، امتنع كونه في زمان ما لأن كل زمان يفرض فأقل منه كاف في دفع تلك الضرورة. وأجيب بأنه يقع في زمان لا يقبل الانقسام إلا بمجرد الوهم، لأنه الذي يمتنع أن يكون بعضه مقداراً للسكون وبعضه لا . الرابع : إنه يستلزم وقوف الجبل الهابط بملاقاته الخردلة الصاعدة. أجيب بأن الخردلة ترجع بمصادمة ريح الجبل، فسكونه يكون قبل ملاقاة الجبل . إن قيل : قد نشاهد أن الملاقاة كانت حال الصعود دون الرجوع، كما في السهم الصاعد بل كما في حركة اليد إلى فوق، فإنه يعلم قطعاً أن الرجوع لم يكن إلا بعد الملاقاة . قلنا : لو سلم فوقوف الجبل مستبعد لا مستحيل .
He said: The objector argued that if this state of rest necessarily occurred, then impossibilities would follow from it. The first: its persistence due to the persistence of the equilibrium, given that the compulsive force was only due to the weakness of the resistance of the penetrated air. The second: its occurrence without a cause, because it is not natural and the assumption is the absence of compulsion and will. The third: its occurrence in no definite time, because every time one supposes, its lesser part is sufficient. The fourth: the halting of a descending mountain to meet an ascending mustard seed. The first was refuted by the fact that the natural force gradually increases in strength and the compulsive force decreases in strength according to essence, and for this reason the descent of a stone near the earth is stronger. The second: that the equilibrium of the two forces is a compeller. The third: that it occurs in a time that does not admit rational division. The fourth: that the mustard seed returns due to collision with the air of the mountain before meeting it, even though its halting is improbable, not impossible. That is, the one who says that no state of rest necessarily occurs between the two motions [argues] with points: No, the first: that if the end of the ascending compulsive [motion] necessarily leads to a time of rest, then its persistence would follow without a descent following it, because there is no cause for the weakness of the compulsive force except the collision with the penetrated [air], and that is absent during rest. The answer is by denial; rather, the natural force gradually increases in strength and the compulsive force decreases according to essence, and for this reason the motion of a descending stone near the earth is more intense. And what Ibn Sina mentioned, that were it not for the collisions of the penetrated air against the compulsive force, the thrown stone would reach the surface of the celestial sphere, is in the realm of denial. The second: that if it necessarily occurred, then it would be either a natural rest, which is obviously false, or a compelled rest, and the assumption is the absence of a compeller to rest. The answer is that the equilibrium of the two forces is a compeller to rest until nature prevails. And in the words of Ibn Sina, the compulsive force is a cause of rest for the body at times. And to one of these two meanings refers what the Imam said: that this rest, since it is necessary in occurrence, does not require a cause like other necessary concomitants. The third: that if it necessarily occurred due to the necessity of the equilibrium of the two forces or the impossibility of the succession of two 'nows', then it would be impossible for it to be in any time, because every time one supposes, a lesser part of it is sufficient to remove that necessity. The answer is that it occurs in a time that does not admit division except by mere illusion, because it is that for which it is impossible that some part of it be a measure for the rest and some part not. The fourth: that it entails the halting of a descending mountain upon its meeting an ascending mustard seed. The answer is that the mustard seed returns due to the collision of the mountain's wind, so its halting occurs before meeting the mountain. If it is said: we observe that the meeting occurs during ascent, not return, like the ascending arrow, and like the motion of the hand upwards, for it is known with certainty that the return did not occur except after the meeting. We say: even if granted, the halting of the mountain is improbable, not impossible.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections (احتجاجات) raised by a hypothetical objector against the position that a state of rest necessarily occurs between two opposite motions (such as a body that first moves upward and then downward). The objector claims that such a rest would entail four absurdities (محالات). The author or commentator then refutes each objection in turn. After that, the passage introduces another set of arguments ('أي القائل بعدم لزوم سكون') with further objections and answers, likely from a different source or more detailed analysis. The first objection argues that if rest occurs, it would persist because the equilibrium of forces remains. The refutation explains that natural force intensifies and compulsive force weakens essentially, not just due to air resistance. The second objection says rest would be uncaused; the answer says equilibrium itself is a compeller. The third objection says rest cannot occur in any specific time, since any time can be divided; the answer posits an indivisible moment (the atom of time). The fourth objection uses the absurd example of a descending mountain meeting an ascending mustard seed; the answer explains that the seed would be pushed back by the mountain's air before contact, and even if not, the stopping of the mountain is improbable, not impossible. The commentator engages with Ibn Sina's views at several points, rejecting or qualifying them. Overall, this passage demonstrates the dialectical method in kalam: raising objections, answering them, and defending the coherence of the original position.
