Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The state of rest between opposite motions leads to impossibilities, so it cannot occur.
Correction: The author refutes each objection, showing that the alleged impossibilities are not actual impossibilities when proper causal analysis is applied.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the third objection that the rest cannot occur in any specific time?
A) By denying that the rest occurs at all
B) By arguing that the rest occurs in a time that does not admit rational division
C) By claiming that the rest is eternal
D) By saying the rest occurs in a time that is infinitely divisible

Key Takeaway
The first set of objections is successfully refuted by appealing to natural force intensification, equilibrium as a compeller, indivisible time, and a plausible physical explanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt that seems impossible to answer, do you examine each part carefully like the refutations here, or do you let the whole doubt unsettle you? How can the method of breaking down objections into specific points strengthen your faith?
?
The refutation of the second objection says equilibrium itself is a compeller. In your life, when you are in a state of seeming inactivity or balance, do you recognize that God may be the cause in that stillness? What would change if you saw equilibrium as a divine compeller rather than mere absence?
?
The fourth refutation shows that what appears absurd may only be improbable. Are there commands of Allah that seem 'absurd' to you? Will you seek deeper understanding before rejecting them, trusting that His wisdom is beyond your immediate perception?

Second Set of Objections and Responses on the State of Rest
Second set of four objections and responses, plus a final exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second set of objections and their more detailed responses, including the role of equilibrium and the concept of an indivisible moment.

Core Idea
Equilibrium acts as a compeller to rest, and the rest can occur in an indivisible moment of time.

After the initial refutations, a more detailed discussion begins with 'أي القائل بعدم لزوم سكون'. This section presents further objections regarding the persistence of rest, its cause, its timing, and the mountain-mustard seed example again, but now answered with more nuanced arguments, including the notion of a time that does not admit rational division and engagement with Ibn Sina's views.

Guided Path
1
Read the second set of four objections
Identify each objection: persistence without cause, nature of rest, timing, and the mountain-mustard seed example.
2
Compare with the first set
Notice how the objections are similar but the refutations go deeper, referencing Ibn Sina and the Imam.
3
Focus on the third answer
The answer about indivisible time is crucial: it occurs in a time that cannot be divided except in illusion.
This is the same concept as the 'atom of time' (al-zaman alladhi la yaqbal al-inqisam).
4
Understand the final exchange
The last part contains an additional objection and answer about the mountain and mustard seed, concluding that the halting is improbable, not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (احتج) المانع بأنه لو لزم هذا السكون لزم منه محالات . الأول : بقاؤه لبقاء التعادل على أن القاسرة إنما كانت بضعف ممانعة الهواء المخروق. الثاني : وقوعه لا عن سبب لأنه ليس طبيعياً والتقدير عدم القسر والإرادة . الثالث: وقوعه لا في زمان معين لأن كل زمان يفرض فأقل لمنه كاف . الرابع : وقوف الجبل الهابط لملاقاة الخردلة الصاعدة . ورد الأول بأن الطبيعية تتدرج إلى القوة والقاسرة إلى الضعف بحسب الذات. ولهذا يكون هبوط الحجر عند القرب من الأرض أقوى. والثاني بأن تعادل القوتين قاسر . والثالث بأنه يقع في زمان لا يقبل الانقسام العقلي، الرابع بأن الخردلة ترجع بمصادمة هواء الجبل قبل أن يلاقيها مع أن وقوفه مستبعد مستحيل) . أي القائل بعدم لزوم سكون بين الحركتين بوجوه : لا الأول : أنه لو لزم انتهاء الصاعدة القسرية إلى زمان سكون، لزم بقاؤه من غير تعقب هبوط، لأنه لا سبب لضعف القاسرة إلا مصادمة المخروق، وهي منتفية عند السكون . وأجيب بالمنع ، بل الطبيعة تتدرج إلى القوة، والقاسرة إلى الضعف بحسب الذات، ولهذا تكون حركة الحجر الهابط عند القرب من الأرض أشد ، وما ذكر ابن سينا من أنه لو لا مصادمات الهواء المخروق للقوة القسرية لوصل الحجر المرمي إلى سطح الفلك في حيز المنع . الثاني : أنه لو لزم لكان إما سكوناً طبيعياً وهو ظاهر البطلان، وإما قسرياً، والتقدير عدم القاسر إلى السكون . وأجيب بأن تعادل القوتين قاسر إلى السكون إلى أن تغلب الطبيعة، وفي كلام ابن سينا أن القوة القاسرة مسكنة للجسم في بعض الأحيان، وإلى أحد هذين المعنيين ينظر ما قال الإمام : أن هذا السكون لما كان ضروري الحصول لم يستدع علة كسائر اللوازم . الثالث : أنه لو لزم لضرورة تعادل القوتين أو استحالة تتالي الآنين، امتنع كونه في زمان ما لأن كل زمان يفرض فأقل منه كاف في دفع تلك الضرورة. وأجيب بأنه يقع في زمان لا يقبل الانقسام إلا بمجرد الوهم، لأنه الذي يمتنع أن يكون بعضه مقداراً للسكون وبعضه لا . الرابع : إنه يستلزم وقوف الجبل الهابط بملاقاته الخردلة الصاعدة. أجيب بأن الخردلة ترجع بمصادمة ريح الجبل، فسكونه يكون قبل ملاقاة الجبل . إن قيل : قد نشاهد أن الملاقاة كانت حال الصعود دون الرجوع، كما في السهم الصاعد بل كما في حركة اليد إلى فوق، فإنه يعلم قطعاً أن الرجوع لم يكن إلا بعد الملاقاة . قلنا : لو سلم فوقوف الجبل مستبعد لا مستحيل .
He said: The objector argued that if this state of rest necessarily occurred, then impossibilities would follow from it. The first: its persistence due to the persistence of the equilibrium, given that the compulsive force was only due to the weakness of the resistance of the penetrated air. The second: its occurrence without a cause, because it is not natural and the assumption is the absence of compulsion and will. The third: its occurrence in no definite time, because every time one supposes, its lesser part is sufficient. The fourth: the halting of a descending mountain to meet an ascending mustard seed. Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections (احتجاجات) raised by a hypothetical objector against the position that a state of rest necessarily occurs between two opposite motions (such as a body that first moves upward and then downward). The objector claims that such a rest would entail four absurdities (محالات). The author or commentator then refutes each objection in turn. After that, the passage introduces another set of arguments ('أي القائل بعدم لزوم سكون') with further objections and answers, likely from a different source or more detailed analysis. The first objection argues that if rest occurs, it would persist because the equilibrium of forces remains. The refutation explains that natural force intensifies and compulsive force weakens essentially, not just due to air resistance. The second objection says rest would be uncaused; the answer says equilibrium itself is a compeller. The third objection says rest cannot occur in any specific time, since any time can be divided; the answer posits an indivisible moment (the atom of time). The fourth objection uses the absurd example of a descending mountain meeting an ascending mustard seed; the answer explains that the seed would be pushed back by the mountain's air before contact, and even if not, the stopping of the mountain is improbable, not impossible. The commentator engages with Ibn Sina's views at several points, rejecting or qualifying them. Overall, this passage demonstrates the dialectical method in kalam: raising objections, answering them, and defending the coherence of the original position.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The equilibrium of forces acts as a cause, and the rest can occur in an indivisible moment of time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the third objection (timing of rest) answered?
A) The rest occurs in no time at all

D) The rest occurs in a time measured by the movement of the mountain

Key Takeaway
The second set of objections is refuted by deeper causal analysis, including the notion of equilibrium as a compeller and the concept of an indivisible moment, showing the state of rest remains necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When opposing forces pull you—desire for this world and duty to Allah—do you recognize the moments of equilibrium as a divinely appointed pause? Will you use that pause to let your natural faith regain strength, or will you resist it and try to force motion?
?
The author refutes the idea that rest is impossible because it would be unnatural. Yet often, when Allah places you in a state of waiting or rest, do you consider it wasted time? How will you now view those moments of stillness—as a necessary part of His design for your trajectory?

Definition of Rest (Sukun) and Its Two Meanings
Al-Taftazani defines sukun and explains its existential and privative meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of rest (sukun) in Islamic theology – as an existential reality opposing motion and as a privation of motion – and the logical distinction between them.

Core Idea
Rest has an existential meaning and a privative meaning; they are not the same.

The concept of rest (sukun) is a counterpart to motion. Islamic theologians debated whether rest is a real existing attribute or merely the absence of motion. In this passage, al-Taftazani presents two definitions: one where rest is a positive thing preserving relations or species, and another where it is simply the absence of motion in something capable of it. Let's explore these two meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the discussion. Notice that the passage begins by quoting the matn and then expands on it.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'sukun' and 'haraka'.
2
Distinguish the two meanings
Identify the first existential meaning (rest as preservation of relations/species) and the second privative meaning (rest as absence of motion). How do they differ in their logical relationship to motion?
The first meaning involves real opposition; the second involves privation-possession opposition.
3
Consider the example of the body in water
Reflect on the example of a body standing in flowing water. Why does the Imam consider it neither moving nor at rest? What is the author's comment on this?
This example tests the boundaries of the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثامن السكون في الأين قال: (المبحث الثامن السكون في الأين حفظ النسب، وفي غيره حفظ النوع فيضاد الحركة، وقيل عدم الحركة عما شأنه فعدم ملكه). من يقابل الحركة فيقع في المقولات الأربع. أما في الأين فيعني به حفظ النسب الحاصلة بالفعل للجسم إلى الأشياء ذوات الأوضاع بأن يكون مستقراً في المكان الواحد، وأما في الثلاثة الباقية فنعني به حفظ النوع الحاصل بالفعل من غير تغير، وذلك بأن يقف في الكم من غير نمو وذبول وتخلخل وتكاثف وفي الكيف من غير اشتداد أو ضعف وفي الوضع من غير تبدل إلى وضع آخر، فهو بهذا المعنى أمر وجودي مضاد للحركة. وقد يراد به عدم الحركة عما من شأنه فيكون بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة، ويقيد عما شأنه يخرج عدم حركة الأعراض والمفارقات من والأجسام في آن ابتداء الحركة أو انتهائها بل في كل آن وكذا الأجسام التي يمتنع خروجها عن أحيازها ككليات الأفلاك والعناصر . قال الإمام ومن الأجسام الخالية عن الحركة والسكون والأجسام التي لا نماسها ما يحيط بها أكثر من آن واحد كالجسم الواقف في الماء السيال فإنه ليس بمتحرك لعدم تبدل أوضاعه بالنسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة عنه، ولا ساكن لعدم استقراره في مكان واحد زماناً وفيه نظر .
The eighth section: Rest in place. He said: (The eighth section: Rest in place is the preservation of relations, and in other categories it is the preservation of the species, so it opposes motion; and it is said: absence of motion from that which is capable of it, so it is the absence of the possessor). It is what opposes motion, so it falls into the four categories. As for in place, it means the preservation of the relations actually existing for the body to things having positions, by being stable in one place. As for the other three, we mean by it the preservation of the actual species without change, such as stopping in quantity without growth, withering, rarefaction, condensation; in quality without intensification or weakening; in position without changing to another position. So in this sense it is an existential matter opposing motion. And it may be meant as the absence of motion from that which is capable of it, so there is between them the opposition of privation and possession, and it is restricted by 'that which is capable' to exclude the absence of motion of accidents, separate substances, and bodies at the moment of beginning or ending motion, or at any moment, as well as bodies whose emergence from their places is impossible, such as the wholes of the spheres and elements. The Imam said: Among bodies there are those devoid of motion and rest, such as bodies whose contact with what surrounds them is more than one moment, like a body standing in flowing water; it is not in motion because its positions relative to external things do not change, and it is not at rest because it does not settle in one place for a duration. And there is a consideration in this.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage defines sukun (rest) and explains two distinct meanings. First, it presents the definition from the matn: rest in place is the preservation of relations, and in other categories it preserves the species, thus opposing motion. The commentary then expands: in place, it means a body maintaining its actual relations to surrounding objects, being stationary; in the other three categories (quantity, quality, position), it means preserving the species without change. This first meaning is an existential reality (wujudi) that truly opposes motion. Second, rest can mean the absence of motion from something capable of it, which is a privative concept (adam al-malaka), not real opposition. The restriction 'camma sha'nuhu' excludes things incapable of motion. The Imam gives an example of a body in flowing water that is neither moving nor at rest in the strict sense, but the author notes that this view has a problem ('fihi nazar'). The passage is a definition and classification of rest.

تقابل العدم والملكة
وجودي
عدم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rest is always a privation, i.e., merely the absence of motion.
Correction: The passage shows that rest has an existential meaning (preservation of relations/species) that is a real attribute opposing motion, not just a lack of motion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Rest can be understood as an existential attribute that preserves relations or species, or as a privation of motion in something capable of motion.

?
If rest can be a positive attribute that preserves what is good, what does that imply about how you should actively maintain your faith? Are you content with merely avoiding sin, or are you actively preserving your relationship with Allah?

The Correct View of Opposition Between Motion and Rest
Al-Taftazani presents the consensus and correct view on motion-rest opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motion and rest oppose each other in both directions (from and to, beginning and end) and why this is the correct view according to al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
Motion and rest oppose each other fully, not in just one direction.

Al-Taftazani begins by stating the accepted opposition between motion and rest. He then identifies a disagreement about the precise direction of this opposition. By analyzing the different possibilities, he settles on the view that motion and rest oppose each other in both directions, because this alone satisfies the logical definition of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the translation to understand the overall meaning. Notice that there is consensus on the fact of opposition, but disagreement on its exact characterization.
The passage uses technical terms like 'مبدأ' (beginning) and 'منتهى' (end).
2
Identify the three possible views
Look for the three possibilities when motion is considered in distance: rest at the beginning, at the end, or both. Then notice the parallel for rest in place: motion from, to, or both. Al-Taftazani considers all six but groups them.
3
Understand why the third view is correct
Al-Taftazani states that the correct view is the last one (both directions). The reason is that only this view satisfies the definition of opposition (تقابل). Reflect on why a one-directional opposition would be incomplete.
The definition of opposition is not given explicitly here, but it implies mutual exclusion in both directions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (ثم إنه يقابل الحركة) منه إليه جميعاً، إلا إذا خص القابل بما يطرأ على السكون أو يطرأ عليه السكون). لا خلاف في تقابل الحركة والسكون وإنما الخلاف في أنه إذا اعتبرت الحركة في المسافة فالمقابل له السكون في المبدأ أو المنتهى أو كلاهما، وإذا اعتبر السكون في المكان فالمقابل له الحركة منه أو إليه أو كلاهما، والحق هو الأخير لصدق حد التقابل عليه، نعم لو أريد بالسكون المقابل للحركة، ما يطرأ على الحركة فهو السكون في المنتهي أو ما يطرأ عليه الحركة فهو السكون في المبدأ، وكذا في جانب الحركة، فإن ما يطرأ على السكون هو الحركة منه وما يطرأ عليه السكون هو الحركة إليه، وما يقال إن السكون في المنتهى كمال للحركة، وكمال الشيء لا يقابله، وأن الحركة تتأدى إلى السكون في المنتهى، والشيء لا يتأدى إلى مقابله فمردود بمنع صغرى الأول وكبرى الثاني. فإن السكون كمال للمتحرك، لا للحركة، والحركة تنتهي إلى عدمها، وهو مقابل قطعاً، وأما احتجاج ابن سينا بأن السكون ليس عدم أية حركة اتفقت وإلا لكان المتحرك في مكان ساكناً من حيث عدم حركته في مكان آخر، بل هو عدم الحركة في المكان الذي يتأتى فيه الحركة، والحركة في المكان نفسه مفارقة المكان بعينه وذلك بالحركة عنه، لا بالحركة إليه. فجوابه أن السكون عدم الحركة في مكان ما بمعنى عموم السلب، أي لا يتحرك في شيء من الأمكنة فيقابل الحركة في مكان ما
He said: Then it (rest) opposes motion in its 'from' and 'to' together, unless the opposite is specified as what supervenes upon rest or upon which rest supervenes. There is no disagreement about the opposition of motion and rest; the disagreement is only about whether, when motion is considered in the distance, the opposite of it is rest at the beginning or end or both; and when rest is considered in place, the opposite of it is motion from or to or both. The correct view is the last, because the definition of opposition applies to it. Yes, if by rest that opposes motion is meant what supervenes upon motion, it is rest at the end; or what motion supervenes upon, it is rest at the beginning. Similarly on the side of motion: what supervenes upon rest is motion from, and what rest supervenes upon is motion to. And what is said that rest at the end is a perfection of motion, and the perfection of a thing does not oppose it, and that motion leads to rest at the end, and a thing does not lead to its opposite, is refuted by denying the minor premise of the first and the major premise of the second. For rest is a perfection of the moving thing, not of motion; and motion ends in its absence, which is certainly opposite. As for Ibn Sina's argument that rest is not the absence of any motion whatsoever (otherwise the mover in one place would be at rest with respect to absence of motion in another place), but it is the absence of motion in the place where motion is possible, and motion in the same place is leaving that place, which is motion from, not motion to. The answer is that rest is the absence of motion in a place in the sense of universal negation, i.e., it does not move in any place, so it opposes motion in a place.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage discusses how rest opposes motion. The author first cites the matn that rest opposes motion from and to, unless specified. Then he explains there is consensus on the opposition, but disagreement on its precise characterization: when motion is considered in a distance, rest opposes it at the start, end, or both; when rest is in a place, motion opposes it from or to or both. The correct view is both directions because that fits the definition of opposition. Then he clarifies that if rest is defined relative to what supervenes on motion, it is rest at the end; if motion supervenes on rest, it is rest at the beginning, and vice versa. He refutes an objection that rest at the end is a perfection of motion, since rest perfects the moving subject, not motion itself, and motion ends in its absence. Finally, he presents Ibn Sina's argument that rest is not absence of any motion but specifically in the same place, and answers that rest is the universal negation of motion in any place, thus opposing motion in a place. This is a logical analysis of opposition (taqabul).

Key Terms
تقابل
حركة
سكون
مبدأ
منتهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think rest only opposes motion at the end (e.g., when motion stops) or only at the beginning (e.g., when motion starts from rest).
Correction: The correct view is that rest opposes motion in both directions: from and to (beginning and end).

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, which view of the opposition between motion and rest is correct?
A) Only rest at the beginning
B) Only rest at the end
C) Both beginning and end
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that motion and rest oppose each other in both directions (from and to, beginning and end).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, have you ever assumed a simple opposition between two things (like good and bad) when there is actually a hidden direction or nuance? How might this lesson's insistence on 'both directions' change your perspective?
?
When you examine your beliefs about fundamental issues, do you tend to accept easy dichotomies or do you push yourself to consider all logical possibilities—even uncomfortable ones? What step will you take to practice this precision?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly simple relationship like motion-rest has depth. Are you willing to re-evaluate other 'simple' opposites in your understanding of faith and reality, even if it challenges your old way of thinking?

Clarifying Definitions and Refuting the Perfection Objection
Al-Taftazani clarifies definitions and refutes a logical objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rest and motion are defined relative to each other and why the objection that rest at the end is a perfection of motion does not invalidate their opposition.

Core Idea
Rest perfects the moving thing, not motion itself, so it can still oppose motion.

After settling the correct basic view, al-Taftazani provides clarifications: rest can be defined by what supervenes on motion (rest at the end) or what motion supervenes on (rest at the beginning). He then refutes an objection that rest at the end is a perfection of motion and cannot be its opposite. He shows that rest perfects the moving subject, not the motion, and that motion ends in its own absence (a true opposite).

Guided Path
1
Read the clarifications and the objection
First, read the part where al-Taftazani explains how rest and motion are defined in terms of what supervenes on what. Then read the objection that rest at the end is a perfection of motion.
Notice the parallelism: 'ما يطرأ على الحركة' (what supervenes on motion) vs. 'ما يطرأ عليه الحركة' (what motion supervenes on).
2
Analyze the refutation
Al-Taftazani refutes the objection by denying the minor premise (that rest is a perfection of motion) and the major premise (that motion does not lead to its opposite). Understand each denial.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the refutation is valid: rest is a perfection of the moving thing (المتحرك), not of motion itself. And motion ends in its own absence (عدمها), which is indeed an opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ibn Sina's Argument on Rest and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes Ibn Sina's specific argument about rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of rest as the absence of motion in a specific place, and al-Taftazani's refutation that rest is the universal negation of motion in any place.

Core Idea
Rest is the absence of motion in any place, not just in the place where motion is possible.

Al-Taftazani presents an argument attributed to Ibn Sina, who claims that rest is not simply the absence of any motion (otherwise a moving object would be at rest with respect to other places), but specifically the absence of motion in the place where motion is possible. Al-Taftazani responds by defining rest as the universal negation of motion, meaning the object does not move in any place at all, and this universal negation opposes motion in a particular place.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's argument carefully
Ibn Sina distinguishes between 'absence of any motion' and 'absence of motion in a specific place where motion is possible'. He uses the example: if a body moves in one place, it is not at rest simply because it lacks motion in another place.
The argument is about the correct definition of rest.
2
Understand al-Taftazani's response
Al-Taftazani answers that rest is the absence of motion in a place in the sense of universal negation (عموم السلب), meaning the body does not move in any place whatsoever. This universal negation then opposes motion in a particular place (which is a positive existence).
Universal negation is 'لا يتحرك في شيء من الأمكنة'.
3
Compare the two positions
Ibn Sina restricts rest to a specific place; al-Taftazani makes it universal. Which one better fits the concept of opposition? Consider that if rest were only in a specific place, a body could be both moving and at rest (in different places) without contradiction, which would weaken the opposition.
Al-Taftazani's view maintains the mutual exclusivity of motion and rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (ثم إنه يقابل الحركة) منه إليه جميعاً، إلا إذا خص القابل بما يطرأ على السكون أو يطرأ عليه السكون). لا خلاف في تقابل الحركة والسكون وإنما الخلاف في أنه إذا اعتبرت الحركة في المسافة فالمقابل له السكون في المبدأ أو المنتهى أو كلاهما، وإذا اعتبر السكون في المكان فالمقابل له الحركة منه أو إليه أو كلاهما، والحق هو الأخير لصدق حد التقابل عليه، نعم لو أريد بالسكون المقابل للحركة، ما يطرأ على الحركة فهو السكون في المنتهي أو ما يطرأ عليه الحركة فهو السكون في المبدأ، وكذا في جانب الحركة، فإن ما يطرأ على السكون هو الحركة منه وما يطرأ عليه السكون هو الحركة إليه، وما يقال إن السكون في المنتهى كمال للحركة، وكمال الشيء لا يقابله، وأن الحركة تتأدى إلى السكون في المنتهى، والشيء لا يتأدى إلى مقابله فمردود بمنع صغرى الأول وكبرى الثاني. فإن السكون كمال للمتحرك، لا للحركة، والحركة تنتهي إلى عدمها، وهو مقابل قطعاً، وأما احتجاج ابن سينا بأن السكون ليس عدم أية حركة اتفقت وإلا لكان المتحرك في مكان ساكناً من حيث عدم حركته في مكان آخر، بل هو عدم الحركة في المكان الذي يتأتى فيه الحركة، والحركة في المكان نفسه مفارقة المكان بعينه وذلك بالحركة عنه، لا بالحركة إليه. فجوابه أن السكون عدم الحركة في مكان ما بمعنى عموم السلب، أي لا يتحرك في شيء من الأمكنة فيقابل الحركة في مكان ما
He said: Then it (rest) opposes motion in its 'from' and 'to' together, unless the opposite is specified as what supervenes upon rest or upon which rest supervenes. There is no disagreement about the opposition of motion and rest; the disagreement is only about whether, when motion is considered in the distance, the opposite of it is rest at the beginning or end or both; and when rest is considered in place, the opposite of it is motion from or to or both. The correct view is the last, because the definition of opposition applies to it. Yes, if by rest that opposes motion is meant what supervenes upon motion, it is rest at the end; or what motion supervenes upon, it is rest at the beginning. Similarly on the side of motion: what supervenes upon rest is motion from, and what rest supervenes upon is motion to. And what is said that rest at the end is a perfection of motion, and the perfection of a thing does not oppose it, and that motion leads to rest at the end, and a thing does not lead to its opposite, is refuted by denying the minor premise of the first and the major premise of the second. For rest is a perfection of the moving thing, not of motion; and motion ends in its absence, which is certainly opposite. As for Ibn Sina's argument that rest is not the absence of any motion whatsoever (otherwise the mover in one place would be at rest with respect to absence of motion in another place), but it is the absence of motion in the place where motion is possible, and motion in the same place is leaving that place, which is motion from, not motion to. The answer is that rest is the absence of motion in a place in the sense of universal negation, i.e., it does not move in any place, so it opposes motion in a place.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage discusses how rest opposes motion. The author first cites the matn that rest opposes motion from and to, unless specified. Then he explains there is consensus on the opposition, but disagreement on its precise characterization: when motion is considered in a distance, rest opposes it at the start, end, or both; when rest is in a place, motion opposes it from or to or both. The correct view is both directions because that fits the definition of opposition. Then he clarifies that if rest is defined relative to what supervenes on motion, it is rest at the end; if motion supervenes on rest, it is rest at the beginning, and vice versa. He refutes an objection that rest at the end is a perfection of motion, since rest perfects the moving subject, not motion itself, and motion ends in its absence. Finally, he presents Ibn Sina's argument that rest is not absence of any motion but specifically in the same place, and answers that rest is the universal negation of motion in any place, thus opposing motion in a place. This is a logical analysis of opposition (taqabul).

سكون
عدم
عموم السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rest is merely the absence of motion in a specific place where motion is possible, as Ibn Sina argued.
Correction: Rest is the universal negation of motion in any place, which opposes motion in a place.

Checkpoint

A) Absence of motion in a specific place
B) Absence of any motion at all
C) Universal negation of motion (no motion in any place)
D) The same as motion

Key Takeaway
Rest is the universal negation of motion (no motion in any place), which opposes motion in a place.

Are you ever truly at rest, or is your 'rest' merely the absence of one type of motion while other motions continue? What does that reveal about your need for spiritual stillness?
?
In your relationship with Allah, do you tend to limit His 'rest' or 'action' to specific places or times, like a masjid or Ramadan, similar to Ibn Sina's restrictive definition? Contradiction of Rest Due to Its Container
Al-Taftazani explains the basis for contradiction of rest and its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the contradiction of rest derives from the contradiction of its container, and that rest is classified into natural, forced, and voluntary types.

Core Idea
Rest is not contradictory by itself; its container makes it so.

This lesson introduces a key nuance in kalam physics: rest (sukun) can be contradictory, but not intrinsically—rather due to the nature of its container. Understanding this clarifies how seemingly static states can be opposite. We will also see the classification of rest based on its cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's concise style come through.
Note that the text is incomplete; it ends mid-sentence.
2
Identify the basis of contradiction
The passage says contradiction of rest is due to contradiction of what it is in. Look for the phrase 'لتضاد ما فيه' and the examples given.
3
List the types of rest
The passage names natural, forced, voluntary, and 'based on natural' rest. Notice how these categories relate to the cause of rest.
The fourth category may be incomplete in the text.
4
Understand the key distinction
Reflect on why this distinction matters: rest is not inherently contradictory; it becomes so through its container or cause.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.

قال : وتضاد السكون يكون لتضاد ما فيه كالسكون في المكان الأعلى والأسفل، أو في الحرارة أو البرودة، ويكون طبيعياً وقسرياً وإرادياً ومستنداً لطبيعي، هو الطبيعة على
He said: And the contradiction of rest is due to the contradiction of what it is in, like rest in the upper and lower place, or in heat or cold; and it is natural, forced, and voluntary, and [rest] based on the natural is nature upon
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage briefly discusses how rest can have contradictory forms. The contradiction of rest arises from the contradiction of the thing in which it is found (tadadd ma fihi). For example, rest in an upper place vs. a lower place, or in hot vs. cold, are contradictory because the places or qualities are contradictory. The author then classifies rest into types: natural (tabi'i), forced (qasri), voluntary (iradi), and that which is based on the natural ('mastanidin li-tabi'i'), but the text cuts off at 'huwa al-tabi'atu 'ala' (it is nature upon...). The passage provides a classification of rest based on its cause or container. The incomplete ending suggests the author was about to explain further, possibly distinguishing between essential and accidental types. This passage is a classification and enumeration of types of rest.

Definition and Types of Relative Accident (al-Idafah)
Defines al-idafah and distinguishes between real and common relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-idafah as a repeated relation and the distinction between real relative and common relative.

Core Idea
Al-idafah is a relation that cannot be known without another relation – a double dependence.

In this passage, Sanusi introduces al-idafah (the relative accident) as one of the remaining relative accidents. He defines it as a 'repeated relation' and distinguishes between the relation itself (real relative) and the composite of relation and substrate (common relative). This distinction is foundational for understanding how kalam treats relational entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the definition of al-idafah as a repeated relation.
Pay attention to the terms 'mudaf haqiqi' and 'mudaf mashhuri'.
2
Identify the two types
Locate where the author distinguishes between real relative (the relation itself) and common relative (the composite). Note the criterion used.
The real relative is the relation, the common relative includes the substrate.
3
Reflect on the philosophers' approach
Consider why the philosophers begin with the common relative: because composites are easier to grasp initially.
This shows a pedagogical choice, not a metaphysical priority.
4
Understand the key definition
Grapple with the phrase 'whose quiddity is not intelligible except by reference to another' and its clarification that 'another' is itself related back to the first.
This captures the double relational dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في باقي الأعراض النسبية قال : الفصل الخامس: في باقي الأعراض النسبية، منها الإضافة وهي النسبة المتكررة التي لا تعقل إلا بالقياس إلى نسبة معقولة بالقياس إليها، وهذه تسمى مضافاً حقيقياً. والمركب منها ومن معروضها مضافاً مشهورياً، ويشملها قولهم ما لا تعقل ماهيته إلا بقياس إلى الغير. إلا أن المراد بالغير ما يكون تعقله بالقياس إلى الأول. الإضافة التي هي أحد أجناس الأعراض هي النسبة المتكررة أي النسبة التي لا تعقل إلا بالقياس إلى نسبة أخرى معقولة بالقياس إلى الأول، وتسمى هذه مضافاً حقيقياً، والمجموع المركب منها ومن معروضها مضافاً مشهورياً، وما وقع في المواقف أن نفس المعروض أيضاً مضافاً مشهورياً، فخلاف المشهور نعم قد يطلق عليه لفظ المضاف بمعنى له الإضافة على ما هو قانون اللغة، والحكماء تكلموا في هذا الباب أولاً في المضاف المشهوري لأن الاطلاع في بادىء النظر على المركبات أسهل، وفسروا المضاف على ما الحقيقي والمشهوري بما تكون ماهيته معقولة بالقياس إلى الغير، وأرادوا بالغير أمراً آخر تكون ماهيته معقولة بالقياس إلى الأول، وهذا معنى تكرر النسبة فيخرج سائر الأعراض النسبية، ومعنى تعقل ماهيته بالقياس إلى الغير أن تعقلها لا يتم إلا بتعقله حتى أن تعقل
The fifth chapter on the remaining relative accidents. He said: The fifth chapter: on the remaining relative accidents. Among them is al-idafah (relation), which is the repeated relation (al-nisbah al-mutakarrarah) that is not intelligible except by reference to a relation intelligible by reference to it. This is called 'real relative' (mudaf haqiqi). The composite of it and its substrate is called 'common relative' (mudaf mashhuri). Their statement includes it: 'that whose quiddity is not intelligible except by reference to another.' But what is meant by 'another' is that whose intellection is by reference to the first. Al-idafah, which is one of the genera of accidents, is the repeated relation, i.e., the relation that is not intelligible except by reference to another relation intelligible by reference to the first. This is called real relative, and the total composite of it and its substrate is called common relative. What occurs in al-Mawaqif is that the substrate itself is also called common relative, contrary to the well-known. Indeed, the term 'relative' may be applied to it in the sense of having relation, according to the rule of language. The philosophers (al-hukama') spoke in this chapter first about the common relative because in the initial view, composites are easier to understand. They interpreted the relative, both real and common, as that whose quiddity is intelligible by reference to another. They meant by 'another' something else whose quiddity is intelligible by reference to the first. This is the meaning of the repetition of the relation, so that other relative accidents are excluded. The meaning of 'its quiddity is intelligible by reference to another' is that its intellection is not completed except by intellection of it, such that intellection...
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of al-idafah (relation) as one of the remaining relative accidents and distinguishes between two types: real relative (mudaf haqiqi) and common relative (mudaf mashhuri). The author begins by stating the chapter topic: the remaining relative accidents. He then defines al-idafah as a 'repeated relation' (nisbah mutakarrarah) – a relation that cannot be conceived except by reference to another relation that is itself conceived by reference to it. This definition immediately gives the core idea: the relational nature of these accidents. He then specifies that the relation itself, when considered as the quality of being relative, is called 'real relative', while the composite of this relation and its substrate (the thing that bears the relation) is called 'common relative'. The passage quotes the standard kalam maxim: 'that whose quiddity is not intelligible except by reference to another' and clarifies that 'another' here means something whose conception is itself dependent on the first. The author then elaborates: al-idafah (relation) is a genus of accident, and it is this repeated relation that is the real relative; the whole composite (relation + substrate) is the common relative. He notes a divergence in al-Mawaqif, where the substrate itself is also called common relative, which is contrary to the common usage. However, in language, the term 'relative' can be applied to the substrate in the sense that it possesses a relation. The passage then discusses the approach of the philosophers (al-hukama'). They treat the common relative first because composites are easier to grasp initially. They define the relative (both real and common) as that whose quiddity is understood by reference to another. By 'another' they mean something whose quiddity is understood by reference to the first. This is the meaning of 'repeated relation' – it excludes other relative accidents. Finally, he explains that 'intelligible by reference to another' means that the intellection of the relative is not completed without intellection of its correlate, to the extent that the intellection of the relative includes the correlate.

Key Terms
الإضافة
النسبة المتكررة
مضاف حقيقي
مضاف مشهوري
الأعراض النسبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'relative accident' refers only to the relation itself, without including the substrate, or that the substrate alone is called relative.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the relation itself is the 'real relative', while the composite of relation and substrate is the 'common relative'. The substrate is only called relative in a linguistic sense, not as a technical term in kalam.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a 'relative accident' (al-idafah)?
A) It is a simple substance that requires no reference to anything else.
B) It is a repeated relation that cannot be conceived except by reference to another relation.
C) It is a composite of two independent substances.
D) It is a quality that inheres in all substances equally.

Key Takeaway
Al-idafah is a repeated relation that requires another relation for its conception; it has two forms: the relation itself (real relative) and the composite of relation and substrate (common relative).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage defines al-idafah as a relation that cannot be known without another relation. In your own life, do you try to define your identity independently of Allah, as if you were a self-sufficient substance? How might that be a form of misidentifying yourself as a 'real' rather than a 'relative' being? Today, commit to a specific time to reflect on how every aspect of your existence is only intelligible in relation to Him.
?
The philosophers begin with the 'common relative' because composites are easier. Are you perhaps satisfied with only the composite—your actions and outward worship—without contemplating the pure relation (the real relative) of your dependence on Allah? What would it take for you to focus more on that relationship itself rather than just its outward expressions? Choose one concrete way to prioritize the inner connection over the outer form.
?

Are you living as if your 'self' were absolute, not relative? Resolve to say 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' (there is no power except through Allah) at least ten times today as a reminder of your relative nature.

Definition of Idafa and Its Two Types
Taftazani defines idafa and classifies it into real and well-known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of idafa as a repeated relative notion and how it is divided into the real relative (mudaf haqiqi) and the well-known relative (mudaf mashhuri).

Core Idea
Idafa is a reciprocal relation: each term is understood only through the other.

This passage from Taftazani's commentary introduces one of the fundamental concepts in Islamic logic and metaphysics: al-idafa (relation). Before we can analyze how relations work in arguments about divine attributes or causality, we must precisely understand what a relation is. Taftazani gives a technical definition and splits it into two categories, real and well-known, clarifying a key condition of reciprocity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Taftazani uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the definition of idafa
Find the definition: 'al-nisba al-mutakarrara' (the repeated relative notion). Understand that it is a relation that can only be understood by reference to another relation that itself refers back to the first.
This reciprocity is the core of idafa.
3
Distinguish the two types
Identify the 'real relative' (mudaf haqiqi) as the relation itself, and the 'well-known relative' (mudaf mashhuri) as the composite of the relation and its subject. Notice also the alternative view from al-Mawaqif.
The well-known type is often the focus of philosophers because composites are easier to grasp initially.
4
Understand the exclusion of non-reciprocal relations
Note that not every relation qualifies as idafa. The passage excludes cases where intelligibility is one-way, such as evident necessary concomitants. Idafa requires mutual dependence.
For example, fire is accompanied by heat, but heat does not require fire in the same way; this is not a true idafa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في باقي الأعراض النسبية قال : الفصل الخامس: في باقي الأعراض لأن النسبية، منها الإضافة وهي النسبة المتكررة التي لا تعقل إلا بالقياس إلى نسبة معقولة بالقياس إليها، وهذه مضافاً حقيقياً. والمركب فيها ومن معروضها مضافاً مشهورياً، ويشملها قولهم ما لا يعقل ماهيته إلا بقياس إلى الغير . إلا أن المراد بالغير ما يكون تعقله بالقياس إلا الأول. من [؟] يسمى أنه شي الإضافة التي هي أحد أجناس الأعراض هي النسبة المتكررة أي النسبة التي لا تعقل إلا بالقياس إلى نسبة أخرى معقولة بالقياس إلى الأول، وتسمى هذه مضافاً حقيقياً، والمجموع المركب منها ، ومن معروضها مضافاً مشهورياً، وما وقع في المواقف أن نفس المعروض أيضاً مضافاً مشهورياً، فخلاف المشهور نعم قد يطلق عليه لفظ المضاف بمعنى له الإضافة على ما هو قانون اللغة، والحكماء تكلموا في هذا الباب أولاً في المضاف المشهوري لأن الاطلاع في بادىء النظر على المركبات أسهل، وفسروا المضاف على ما الحقيقي والمشهوري بما تكون ماهيته معقولة بالقياس إلى الغير، وأرادوا بالغير أمراً آخر تكون ماهيته معقولة بالقياس إلى الأول، وهذا معنى تكرر النسبة فيخرج سائر الأعراض النسبية، ومعنى تعقل ماهيته بالقياس إلى الغير أن تعقلها لا يتم إلا بتعقله حتى أن تعقل المضافين معاً لا تقدم لأحدهما على الآخر، فيخرج ما كان تعقله مستلزماً ومستعقباً لتعقل شيء آخر كالملزومات البينة اللوازم، على أن هذا إنما يـتـوهـم وروده إذا كـان تـعـقـل للوازم أيضاً مستلزماً لتعقل الملزومات، وما ذكر في المواقف من أنه ليس معنى قولهم تعقل ماهيته بالقياس إلى الغير أنه يلزم من تعقله تعقل الغير فإن اللوازم البينة كذلك محمول على حذف المضاف. أي ملزومات اللوازم، أو على أن ذلك إشارة إلى الغير بمعنى أن اللوازم البينة من قبيل الغير الذي يلزم من تعقل الملزومات تعقله وإن لم يكن الملزوم مضافاً .
The fifth chapter on the remaining relative accidents. He said: The fifth chapter: on the remaining accidents because the relative ones, among them is al-idafa (relation), which is the repeated relative notion (al-nisba al-mutakarrara) that is not intelligible except by relation to a relative notion intelligible by relation to it, and this is called a real relative (mudaf haqiqi). And the compound consisting of it and its subject is called a well-known relative (mudaf mashhuri). And their saying 'that whose quiddity is not intelligible except by relation to another' includes them, except that what is meant by 'another' is that whose intelligibility is by relation to the first. From [؟] is called that thing: al-idafa, which is one of the genera of accidents, is the repeated relative notion, i.e., the relative notion that is not intelligible except by relation to another relative notion intelligible by relation to the first, and this is called a real relative (mudaf haqiqi), and the totality composed of it and its subject is called a well-known relative (mudaf mashhuri). And what occurs in al-Mawaqif is that the subject itself is also called a well-known relative, contrary to the well-known view. Rather, the term 'relative' may be applied to it in the sense of 'having a relation' according to the rule of language. And the philosophers spoke in this chapter first about the well-known relative because gaining knowledge of composites is easier at first glance. And they interpreted the relative, both the real and the well-known, as that whose quiddity is intelligible by relation to another, and they meant by 'another' a different thing whose quiddity is intelligible by relation to the first. This is the meaning of the repetition of the relative notion, and it excludes the remaining relative accidents. And the meaning of 'its quiddity is intelligible by relation to another' is that its intelligibility is not completed except by its intelligibility, such that the intelligibility of the two relatives together has no priority of one over the other. This excludes that whose intelligibility entails and is entailed by the intelligibility of something else, like evident necessary concomitants. But this is only imagined if the intelligibility of the concomitants also entails the intelligibility of the things they accompany. And what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif, that the meaning of their saying 'its quiddity is intelligible by relation to another' is not that its intelligibility necessitates the intelligibility of the other, for evident necessary concomitants are like that, is interpreted as ellipsis of the relative, i.e., the things accompanied by the concomitants, or as indicating that the other means that the evident necessary concomitants are of the kind of the other whose intelligibility is necessitated by the intelligibility of the things accompanied even if the thing accompanied is not a relative.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage defines al-idafa (relation) as one of the relative accidents and distinguishes between two types: real and well-known. The author begins by stating that idafa is a 'repeated relative notion' (al-nisba al-mutakarrara) that can only be understood by reference to another relative notion that itself is understood by reference to the first. This first type is called 'real relative' (mudaf haqiqi). When the real relative is combined with its subject, the composite is called 'well-known relative' (mudaf mashhuri). The author then explains that the well-known definition 'that whose quiddity is not intelligible except by relation to another' covers both types, but the phrase 'another' here means something whose quiddity is understood by relation to the first, not just any other. He clarifies that the philosophers (al-hukama') focus first on the well-known relative because composites are easier to grasp initially. They define both real and well-known relative as that whose quiddity is intelligible by relation to another, where 'another' is something whose quiddity is also intelligible by relation to the first—this is the meaning of 'repetition of the relative notion' (takarrur al-nisba). This condition excludes other relative accidents where the reciprocity is absent. The author further explains that the intelligibility of the relative must be simultaneous with the intelligibility of the other relative, with no temporal priority. This excludes cases where the relation is one-way, like evident necessary concomitants (al-lazimat al-bayyina) that are necessarily understood when their substrate is understood. He addresses an objection from al-Mawaqif: the phrase 'its quiddity is intelligible by relation to another' does not mean that understanding it necessarily entails understanding the other (as in evident concomitants), but rather that the relationship is reciprocal. If there is entanglement, it is resolved by assuming ellipsis or by reinterpreting 'another' as the kind of other that is necessitated without the substrate being a relative.

Key Terms
إضافة
مضاف حقيقي
مضاف مشهوري
النسبة المتكررة
تعقل بالقياس إلى الغير
الملزومات البينة اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any term linked to another is an example of idafa.
Correction: Idafa requires a specific bi-directional interdependence where each term is understood only through the other. One-way dependencies, like fire and heat, are not idafa.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a 'real relative' (mudaf haqiqi) from other relative accidents?
A) It requires a linguistic particle to express the relation.
B) Its quiddity is intelligible only by relation to another relative notion that itself is intelligible by relation to the first.
C) It exists only in the mind, not externally.
D) It is a substance, not an accident.

Key Takeaway
Idafa is a reciprocal relation where the intelligibility of each term depends on the other; it excludes one-way dependencies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that real idafa is a relation that cannot be understood except by reference to another relation that refers back to it. Think of a relationship in your life that you consider defining for your identity—like parent-child, teacher-student, or friend. Is your understanding of that relationship mutually dependent, or do you see yourself as independent and the other as dependent? How would acknowledging mutual dependence change how you act in that relationship?
?
The philosophers prioritize studying the well-known relative because composites are easier to grasp. In your spiritual journey, do you tend to focus on 'composite' concepts (like a good deed) without understanding the underlying 'relation' (like your intention towards Allah)? What might you be missing by not examining the 'real' relation behind your actions?

The Necessity of Reciprocity and Its Types
Taftazani defines reciprocity and classifies it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of reciprocity (in'ikas), the method to identify the relatum, and the classification of reciprocity with and without a particle.

Core Idea
Reciprocity means each relative is related to the other precisely as a relative.

Taftazani explains that the necessity of reciprocity is essentially the repetition of the relative notion. He provides a practical method to determine which term is the relatum and then classifies reciprocity into types based on whether a linguistic particle is needed and whether the particle is the same or different on both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and condition
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the opening sentence and the explanation of wujub al-in'ikas. Note that the relation must be considered from the aspect of relativity (al-haythiyya).
The example of 'father of the son' vs. 'father of a human' illustrates the condition.
2
Learn the method to identify the relatum
Study the method: examine the descriptions of both sides. The description whose inclusion preserves the relation and whose removal destroys it is the relatum.
The son/father example demonstrates how negating sonhood breaks the relation.
3
Recognize the classification
Identify the three types: (1) no particle needed (e.g., great/small), (2) particle needed with equality (e.g., servant of master), (3) particle needed with difference (e.g., knower of known).
The particle is the preposition expressing relation, like 'li' or 'bi'.
4
Apply to examples
Confirm your understanding by applying the method to the examples given: 'great/small' requires no particle; 'servant of master' uses the same 'li' on both sides; 'knower of known' uses 'bi' and 'li'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (هذا) معنى وجوب الانعكاس والانعكاس قد لا يفتقر إلى اعتبار حرف كالعظيم والصغير وقد يفتقر إما على التساوي مثل عبد للمولى، ومولى للعبد أو لا مثل عالم بمعلوم ومعلوم لعالم . أي الذي ذكرنا من معنى تكرر النسبة هو معنى وجوب الانعكاس أي يحكم إضافة كل من المضافين إلى الآخر من حيث هو مضاف فكما يقال الأب أبو الابن يقال الابن ابن الأب وأما إذا لم تعتبر الحيثية لم يتحقق الانعكاس كما إذا قيل الأب أبو إنسان لم يكن الإنسان مضافاً إلى الأب فلا يقال إنسان أب، وطريق معرفة الانعكاس أن ينظر في أوصاف الطرفين فما كان بحيث إذا وضعته، ورفعت غيره بقيت الإضافة، وإذا رفعته ووضعت غيره لم تبق الإضافة، فهو الذي إليه الإضافة مثلاً إذا اعتبرت من الابن البنوة مع نفي سائر الصفات بل الذاتيات كان الأب مضافاً إليه وإذا رفعت البنوة مع اعتبار البواقي لم تتحقق الإضافة ثم الانعكاس قد لا يفتقر إلى اعتبار حرف النسبة كالعظيم والصغير، وقد يفتقر إما على تساوي الحرف في الجانبين كقول العبد عبد للمولى . والمولى مولى للعبد أو على اختلافه كقولنا العالم عالم
He said: This is the meaning of the necessity of reciprocity (wujub al-in'ikas). And reciprocity may not require the consideration of a particle, like 'great' and 'small', and may require it, either with equality, like 'servant of the master' and 'master of the servant', or without equality, like 'knower of the known' and 'known of the knower'. That is, what we mentioned about the meaning of the repetition of the relative notion is the meaning of the necessity of reciprocity: that the relation of each of the two relatives to the other is judged inasmuch as it is a relative. So just as one says 'the father is father of the son', one says 'the son is son of the father'. But if the aspect is not considered, reciprocity does not occur, as when one says 'the father is father of a human', the human is not related to the father, so one does not say 'a human father'. And the way to know reciprocity is to look at the descriptions of the two sides. That which, when you posit it and remove the other, the relation remains, and when you remove it and posit the other, the relation does not remain, is that to which the relation is directed. For example, if you consider from the son sonhood while negating all other attributes, even the essentials, then the father is related to him, and if you remove sonhood while retaining the rest, the relation does not occur. Then reciprocity may not require the consideration of a particle of relation, like 'great' and 'small', and may require it, either with equality of the particle on both sides, like one says 'the servant is servant of the master' and 'the master is master of the servant', or with its difference, like our saying 'the knower is knower'
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of reciprocity (in'ikas) in relative notions. The author begins by stating that the meaning of 'necessity of reciprocity' (wujub al-in'ikas) is what was previously described as the repetition of the relative notion (takarrur al-nisba): that each relative is related to the other precisely insofar as it is a relative. For example, if we say 'father of the son', we can also say 'son of the father' because the relation is mutual. However, if the aspect (al-haythiyya) is not considered—i.e., if the relation is not taken as a relative-to-relative connection—then reciprocity fails: e.g., 'father of a human' does not imply 'human of a father' because 'human' is not a relative to 'father'. The author then provides a method for identifying which term is the relatum in a reciprocal relation. He says to examine the descriptions of the two sides: if positing a certain description while removing the other preserves the relation, but removing it and positing the other destroys it, then that description is the relatum (masdar al-idafa). For instance, if from 'son' we consider only sonhood (al-bunuwwa) while negating all other attributes, then 'father' is related to it (the sonhood); if we remove sonhood while keeping other attributes, the relation disappears. Next, the author classifies reciprocity into types based on the need for a linguistic particle (harf al-nisba). Some reciprocities do not require a particle, like 'al-azim wa al-saghir' (the great and the small) where the relation is inherent in the meanings. Others require a particle, and these are subdivided: (a) with equality of the particle on both sides, e.g., 'abdun li-l-mawla' and 'mawla li-l-abd' (servant of the master and master of the servant), both using the same particle 'li'; (b) with difference of the particle, e.g., 'alimun bi-ma'lum' and 'ma'lumun li-alim' (knower of the known and known of the knower) using 'bi' versus 'li'. The passage concludes with an incomplete example but the categories are clear.

Key Terms
وجوب الانعكاس
الانعكاس
حرف النسبة
عبد للمولى
عالم بمعلوم
المضاف المشهوري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reciprocity always requires a linguistic particle.
Correction: Some relations like 'great/small' have inherent reciprocity without a particle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of reciprocity that requires a linguistic particle with equality on both sides?
A) Great and small.
B) Servant of the master and master of the servant.
C) Knower of the known and known of the knower.
D) Father of the son and son of the father.

Key Takeaway
Reciprocity is a mutual relation that may be expressed with or without a particle, and the relatum is identified by a method of testing descriptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your relationship with your parents. In what aspects do you view them as 'father' or 'mother' to you, and in what aspects do you view yourself as 'son' or 'daughter' to them? Are these aspects mutual? How does this lesson's emphasis on considering the aspect (haythiyya) challenge you to be more mindful of how you define your relationships?
?
The passage shows that reciprocity fails when the relative aspect is not considered. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes treat your servitude as a general 'human' attribute rather than a specific, reciprocal bond? How can you strengthen the aspect of 'abd (servant) in a way that acknowledges His lordship in every detail of your life?
?
Taftazani uses examples like 'knower of the known'. In your pursuit of knowledge, do you recognize that being a 'knower' implies a relation to a 'known' thing? How does this awareness affect the way you seek knowledge and the responsibility you feel toward what you learn?

True Relatives Condition: No Precedence
Condition for true relatives: no temporal precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition that true relatives involve simultaneous conception without temporal precedence, and why necessary entailments are excluded.

Core Idea
True relatives require simultaneous conception, not necessary entailment.

This passage from al-Taftazani clarifies a key condition for something to be a true relative (muḍāf) in Islamic theology. He explains that the two terms must be conceived together without one preceding the other. This rule excludes cases where understanding one concept necessarily brings another to mind, such as necessary concomitants. Let's read his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read its literal English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice the technical terms like 'muḍāf' and 'lawāzim'.
2
Identify the condition and the exclusion
Find the main condition (no temporal precedence) and the excluded cases (things whose understanding entails understanding another).
The exclusion includes 'necessary entailments of clear concomitants'.
3
Understand the clarification from al-Mawaqif
Al-Taftazani addresses a subtle point: the objection only arises if understanding the concomitant also entails the entailment. He explains how to interpret the statement from al-Mawaqif.
The clarification involves deletion of the annexed noun or reinterpreting 'other'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المضافين معاً لا تقدم لأحدهما على الآخر، فيخرج ما كان تعقله مستلزماً ومستعقباً لتعقل شيء آخر كالملزومات البينة اللوازم، على أن هذا إنما يتوهم وروده إذا كان تعقل اللوازم أيضاً مستلزماً لتعقل الملزومات، وما ذكر في المواقف من أنه ليس معنى قولهم تعقل ماهيته بالقياس إلى الغير أنه يلزم من تعقله تعقل الغير فإن اللوازم البينة كذلك محمول على حذف المضاف. أي ملزومات اللوازم، أو على أن ذلك إشارة إلى الغير بمعنى أن اللوازم البينة من قبيل الغير الذي يلزم من تعقل الملزومات تعقله وإن لم يكن الملزوم مضافاً.
The two relatives together have no temporal precedence of one over the other, so what is excluded is that whose understanding entails and is entailed by understanding something else, like necessary entailments of clear concomitants. However, this is only thought to occur if understanding the concomitants also entails understanding the entailments. And what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif—that the meaning of their saying 'understanding its essence in relation to another' is not that understanding it necessitates understanding that other, for clear concomitants are like that—is interpreted as deletion of the annexed noun, i.e., the entailments of the concomitants, or as that being an indication of the other in the sense that clear concomitants are of the type of other that necessitates understanding the entailments upon understanding them, even if the entailed is not a relative.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage defines a condition for something to be truly a relative (muḍāf): the two terms must be considered together without one preceding the other in conception. It then excludes cases where understanding one concept necessarily entails understanding another, using the example of self-evident concomitants (al-malzumāt al-bayyina al-lawāzim). The author addresses a potential objection from al-Mawaqif, clarifying that such necessary entailment does not make the concepts true relatives. Instead, the apparent relationship is explained either as a deletion of the annexed noun (meaning the entailments of the concomitants, not the concepts themselves) or as a reference to a different kind of relation, where the entailment is not based on genuine attribution.

Key Terms
المضاف
الانعكاس
الملزومات البينة اللوازم
تعقل
حذف المضاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that necessary concomitants (like fire and heat) are true relatives because they are always thought of together.
Correction: The passage clarifies that true relatives require no temporal precedence, whereas in necessary concomitants, one concept (like fire) can be understood without the other (heat) being necessarily relative to it; heat's understanding is entailed, but fire is not a relative to heat.

Checkpoint

A) Things that are considered together simultaneously
B) Things whose understanding necessarily entails understanding something else
C) Things that have a shared cause
D) Things that are opposites

Key Takeaway
True relatives must be conceived together without temporal precedence, excluding cases where understanding one necessarily entails the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might the condition of no precedence challenge you to conceive of His attributes as simultaneous realities without hierarchy?

Consider a relationship in your life (e.g., parent-child, teacher-student, creator-creation). Have you ever treated it as a mere cause-and-effect, forgetting the simultaneous mutual recognition it demands? Will you commit today to reflect on that relationship with the precision this lesson teaches?
?
When you learn a new theological concept, do you rush to connect it to others without checking if the connection is one of necessary entailment or true relation? What is one assumption about a religious belief that you now realize might be a 'necessary concomitant' rather than a genuine relative, and how will you correct it?

Meaning and Test of Mutual Relation (al-Inʿikās)
Al-Taftazani defines mutual relation and provides a test for it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what mutual relation (al-inʿikās) requires and how to test whether a relation is mutual.

Core Idea
Mutual relation requires considering the precise aspect that relates two terms.

Mutual relation is a key concept in kalam for understanding how attributes relate to each other. This passage from al-Taftazani defines it and gives a practical test to determine when two terms are mutually related. Let's explore the definition and the test.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Understand the condition of ḥaythiyya (the aspect)
Notice that mutual relation only holds when each term is considered in its capacity as a relative. The example of 'father of the son' vs 'father of a human' illustrates this.
The aspect (al-ḥaythiyya) is crucial – without it, the relation is not mutual.
3
Apply the test for mutual relation
Learn the test: examine the attributes of both terms. If positing one attribute and removing the other preserves the relation, that attribute is the one on which the relation depends. Use the sonship example to see how it works.
Try to think of other examples where the test would pass or fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (هذا) معنى وجوب الانعكاس والانعكاس قد لا يفتقر إلى اعتبار حرف كالعظيم والصغير وقد يفتقر إما على التساوي مثل عبد للمولى، ومولى للعبد أو لا مثل عالم بمعلوم ومعلوم لعالم . أي الذي ذكرنا من معنى تكرر النسبة هو معنى وجوب الانعكاس أي يحكم إضافة كل من المضافين إلى الآخر من حيث هو مضاف كما يقال الأب أبو الابن يقال الابن ابن الأب وأما إذا لم تعتبر الحيثية لم يتحقق الانعكاس كما إذا قيل الأب أبو إنسان لم يكن الإنسان مضافاً إلى الأب فلا يقال إنسان أب، وطريق معرفة الانعكاس أن ينظر في أوصاف الطرفين فما كان بحيث إذا وضعته، ورفعت غيره بقيت الإضافة، وإذا رفعته ووضعت غيره لم تبق الإضافة، فهو الذي إليه الإضافة مثلاً إذا اعتبرت من الابن البنوة مع نفي سائر الصفات بل الذاتيات كان الأب مضافاً إليه وإذا رفعت البنوة مع اعتبار البواقي لم تتحقق الإضافة
He said: 'This is the meaning of compulsory mutual relation, and mutual relation may not need a particle, like great and small, or may need it, either equally like servant of the master and master of the servant, or unequally like knower of the known and known of the knower.' That is, what we mentioned about the meaning of the repetition of the relation is the meaning of compulsory mutual relation. That is, it is judged that each of the two relatives is added to the other in so far as it is relative. Just as it is said 'the father is the father of the son,' it is said 'the son is the son of the father.' But if the manner is not considered, mutual relation does not obtain, as when it is said 'the father is the father of a human,' the human is not relative to the father, so it is not said 'a human father.' The way to know mutual relation is to look at the attributes of the two terms: if, when you posit it and remove the other, the relation remains, and when you remove it and posit the other, the relation does not remain, then it is that to which the relation belongs. For example, if from the son you consider sonship while negating all other attributes, even essential ones, the father is relative to it, and if you remove sonship while considering the others, the relation does not obtain.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage defines mutual relation (al-inʿikās). The author quotes the matn, which states that mutual relation may or may not require a particle (like a preposition), with examples. Then the commentary explains that the meaning of mutual relation is that each of the two relatives is predicated on the other precisely insofar as it is relative. For example, 'father of the son' implies 'son of the father' only when the relation is considered from the standpoint of fatherhood and sonship respectively. If the manner is not considered, as in 'father of a human,' the relation is not mutual because 'human' is not inherently relative. The author then provides a test: examine the attributes of the two terms; if substituting one attribute while removing the other preserves the relation, then that attribute is the one on which the relation depends. He illustrates with the example of sonship: if you consider only sonship in the son, the father is relative to that; if you remove sonship, the relation is lost.

Key Terms
الانعكاس
وجوب الانعكاس
المضاف
الحيثية
طريق معرفة الانعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking mutual relation holds automatically for any pair of terms, even when the relation is not considered from the proper aspect.
Correction: Mutual relation requires that each term be considered precisely in its capacity as a relative. Without this aspect, the relation is not mutual, as shown by the example 'father of a human'.

Checkpoint

A) If positing that attribute and removing the other preserves the relation
B) If the attribute is essential to both terms
C) If the attribute is a necessary concomitant
D) If the relation remains when both attributes are removed

Key Takeaway
Mutual relation between two terms holds only when each is considered precisely insofar as it is related to the other, and the test is to see which attribute preserves the relation when the other is removed.

Need for a Particle in Mutual Relation
Al-Taftazani classifies mutual relation cases regarding particle necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which mutual relation requires a relational particle in Arabic, and the critical stance on the claim that coined terms eliminate this need.

Core Idea
Not all relations need a particle—some terms are inherently relative.

Al-Taftazani continues his analysis of mutual relation (الانعكاس) by examining a practical question: does every relational expression require a particle like 'of'? He presents a classification of cases where the particle is needed or not, and then critiques a common opinion. This passage sharpens your understanding of how Arabic expresses relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the translation. Notice the examples and the structure.
Pay attention to the terms 'great and small' vs. 'servant and master'.
2
Identify the two main categories
Find where the passage distinguishes cases that do not need a particle from those that do. Note the subcategories within the latter.
The second category splits into equality vs. difference of particle.
3
Analyse the quoted opinion
Read the statement 'they said...' and note the author's response 'this is open to scrutiny'. Consider why the author might question it.
4
Reflect on the examples
Think about each example: why might 'great and small' not need a particle, while 'servant of the master' does?
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الانعكاس قد لا يفتقر إلى اعتبار حرف النسبة كالعظيم والصغير، وقد يفتقر إما على تساوي الحرف في الجانبين كقول العبد عبد للمولى والمولى مولى للعبد أو على اختلافه كقولنا العالم عالم بالعلوم والمعلوم معلوم للعالم، قالوا: وعدم الافتقار إنما هو حيث يكون للمضاف بما هو مضاف لفظ موضوع وفيه نظر.
Then, mutual relation may not need the consideration of a particle of relation, like great and small, or may need it, either with equality of the particle on both sides, like the statement 'the servant is a servant of the master' and 'the master is a master of the servant,' or with its difference, like our statement 'the knower is a knower of the sciences' and 'the known is known to the knower.' They said: 'And the lack of need is only when there is a term coined for the relative as such,' and this is open to scrutiny.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of mutual relation, now focusing on whether a relational particle (like a preposition) is required. The author states that mutual relation sometimes does not need a particle, as in the case of 'great' and 'small,' where each term inherently implies the relation. Other times it does need a particle, either with the same particle on both sides (e.g., 'servant of the master' and 'master of the servant' both use 'of') or with different particles (e.g., 'knower of the sciences' vs. 'known to the knower'). The author then quotes an opinion: 'The lack of need is only when there is a coined term for the relative as such,' but notes that this claim is subject to scrutiny (fīhi naẓar). This indicates a critical stance, suggesting that even in such cases, a particle may still be implied.

Key Terms
حرف النسبة
الانعكاس
المضاف
تساوي الحرف
اختلاف الحرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All mutual relations in Arabic require a relational particle.
Correction: Some terms (like 'great' and 'small') are inherently relative and do not need a particle, though the author questions the scope of this exemption.

Checkpoint
What is the condition under which mutual relation does not need a particle, according to the quoted opinion?
A) When the two terms are essential opposites
B) When there is a term coined specifically for the relative as such
C) When the relation is one of causation
D) When the particle is understood from context

Key Takeaway
Mutual relation in Arabic may or may not require a relational particle, and the claim that coined terms eliminate this need is not universally accepted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often use words like 'big' or 'small' without thinking about their hidden standard. Is there an area of your life where you judge yourself or others as 'big' or 'small' relative to an unexamined measure? What would it mean to consciously expose that implicit particle?
?
The author casts doubt on the idea that a coined term can fully stand alone without a relational particle. In your own faith, are there beliefs you treat as 'coined truths'—seemingly absolute statements that actually depend on hidden relationships or contexts? Are you willing to scrutinize them as Taftazani does?
?
When you say 'I am a servant of Allah,' do you realize that the particle 'of' makes you equally dependent on Allah as on the definition of servanthood? How does this awareness change the way you live that relationship today?
