The Two Relations and Their Expression
Al-Taftazani explains the two relations and their expression and occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of relations in kalam and how their expression and occurrence can vary.

Core Idea
Relations can be symmetric or asymmetric, and their expression may require particles or not.

In this passage, al-Taftazani explores the nature of relations (nisbatān). He shows that relations can be identical in both directions (like brotherhood) or different (like fatherhood). He also explains that some relations are expressed directly by words, while others need a connective; similarly, some relations depend on attributes in both terms, one term, or neither.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the overall classification of relations.
2
Identify the three aspects
Notice how al-Taftazani discusses the relations from three angles: harmony/opposition, expression (with/without particle), and occurrence (attribute needed in both, one, or none).
3
Apply the concepts
Think of real-world examples for each type to solidify understanding, e.g., brotherhood for symmetric, father/son for asymmetric, right/left for no attribute required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (والنسبتان) قد تتوافقان كالأخوة وقد تتخالفان كالأبوة والبنوة، والتعبير عنهما قد يكون باسم مثل الأب والابن وقد يفتقر إلى رابطة إحداهما مثل الرأس وذو الرأس، وعروضها قد يفتقر إلى صفة في الطرفين كالعاشق والمعشوق أو في أحدهما كالعالم والمعلوم أولا كاليمين واليسار. يعنى أن النسبة التي هي المضاف الحقيقي قد تكون متوافية في الجانبين كالإخوة، وقد تكون متخالفة كالأبوة والبنوة، والاختلاف قد يكون محدوداً كما الضعف والنصف وقد لا يكون كما في الزائد والناقص، والتعبير عن المضافين قد لا يفتقر إلى حرف نسبة، وذلك حيث يكون لكل منهما لفظ موضوع يدل بالتضمن على الإضافة مثل الأب والابن والعبد والمولى، وما أشبه ذلك وقد يفتقر وذلك حيث تنتفي تلك الدلالة في المضاف إليه مثل جناح الطير، فيعبر عنه بذي الجناح أو في المضاف كعلم العالم فيعبر عنه بما للعالم وعروض الإضافة قد يفتقر إلى حصول صفة في كل من الطرفين كالعاشقية إلى الإدراك والمعشوقية إلى الجمال أو في أحدهما كالعالمية إلى العلم بخلاف المعلومية، وقد لا يفتقر أصلاً كما في المتيامن والمتياسر، فإن الاتصاف بذلك لا يكون باعتبار صفة حقيقية في شيء منهما.
He said: 'The two relations: they may harmonize, like brotherhood, or oppose, like fatherhood and sonship; the expression of them may be by a name, like father and son, or may need a connective for one of them, like head and possessor of a head; their occurrence may need an attribute in both terms, like lover and beloved, or in one of them, like knower and known, or not at all, like right and left.' That is, the relation which is the true relative may be equivalent on both sides, like brotherhood, or opposed, like fatherhood and sonship. The opposition may be definite, like double and half, or not, like more and less. The expression of the two relatives may not need a particle of relation, that being when each has a term coined that implies the relation by inclusion, like father, son, servant, master and the like; or it may need it, that being when such implication is absent in the second relative, like the wing of a bird, which is expressed as 'possessor of the wing,' or in the first relative, like the knowledge of the knower, which is expressed as 'that which belongs to the knower.' The occurrence of the relation may need the existence of an attribute in both terms, like lover's love for perception and beloved's being loved for beauty, or in one of them, like the knower's knowledge as opposed to the known's being known, or it may not need anything at all, like right and left, since being described as such is not based on a real attribute in either of them.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage explains the two relations (al-nisbatān). The matn states that the two relations can be harmonious (e.g., brotherhood, where the relation is symmetric) or opposed (e.g., fatherhood vs. sonship). The expression of the relatives may use a direct name (e.g., 'father' and 'son') or may require a connective for one of them (e.g., 'head' vs. 'possessor of a head'). Occurrence of the relation may depend on an attribute in both terms (e.g., lover and beloved requiring perception and beauty), in one term (e.g., knower needing knowledge, but known does not), or not at all (e.g., right and left, which are purely relational without inherent attributes). The commentary expands on each point: opposition can be definite (double/half) or indefinite (more/less); expression without a particle requires each term to have a word implying the relation; with particle when one term lacks such implication; and the basis for the occurrence of the relation can be in both, one, or neither term.

Key Terms
النسبتان
المضاف الحقيقي
الاختلاف المحدود
التعبير بالمسمى
عروض الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All relations require a real attribute in both terms.
Correction: The passage shows that some relations like right and left do not depend on a real attribute in either term; they are purely conventional.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example where the occurrence of the relation does not require an attribute in either term?
A) Lover and beloved
B) Knower and known
C) Right and left
D) Father and son

Key Takeaway
Relations can be symmetric or asymmetric, expressed directly or with particles, and their basis can be in attributes or purely conventional.

The passage says some relations like 'right' and 'left' have no real attribute. Ibn Sina's Classification of Relatives
Ibn Sina classifies relatives into four categories with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's four-category classification of relatives (al-muḍāfāt) and the rationale behind each category.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's classification makes relational concepts rationally tractable.

Ibn Sina offers a structured taxonomy of relationships (al-muḍāfāt) that anchors theological and philosophical discourse on causation and comparison. His four categories—equality, excess, action-passion, and imitation—provide a framework for analyzing relational concepts. This passage presents that classification with initial examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall structure of the classification.
Notice the phrase 'takādu takūnu munḥaṣiratan' — 'almost confined'.
2
Identify the four main categories
From the passage, list the four categories: equality, excess, action-passion, and imitation. Note that the passage elaborates on excess and action-passion, while equality and imitation are only named.
3
Examine the subcategories of excess
Ibn Sina divides excess into two subcategories: quantitative (like greater/lesser) and potential (like subduer/dominant). Find these in the text and reflect on the examples.
The category of action-passion is exemplified by father and son.
4
Understand the incomplete enumeration
The phrase 'almost confined' suggests the classification is comprehensive but not absolutely exhaustive. Consider why Ibn Sina might have left room for exceptions.
This guards against assuming the list is exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا تكاد تكون المضافات منحصرة في أقسام المعادلة، والتي بالزيادة والتي بالفعل والانفعال ومصدرهما من القوة والتي بالمحاكاة. فما التي بالزيادة، فإما من الكم كما يعلم، وأما في القوة مثل القاهر والغالب، والمانع وغير ذلك والتي بالفعل والانفعال كالأب والابن والقاطع
Ibn Sina said: 'Relatives (al-muḍāfāt) are almost confined to categories of equality, those of excess, those of action and passion and their sources of potentiality, and those of imitation. As for those of excess, they are either in terms of quantity, as is known, or in potentiality, like the subduer, the dominant, the preventer, and so on. And those of action and passion are like father and son, and the cutter...'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's classification of relatives (al-muḍāfāt) into four main categories: (1) equality (al-muʿādala), (2) excess (bi-l-ziyāda), (3) action and passion (bi-l-fiʿl wa-l-infiʿāl) and their sources of potentiality, and (4) imitation (al-muḥākāh). The author then elaborates on the category of excess: it can be in terms of quantity (e.g., greater/lesser) or in terms of potentiality (e.g., subduer/dominant). The category of action and passion is exemplified by father (active) and son (passive). The text is incomplete due to page break, but the remaining categories are likely covered in subsequent pages.

Key Terms
المضافات
أقسام المعادلة
الزيادة
الفعل والانفعال
المحاكاة
القوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's classification covers all possible types of relatives.
Correction: Ibn Sina states the categories are 'almost confined' (takādu takūnu munḥaṣiratan), indicating near-completeness but not absolute exhaustion. There may be rare or exceptional relatives outside these four categories.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's classification, which category includes relatives like 'father' and 'son'?
A) Equality (al-muʿādala)
B) Excess (bi-l-ziyāda)
C) Action and passion (bi-l-fiʿl wa-l-infiʿāl)
D) Imitation (al-muḥākāh)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's classification organizes relatives into four categories: equality, excess, action-passion, and imitation, with excess further divided into quantitative and potential types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily interactions, which relationships fit Ibn Sina's category of 'excess in potentiality' (like subduer/dominated)? Are you ever the one exercising power over others? How does this affect your responsibility before Allah, and what can you do today to ensure you use that power justly?
?
Ibn Sina's classification includes 'action and passion' as in father/son. Reflect on your relationship with your parents. Do you tend to view this relationship as one of action (you as passive) or passion (you as active)? How can you better fulfill your duties toward them, especially if you have been neglecting this fundamental bond?
?
The text says relatives are 'almost confined' to these categories—implying some may not fit. Consider the relationship between the Creator and creation: can it be placed into any of Ibn Sina's categories? Why does understanding that Allah's attributes (like al-Khāliq) are not relatives in the same sense deepen your tawḥīd?

Agreement and Opposition in Relations
Al-Taftazani defines the two types of relations and their subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two fundamental types of relations: those that are identical from both sides (agreement) and those that are different (opposition), including the subcategory of defined and non-defined opposition.

Core Idea
Relations can be mutual like brotherhood or one-way like fatherhood.

Relations between entities are not all the same. Some are reciprocal, meaning the relation is identical from both perspectives, while others are one-sided. Al-Taftazani begins by distinguishing these two types, which is essential for understanding how things are described in relation to each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Pay attention to the Arabic terms for agreement and opposition.
2
Identify the two types
Find where the passage presents agreement (توافق) and opposition (تخالف) between relations.
The examples brotherhood, fatherhood-sonship are key.
3
Notice the subcategory of opposition
The passage mentions that opposition can be defined (like double and half) or non-defined (like excess and deficiency). Reflect on what makes a relation 'defined'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (والنسبتان) قد تتوافقان كالأخوة وقد تتخالفان كالأبوة والبنوة ، والتعبير عنهما قد يكون باسم مثل الأب والابن وقد يفتقر إلى رابطة إحداهما مثل الرأس وذو الرأس ، وعروضها قد يفتقر إلى صفة في الطرفين كالعاشق والمعشوق أو في أحدهما كالعالم والمعلوم أولا كاليمين واليسار.
يعني أن النسبة التي هي المضاف الحقيقي قد تكون متوافية في الجانبين كالإخوة، وقد تكون متخالفة كالأبوة والبنوة، والاختلاف قد يكون محدوداً كما الضعف والنصف وقد لا يكون كما في الزائد والناقص، والتعبير عن المضافين قد لا يفتقر إلى حرف نسبة، وذلك حيث يكون لكل منهما لفظ موضوع يدل بالتضمن على الإضافة مثل الأب والابن والعبد والمولى، وما أشبه ذلك وقد يفتقر وذلك حيث تنتفي تلك الدلالة في المضاف إليه مثل جناح الطير، فيعبر عنه بذي الجناح أو في المضاف كعلم العالم فيعبر عنه بما للعالم وعروض الإضافة قد يفتقر إلى حصول صفة في كل من الطرفين كالعاشقية إلى الإدراك والمعشوقية إلى الجمال أو في أحدهما كالعالمية إلى العلم بخلاف المعلومية، وقد لا يفتقر أصلاً كما في المتيامن والمتياسر، فإن الاتصاف بذلك لا يكون باعتبار صفة حقيقية في شيء منهما.
He said: (And the two relations) may be in agreement like brotherhood and may be in opposition like fatherhood and sonship, and the expression of them may be by a name like 'father' and 'son', and may need a link for one of them like 'head' and 'possessor of a head', and their occurrence may need an attribute in both sides like 'lover' and 'beloved' or in one of them like 'knower' and 'known' or not at all like 'right' and 'left'.
Meaning that the relation, which is the true relative, may be equal in the two sides like brotherhood, and may be different like fatherhood and sonship, and the difference may be defined like double and half, and may not be like in excess and deficiency. And the expression of the two relatives may not need a letter of relation, and that is when each of them has a lexical term that connotes the addition by implication like 'father' and 'son', 'slave' and 'master', and so on; and it may need that, namely when that connotation is absent in the second relative like 'wing of a bird', so it is expressed by 'possessor of a wing', or in the first relative like 'knowledge of the knower', so it is expressed by 'that which belongs to the knower'. And the occurrence of addition may need the existence of an attribute in each of the two sides like lovingness to perception and belovedness to beauty, or in one of them like knowership to knowledge unlike being known, and may not need at all like in being right and being left, for the description thereby is not considered a real attribute in either of them.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of relations (al-nisbatayn) and how they are expressed. It begins with the author's original statement that the two relations may be in agreement (mutawāfiqatayn) like brotherhood where the relation is the same from both sides, or may be different (mukhtālifatayn) like fatherhood and sonship where the relation is not reciprocal. Then it explains expression: sometimes each relative has a name that directly implies the relation (like 'father'), sometimes a linking particle is needed (like 'head' and 'possessor of a head'). The commentary then expands: the true relative (al-muḍāf al-ḥaqīqī) is the relation itself. It can be equal or different; difference may be defined (like double and half) or not (like excess and deficiency). Expression may avoid a particle when each term has a word that connotes the addition (like 'father' implies a son). When that connotation is missing, a particle is needed (like 'wing of a bird' requires 'possessor of a wing'). The occurrence of the relation may require an attribute in both sides (e.g., lover needs perception, beloved needs beauty), or in one side (e.g., knower needs knowledge, but known does not need a real attribute), or no attribute at all (like right and left, which are purely relative).

Key Terms
النسبتان
التوافق
التخالف
المضاف الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All relations are reciprocal.
Correction: Some relations are one-sided, like fatherhood and sonship, where the relation from one side is not the same as from the other.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of relations in agreement (توافق)?
A) Brotherhood
B) Fatherhood
C) Double
D) Excess

Key Takeaway
Relations can be mutual (agreement) or one-sided (opposition), and opposition may be defined by a ratio or not.

Expressing Relations: Direct Names vs Linking Particles
Al-Taftazani explains the two ways relations are expressed: by independent names or with a linking particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how relations can be expressed either by a single word that implies the relation (like 'father') or by using a linking particle (like 'possessor of a head').

Core Idea
Some relations have their own names; others need a helper word.

Not every relative term comes ready-made. Some relations are so common that language has a single word for each side (like father and son). Others require a linking particle to connect one thing to another. Al-Taftazani explains this linguistic nuance and why it matters for precise theological and logical discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the contrast between having a direct name and needing a linking particle.
2
Identify the two cases
Look for where the passage says expression may be by a name (باسم) or may need a link (رابطة).
Examples: 'father' and 'son' vs 'head' and 'possessor of a head'.
3
Understand the commentary's expansion
The commentary adds that a linking particle is needed when the connotation of relation is absent in one of the terms, like 'wing of a bird' requiring 'possessor of a wing'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (والنسبتان) قد تتوافقان كالأخوة وقد تتخالفان كالأبوة والبنوة ، والتعبير عنهما قد يكون باسم مثل الأب والابن وقد يفتقر إلى رابطة إحداهما مثل الرأس وذو الرأس ، وعروضها قد يفتقر إلى صفة في الطرفين كالعاشق والمعشوق أو في أحدهما كالعالم والمعلوم أولا كاليمين واليسار.
يعني أن النسبة التي هي المضاف الحقيقي قد تكون متوافية في الجانبين كالإخوة، وقد تكون متخالفة كالأبوة والبنوة، والاختلاف قد يكون محدوداً كما الضعف والنصف وقد لا يكون كما في الزائد والناقص، والتعبير عن المضافين قد لا يفتقر إلى حرف نسبة، وذلك حيث يكون لكل منهما لفظ موضوع يدل بالتضمن على الإضافة مثل الأب والابن والعبد والمولى، وما أشبه ذلك وقد يفتقر وذلك حيث تنتفي تلك الدلالة في المضاف إليه مثل جناح الطير، فيعبر عنه بذي الجناح أو في المضاف كعلم العالم فيعبر عنه بما للعالم وعروض الإضافة قد يفتقر إلى حصول صفة في كل من الطرفين كالعاشقية إلى الإدراك والمعشوقية إلى الجمال أو في أحدهما كالعالمية إلى العلم بخلاف المعلومية، وقد لا يفتقر أصلاً كما في المتيامن والمتياسر، فإن الاتصاف بذلك لا يكون باعتبار صفة حقيقية في شيء منهما.
He said: (And the two relations) may be in agreement like brotherhood and may be in opposition like fatherhood and sonship, and the expression of them may be by a name like 'father' and 'son', and may need a link for one of them like 'head' and 'possessor of a head', and their occurrence may need an attribute in both sides like 'lover' and 'beloved' or in one of them like 'knower' and 'known' or not at all like 'right' and 'left'.
Meaning that the relation, which is the true relative, may be equal in the two sides like brotherhood, and may be different like fatherhood and sonship, and the difference may be defined like double and half, and may not be like in excess and deficiency. And the expression of the two relatives may not need a letter of relation, and that is when each of them has a lexical term that connotes the addition by implication like 'father' and 'son', 'slave' and 'master', and so on; and it may need that, namely when that connotation is absent in the second relative like 'wing of a bird', so it is expressed by 'possessor of a wing', or in the first relative like 'knowledge of the knower', so it is expressed by 'that which belongs to the knower'. And the occurrence of addition may need the existence of an attribute in each of the two sides like lovingness to perception and belovedness to beauty, or in one of them like knowership to knowledge unlike being known, and may not need at all like in being right and being left, for the description thereby is not considered a real attribute in either of them.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of relations (al-nisbatayn) and how they are expressed. It begins with the author's original statement that the two relations may be in agreement (mutawāfiqatayn) like brotherhood where the relation is the same from both sides, or may be different (mukhtālifatayn) like fatherhood and sonship where the relation is not reciprocal. Conditions for the Occurrence of Relations
Al-Taftazani explains the three conditions for the occurrence of a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the occurrence of a relation may require an attribute in both sides, in one side, or in neither side.

Core Idea
Some relations are rooted in real attributes; others are purely relational.

When we say two things are related, sometimes that relation depends on a real quality in both (like lover and beloved), sometimes only in one (like knower and known), and sometimes in none at all (like right and left). Al-Taftazani explains these three possibilities, which is crucial for accurate reasoning about existence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three cases mentioned.
The examples are lover/beloved, knower/known, and right/left.
2
Distinguish the three cases
Case 1: attribute in both sides (lover needs perception, beloved needs beauty). Case 2: attribute in one side (knower needs knowledge, but known does not need a real attribute). Case 3: no attribute (right and left are purely relative).
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this relates to attributes of Allah: some relations require an attribute in the creature but not in Allah (like Creator and creation). This passage gives the logical groundwork.
This step goes slightly beyond the passage but is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (والنسبتان) قد تتوافقان كالأخوة وقد تتخالفان كالأبوة والبنوة ، والتعبير عنهما قد يكون باسم مثل الأب والابن وقد يفتقر إلى رابطة إحداهما مثل الرأس وذو الرأس ، وعروضها قد يفتقر إلى صفة في الطرفين كالعاشق والمعشوق أو في أحدهما كالعالم والمعلوم أولا كاليمين واليسار.
يعني أن النسبة التي هي المضاف الحقيقي قد تكون متوافية في الجانبين كالإخوة، وقد تكون متخالفة كالأبوة والبنوة، والاختلاف قد يكون محدوداً كما الضعف والنصف وقد لا يكون كما في الزائد والناقص، والتعبير عن المضافين قد لا يفتقر إلى حرف نسبة، وذلك حيث يكون لكل منهما لفظ موضوع يدل بالتضمن على الإضافة مثل الأب والابن والعبد والمولى، وما أشبه ذلك وقد يفتقر وذلك حيث تنتفي تلك الدلالة في المضاف إليه مثل جناح الطير، فيعبر عنه بذي الجناح أو في المضاف كعلم العالم فيعبر عنه بما للعالم وعروض الإضافة قد يفتقر إلى حصول صفة في كل من الطرفين كالعاشقية إلى الإدراك والمعشوقية إلى الجمال أو في أحدهما كالعالمية إلى العلم بخلاف المعلومية، وقد لا يفتقر أصلاً كما في المتيامن والمتياسر، فإن الاتصاف بذلك لا يكون باعتبار صفة حقيقية في شيء منهما.
He said: (And the two relations) may be in agreement like brotherhood and may be in opposition like fatherhood and sonship, and the expression of them may be by a name like 'father' and 'son', and may need a link for one of them like 'head' and 'possessor of a head', and their occurrence may need an attribute in both sides like 'lover' and 'beloved' or in one of them like 'knower' and 'known' or not at all like 'right' and 'left'.
Meaning that the relation, which is the true relative, may be equal in the two sides like brotherhood, and may be different like fatherhood and sonship, and the difference may be defined like double and half, and may not be like in excess and deficiency. And the expression of the two relatives may not need a letter of relation, and that is when each of them has a lexical term that connotes the addition by implication like 'father' and 'son', 'slave' and 'master', and so on; and it may need that, namely when that connotation is absent in the second relative like 'wing of a bird', so it is expressed by 'possessor of a wing', or in the first relative like 'knowledge of the knower', so it is expressed by 'that which belongs to the knower'. And the occurrence of addition may need the existence of an attribute in each of the two sides like lovingness to perception and belovedness to beauty, or in one of them like knowership to knowledge unlike being known, and may not need at all like in being right and being left, for the description thereby is not considered a real attribute in either of them.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of relations (al-nisbatayn) and how they are expressed. It begins with the author's original statement that the two relations may be in agreement (mutawāfiqatayn) like brotherhood where the relation is the same from both sides, or may be different (mukhtālifatayn) like fatherhood and sonship where the relation is not reciprocal. Then it explains expression: sometimes each relative has a name that directly implies the relation (like 'father'), sometimes a linking particle is needed (like 'head' and 'possessor of a head'). The commentary then expands: the true relative (al-muḍāf al-ḥaqīqī) is the relation itself. It can be equal or different; difference may be defined (like double and half) or not (like excess and deficiency). Expression may avoid a particle when each term has a word that connotes the addition (like 'father' implies a son). When that connotation is missing, a particle is needed (like 'wing of a bird' requires 'possessor of a wing'). The occurrence of the relation may require an attribute in both sides (e.g., lover needs perception, beloved needs beauty), or in one side (e.g., knower needs knowledge, but known does not need a real attribute), or no attribute at all (like right and left, which are purely relative).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every relation involves a real attribute in both sides.
Correction: Some relations require an attribute only in one side or none at all, like knower-known or right-left.

Checkpoint

B) Knower and known
C) Right and left
D) Father and son

Key Takeaway
Relations may depend on real attributes in both sides, one side, or neither, depending on the type of relation.

?

Ibn Sina's Classification of Relations
Al-Taftazani quotes Ibn Sina's division of relations into four types and notes a variant transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's fourfold classification of relations and the variant transmission that adds a fifth category.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina classified relations into four types, but later scholars noted a fifth.

Ibn Sina, the great philosopher, attempted to categorize all relations (al-muḍāfāt) into a limited set of types. His classification is foundational in logic and theology. In this passage, al-Taftazani quotes Ibn Sina's division and then notes a variant transmission from al-Mawaqif that adds a fifth category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get an overview. Pay attention to the list of types.
Notice the word 'takādu' (almost) – it hints at non-exhaustiveness.
2
Identify the four original types
List the four categories Ibn Sina gives: equality, excess, action/passion, and imitation.
Each type is illustrated with examples.
3
Understand the examples
For each type, note the specific examples: excess includes quantity and power (e.g., dominant); action/passion includes father-son, cutter-cut; imitation includes knowledge-known, sense-sensible.
These examples clarify the meaning of each relational category.
4
Note the variant transmission
Read the final part where al-Taftazani transmits a version from al-Mawaqif that replaces the four types with a different set and adds union as a fifth type.
This shows that the classification was debated and not fixed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا تكاد تكون المضافات منحصرة في أقسام المعادلة، والتي بالزيادة والتي بالفعل والانفعال ومصدرهما من القوة والتي بالمحاكاة. فما التي بالزيادة، فإما من الكم كما يعلم، وأما في القوة مثل القاهر والغالب، والمانع وغير ذلك والتي بالفعل والانفعال كالأب والابن والقاطع والمنقطع، وما أشبه ذلك. والتي بالمحاكاة كالعلم والمعلوم، والحس والمحسوس فإن بينهما محاكاة فإن العلم يحاكي هيئة المعلوم والحس يحاكي هيئة المحسوس على هذا لا يضبط تقديره وتحديده هذه عبارته وقد نقلها في المواقف هكذا تكاد الإضافة تنحصر في أقسام المعادلة كالغالب والقاهر والمانع وفي الفعل والانفعال كالقطع والكسر، وفي المحاكاة كالعلم والحس وفي الاتحاد كالمجاورة والمشابهة.
Ibn Sina said: The relatives are almost confined to the types of equality, those of excess, those of action and passion and their source from power, and those of imitation. As for those of excess, they are either from quantity as is known, or in power like the subjugator, the dominant, the preventer, and so on. And those of action and passion like father and son, cutter and cut, and the like. And those of imitation like knowledge and known, sense and sensible, for between them there is imitation; for knowledge imitates the form of the known, and sense imitates the form of the sensible. On this basis, its estimation and definition are not fixed. This is his expression, and it was transmitted in al-Mawaqif as follows: The addition is almost confined to the types of equality like the dominant, the subjugator, the preventer; in action and passion like cutting and breaking; in imitation like knowledge and sense; and in union like contiguity and similarity.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's classification of relations (al-muḍāfāt) into four types. First are relations of equality (al-muʿādalah), such as the dominant and subjugator. Second are relations of excess (al-ziyādah), which can be of quantity or of power (like the dominant, the subjugator, the preventer). Third are relations of action and passion (al-fiʿl wa al-infiʿāl), like father (agent) and son (patient), or cutter and cut. Fourth are relations of imitation (al-muḥākāh), like knowledge and known, sense and sensible, where one imitates the other. The author notes that Ibn Sina's exact wording is not fully precise ('on this basis, its estimation and definition are not fixed'). He then cites a version transmitted in al-Mawaqif (by al-Jurjani) that adds a fifth type: union (al-ittiḥād), like contiguity and similarity. Thus the classification here is followed by a critical note that the division is not fully exhaustive or precise.

الزيادة
الفعل والانفعال
المحاكاة
الاتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's classification is exhaustive and fixed.
Correction: The author notes that the classification is only approximate (takādu) and that a variant transmission adds a fifth type, showing it is not exhaustive.

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is an example of relations of imitation?
A) Father and son
B) Dominant and subjugator
C) Knowledge and known
D) Cutter and cut

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's classification of relations into four types is a foundational logical tool, but it is not exhaustive and later scholars modified it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Can you identify one belief about yourself or others that you have rigidly classified, and commit to re-examining it this week in light of a deeper truth?
?
The category of 'imitation' describes how knowledge imitates the known. When you learn about Allah's names and attributes, is your knowledge merely a passive copy, or does it transform you into a true servant who reflects those attributes in action? What specific attribute (e.g., mercy, justice) will you strive to embody today, not just know about?

The variant classification adds 'union' (e.g., contiguity, similarity). Relation as a Universal Accident in the Ten Categories
Al-Taftazani lists relation examples for each category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how relation (al-iḍāfah) can be applied to each of the ten Aristotelian categories, and why this shows that relation is not limited to its own category but is a universal accident.

Core Idea
Relation touches all categories, not just itself.

In this passage, al-Taftazani demonstrates a key point in the logic of categories: relation (al-iḍāfah) is not confined to its own category but rather occurs in every category of existence. He provides concrete examples for each of the ten categories, from the Necessary (wājib) to passion (infiʿāl). Let's examine these examples to see how relation manifests universally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two parts: a general list of examples and then a specific mapping to categories.
2
Identify the ten categories
The passage lists ten categories: Necessary (not a category but the subject), substance, quantity, quality, where, when, relation, position, possession, action, passion. Make sure you know which is which.
The Necessary is not one of the ten; it is above them.
3
Match each example to its category
For each category, al-Taftazani gives a relative term as an example. Write down the category and its example. For example, substance is like 'father'.
There is a probable miscopy: 'son' might be 'where'.
4
Understand the significance
Reflect on why this list matters: it shows that relation is not a separate category but an accident that can attach to all existents.
This is a key insight in Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ( ويعرض) لكل موجود كالأول والأب والأقل والآخر والأعلى والأقدم والأقرب والأشد انتصاباً والأكسى والأقطع والأشد تسخناً.
أي الإضافة لكل موجود فالواجب كالأول والجوهر كالأب والكم كالأقل، والكيف كالآخر، والابن كالأعلى والمتى كالأقدم والإضافة كالأقرب والوضع كالأشد انتصاباً والملك كالأكسى والفعل كالأقطع والانفعال كالأشد تسخناً.
He said: (And it occurs) to every existent like the first, the father, the less, the other, the higher, the older, the nearer, the more upright, the more dressed, the cut, and the hotter.
Meaning the relation is to every existent: the Necessary as the first, substance as the father, quantity as the less, quality as the other, relation as the higher? Wait, there is a confusion in the Arabic text: actually the text says 'والابن كالأعلى' which seems out of place. Let me re-translate literally: The Necessary like the first, substance like the father, quantity like the less, quality like the other, the son? Actually the Arabic has 'والابن كالأعلى' which is likely a miscopy; probably it should be 'والأين كالأعلى' (where as higher) as per categories. But we translate as given: and the son like the higher, and when like the older, and relation like the nearer, and position like the more upright, and possession like the more dressed, and action like the cut, and passion like the hotter.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage lists how relation (al-iḍāfah) occurs in every category of existence. The author first gives examples: the first (al-awwal) for the Necessary, father (al-ab) for substance, less (al-aqall) for quantity, other (al-ākhar) for quality, higher (al-aʿlā) for the 'where'? Actually the text says 'ibn' (son) but likely is 'ayn' (where). The commentary then explains each: the Necessary (al-wājib) corresponds to 'first', substance (jawhar) to 'father', quantity (kamm) to 'less', quality (kayf) to 'other', and then there is a list: the 'where' (al-ayn) is like 'higher', and 'when' (matā) like 'older', relation (iḍāfah) like 'nearer', position (waḍʿ) like 'more upright', possession (milk) like 'more dressed', action (fiʿl) like 'cutter', passion (infiʿāl) like 'hotter'. This shows that relation is not a category itself but an accident that can attach to all ten categories. The list is a mnemonic illustrating how relation manifests in each category.

The Existence of Relation as a Concomitant
Al-Taftazani explains that relation has no independent existence but is a concomitant of things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that relation (al-iḍāfah) does not have an independent existence but is a supervenient property that obtains through being added to an assumed subject.

Core Idea
Relation has no separate existence; it is a concomitant that depends entirely on the thing it qualifies.

In this passage, al-Taftazani clarifies a key point about the ontological status of relation. He argues that relation is not a self-subsistent entity; rather, it exists as an accident that supervenes on things. Understanding this helps avoid the misconception that relation is an independent category.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the passage carefully, noticing that al-Taftazani begins with a statement about 'obtaining' (taḥaṣṣul). Pay attention to the phrase 'bi-al-iḍāfati ilā al-mafrūḍ'.
The term 'al-mafrūḍ' (the assumed) is key to understanding the dependent nature of relation.
2
Understand the Distinction: Category vs Existence
Al-Taftazani specifies that even though relation is considered a category (maqūlah), its existence is not separate; it is an accident (ʿāriḍ). This clarifies that relation is not a substance.
The phrase 'wa in kānat al-maqūlah hiya al-ʿāriḍ faqaṭ' is crucial.
3
Identify the Specification Mechanism
The passage says relation's specification comes from the thing it supervenes upon (al-malḥūq). That is why relation is diverse: it takes its specific nature from the subject it attaches to.
The example of similarity (al-mushābahah) illustrates this: it is a type of relation that agrees with quality.
4
Reflect on the Common Misunderstanding
Many might think relation is an independent entity, but the text shows it is dependent. Consider how this guards against a mistaken reification of relation.
This lesson corrects the notion that relation has a self-subsistent reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وتحصلها يكون بالإضافة إلى المفروض . وإن كانت المقولة هي العارض فقط . يريد أن الإضافة ليس لها وجود مفرد بل وجودها أن يكون أمراً لاحقاً للأشياء، وتخصصها بتخصيص هذا الملحوق، وهذا معنى تنوع الإضافة وتحصلها فإن المشابهة مثلا موافق في الكيفية وهو نوع من الإضافة.
He said: And its obtaining is by addition to the assumed. Even if the category is only the accident. He intends that the addition has no separate existence, but its existence is that it is something supervenient upon things, and its specification is by the specification of this supervened thing. And this is the meaning of the diversification and obtaining of the addition, for similarity, for example, is in agreement in quality, and it is a type of addition.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage clarifies the mode of existence of relation (al-iḍāfah). The author states that relation's 'obtaining' (taḥaṣṣul) is by being added to something assumed (al-mafrūḍ), even though the category (i.e., relation as a category) is only the accident (al-ʿāriḍ). He explains that relation does not have a separate, independent existence; rather, its existence consists in being something that supervenes (lāḥiq) upon things. Its specific nature is determined by the specific nature of the thing it supervenes upon (al-malḥūq). This is why relation is diverse: for example, similarity is a kind of relation that agrees with quality. The diversification of relation is not rooted in its own essence but in the subjects to which it attaches.

Correction: Al-Taftazani explains that relation has no separate existence; it is an accident that supervenes on things and its specification depends on the subject it attaches to.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Four Types of Relation in Kalam
Quotes al-Mawaqif to classify relations into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of relation (al-idafah) as classified in the kalam tradition: equation, action/passion, imitation, and union.

Core Idea
Relation is not a single category but occurs in four fundamental modes.

In this passage, the author quotes al-Mawaqif to present a classification of the different ways things can be related. Rather than a single concept, relation manifests in four distinct forms: by equation, by action and passion, by imitation, and by union. This classification sets the stage for understanding how relation attaches to all categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the author quotes another work (al-Mawaqif) to present the classification.
The passage begins with examples of imitation and then lists the four types.
2
Identify the four types
Look for the four categories mentioned: equation, action/passion, imitation, and union. Write them down along with the examples given.
Equation examples include 'dominant' and 'vanquisher'; action/passion includes 'cutting' and 'breaking'.
3
Understand each type
Reflect on what each type means: equation implies mutual relation, action/passion involves agent and patient, imitation is representation, and union is contiguity or similarity.
The example of knowledge imitating the known illustrates imitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنقطع وما أشبه ذلك. والتي بالمحاكاة كالعلم والمعلوم، والحس والمحسوس فإن بينهما محاكاة فإن العلم يحاكي هيئة المعلوم والحس يحاكي هيئة المحسوس على هذا لا يضبط تقديره وتحديده هذه عبارته وقد نقلها في المواقف هكذا تكاد الإضافة تنحصر في أقسام المعادلة كالغالب والقاهر والمانع وفي الفعل والانفعال كالقطع والكسر، وفي المحاكاة كالعلم والحس وفي الاتحاد كالمجاورة والمشابهة.
قال: (ويعرض) لكل موجود كالأول والأب والأقل والآخر والأعلى والأقدم والأقرب والأشد انتصاباً والأكسى والأقطع والأشد تسخناً.
أي الإضافة لكل موجود فالواجب كالأول والجوهر كالأب والكم كالأقل، والكيف كالآخر، والابن كالأعلى والمتى كالأقدم والإضافة كالأقرب والوضع كالأشد انتصاباً والملك كالأكسى والفعل كالأقطع والانفعال كالأشد تسخناً.
And the disconnected and the like. And those by imitation, like knowledge and the known, and sensation and the sensed, for between them is imitation: knowledge imitates the form of the known, and sensation imitates the form of the sensed. According to this, its estimation and determination are not fixed. This is his expression, and it has been transmitted in al-Mawaqif. Thus relation is almost restricted to the divisions of equation, like the dominant, the vanquisher, and the preventer; and in action and passion, like cutting and breaking; and in imitation, like knowledge and sensation; and in union, like adjacency and similarity.
He said: "And it occurs" for every existent, like the First, the Father, the Less, the Other, the Higher, the Older, the Nearest, the Most Upright, the Most Comprehensive, the Most Cutting, the Hottest.
Meaning relation applies to every existent: the Necessary like the First, substance like the Father, quantity like the Less, quality like the Other, and [al-ibn] like the Higher, time like the Older, relation like the Nearest, position like the Most Upright, possession like the Most Comprehensive, action like the Most Cutting, passion like the Hottest.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage classifies relations into types—by imitation, equation, action/passion, and union—and then applies the category of relation to each of the ten Aristotelian categories. The author first presents a quotation from al-Mawaqif listing four types of relation: (1) relations of equation (like dominant and dominated), (2) relations of action and passion (like cutting and being cut), (3) relations of imitation (like knowledge imitating the known), and (4) relations of union (like adjacency). Then the author states that relation (al-idafah) occurs for every existent, giving examples for each category: for the Necessary (al-wajib) it is 'the First', for substance (al-jawhar) it is 'the Father', for quantity (al-kamm) it is 'the Less', for quality (al-kayf) it is 'the Other', for color (al-lawn) it is 'the Higher', for time (al-mata) it is 'the Older', for relation itself it is 'the Nearest', for position (al-wad') it is 'the Most Upright', for possession (al-milk) it is 'the Most Comprehensive', for action (al-fi'l) it is 'the Most Cutting', and for passion (al-infi'al) it is 'the Hottest'. This shows that relation is not a separate category but a concomitant that attaches to all categories.

Relation as a Concomitant Without Independent Existence
Explains that relation has no separate existence but is a concomitant attaching to things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that relation (al-idafah) has no separate existence but is a concomitant that attaches to things, and why the category of relation is the pure relative, not the composite.

Core Idea
Relation has no existence of its own; it exists only as an attachment to something else.

In this passage, the author explains a key point in the ontology of categories: relation does not exist as an independent entity. Rather, it is a concomitant (lahiq) that adheres to things and takes its specification from them. This distinction is crucial for understanding the category of relation and why it is limited to pure relatives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main claim: relation has no separate existence.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'tahassul' and 'idafah'.
2
Identify the key contrast
Notice the distinction between the composite compound (majmu' murakkab) and the pure relative (mudaf). The author gives the example of similarity as something possessing relation versus relation itself.
3
Understand the consequence
If the category were the composite, categories would not be limited; every derivative from an accident would be a category.
This shows the importance of the correct definition.
4
Connect to broader kalam
Reflect on how this fits into the discussion of the ten categories and the nature of accidental attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وتحصلها يكون بالإضافة إلى المفروض.
وإن كانت المقولة هي العارض فقط.
يريد أن الإضافة ليس لها وجود مفرد بل وجودها أن يكون أمراً لاحقاً للأشياء، وتخصصها بتخصص هذا الملحوق، وهذا معنى تنوع الإضافة وتحصلها فإن المشابهة مثلا موافق في الكيفية وهو نوع من الحقيقي، وأما المجموع المركب من اللحوق والإضافة كالكيف الموافق فإنما هو شيء ذو إضافة لا إضافة، ولهذا اتفقوا على أن المقولة هي الأمر الذي يعارض له التقيد واللحوق، أعني المضاف الذي لا ماهية له سوى كونه مضافاً لا المجموع المركب وإلا لما انحصرت المقولات بل كان كل مشتق من العرض مقولة.
He said: "And its actualization is by relation to the assumed." Even if the category is only the accident. He means that relation has no single existence, but its existence is that it is a thing adhering to things, and its specification is by the specification of this adhered-to. And this is the meaning of the diversity of relation and its actualization. For example, similarity agrees in quality and is a type of the real. As for the composite compound of adherence and relation, like the agreeing quality, it is something possessing relation, not relation. For this reason they agreed that the category is the thing that is opposed by restriction and adherence, I mean the relative which has no essence except being relative, not the composite compound. Otherwise the categories would not be limited, but every derivative from the accident would be a category.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage explains that relation (al-idafah) has no independent existence in itself. The author quotes the statement that its actualization (tahassul) is only by relation to an assumed term (al-mafrud). Even though the category of relation is only an accident (al-‘arid), it is not a separately existing entity. The commentary clarifies: relation is a thing that adheres to other things (lahiq lil-ashya'), and its specificity comes from the thing it adheres to. This is the meaning of the diversity of relation. For example, similarity agrees in quality but is a type of the real (meaning it has some reality). However, the composite compound (al-majmu' al-murakkab) consisting of adherence and relation—like a quality that is similar—is something that possesses relation (dhu idafah), not relation itself (laysa bi-idafah). Therefore, scholars agreed that the category (al-maqulah) is what is subject to restriction and adherence, i.e., the relative (al-mudaf) which has no essence other than being relative, not the composite compound. If it were the composite, then every derivative from the accident would be a category, which would lead to an infinite number of categories. Thus, relation as a category is the pure relative, not the composite.

إضافة
عارض
لحوق
مضاف
مقولة
مجموع مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the composite compound (e.g., a quality with its relation) is itself the category of relation.
Correction: The passage insists the category is only the relation attribute itself (the pure relative), not the composite of relation and its subject.

Checkpoint

B) Because otherwise every derivative from accident would be a category
C) Because relation is a substance
D) Because the composite is not real

Key Takeaway
Relation as a category is the pure relative, not the compound of relation and quality.

Equality of Correlatives and the Objection of Precedence
Proves equality of correlatives, answers a key objection, and classifies types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that correlatives are equal in actualization, absoluteness, existence, and non-existence, and how the objection about priority and posteriority is resolved by distinguishing conceptual correlation from existential separation.

Core Idea
Correlation is between concepts, not essences — they coexist in the mind.

This passage establishes a foundational principle in kalam: the two sides of a relation must be symmetrical in all respects. Using the example of double and half, the author shows that if a relation is actual, absolute, or existent in one side, it is likewise in the other. An objection about priority and posteriority is then addressed, clarifying that correlation is conceptual. The passage also classifies three types of relative terms and begins the majority view against external realization of relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once through
Read the Arabic text aloud, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: statement, explanation, objection, answer, classification, and a concluding note.
The last sentence is incomplete; we will note that later.
2
Identify the main principle
Look at the opening statement: 'the two sides are equal in actualization, absoluteness, existence and non-existence, mentally and externally, potentially and actually.' Understand what each pair means.
This is a universal claim about all relations.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The objection says prior and posterior are correlatives but they don't exist together. The answer: correlation is between concepts, not essences. Understand the distinction between concept (mafhum) and essence (dhat).
Concepts like 'priority' and 'posteriority' coexist in the mind even if the actual things are separated in time.
4
Review the three types of relatives
The passage lists three cases: each can exist without the other, one can exist without the other but not vice versa, or each cannot exist without the other. Find examples for each.
Father and son is an example of the first type; world and knowledge is the second; cause and effect is the third.
5
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage ends mid-sentence with the majority view that relations have no external existence to avoid infinite regress. Think about why infinite regress might be a problem.
This point is incomplete in the source; we will revisit it when we have more context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (ويتكافأ الطرفان) في التحصيل والإطلاق والوجود والعدم ذهناً وخارجاً وقوة وفعلاً، والمتضايفان من المتقدم والمتأخر هما المفهومان، وهما معاً في الذهن، وإنما الانفكاك بين المعروضين.
يعني أن الإضافة إذا كانت في أحد الطرفين محصلة كانت في الطرف الآخر كذلك، وإذا كانت مطلقة فمطلقة. مثلاً الضعف العددي على الإطلاق بازاء النصف العددي على الإطلاق، والضعف الذي هو هذا العدد كالأربعة مثلاً بازاء نصفه كالاثنين، وكذا إذا كانت في أحد الطرفين موجوداً أو معدوماً بالقوة أو بالفعل بحسب الذهن أو بحسب الخارج كان في الطرف الآخر كذلك.
فإن قيل: المتقدم والمتأخر متضايفان مع أنهما لا يوجدان معاً.
قلنا: التضايف إنما هو بين مفهوميهما لا بين ذاتيهما بل بين مفهومي التقدم والتأخر، وهما معاً في الذهن، وإنما الافتراق بين الذاتين. وذاتا المضافين قد يوجد كل منهما بدون الآخر كالأب والابن، وقد يوجد أحدهما بدون الآخر من غير عكس كالعالم والعلم، وقد يمتنع كل بدون الآخر كالعلة مع معلولها الخاص.
قال: (هذا) والجمهور على أن لا تحقق للإضافة في الخارج وإلا لزم التسلسل، لأن
He said: "And the two sides are equal" in actualization, absoluteness, existence, and non-existence, mentally and externally, potentially and actually. And the two correlatives among the prior and the posterior are the two concepts, and they are together in the mind, while the separation is only between the things characterized.
He means that if relation is actualized in one of the two sides, it is so in the other side; if it is absolute, then absolute. For example, numerical double absolutely is opposite numerical half absolutely, and the double that is this number, like four, is opposite its half, like two. Similarly, if it is in one side existent or non-existent potentially or actually, according to the mind or according to the external, it is so in the other side.
If it is said: The prior and the posterior are correlatives while they do not exist together.
We say: The correlation is only between their concepts, not between their essences, but rather between the concepts of priority and posteriority, and they are together in the mind; the separation is only between the essences. And the essences of the two relatives: sometimes each exists without the other, like father and son; sometimes one exists without the other without reciprocity, like the world and knowledge; and sometimes each is impossible without the other, like the cause with its specific effect.
He said: "This" and the majority are of the view that there is no realization for relation externally, otherwise infinite regress would follow, because...
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage addresses the equality of correlatives (al-mutada'ifan) in all respects. The author states that if relation is actualized, absolute, existent, or non-existent in one side, it is the same in the other side, whether mentally or externally, potentially or actually. He gives the example of the double and half: the absolute double corresponds to absolute half, and a specific double (like four) corresponds to its specific half (two). This shows symmetry. Then an objection is raised: priority and posteriority are correlatives, yet the prior and posterior do not exist together (e.g., a father may exist without a son at some time). The answer is that the correlation is between the concepts (mafhumay al-taqaddum wa al-ta'akhkhur), which exist together in the mind, not between the actual essences (dhatayn). The essences may be separated in time. The passage then classifies three types of relative terms: (1) each can exist without the other (e.g., father and son—though one can exist without the other at certain times, but typically they coexist; here it means each can be conceived without the other); (2) one can exist without the other but not vice versa (e.g., the world exists but knowledge of the world does not necessarily coexist); (3) each is impossible without the other (e.g., cause and its specific effect). Finally, the author records the majority position that relation has no realized external existence, otherwise an infinite regress would follow (though the argument is cut off).

طرفان

إطلاق

متأخر
مفهوم
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correlatives must always coexist in external existence.
Correction: Correlation is between concepts, not essences; the concepts coexist in the mind even if the essences are separated in time or existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the objection, why can the prior and posterior be correlatives without existing together?
A) Because they are not real relations
B) Because correlation is between their concepts, not their essences
C) Because they exist potentially
D) Because one always exists before the other

Key Takeaway
Correlatives are equal in all respects, and their correlation is conceptual, not dependent on external coexistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that the concepts 'father' and 'son' are correlatives in the mind, but their essences can exist separately in time. In your own life, do you treat your relationships (like 'father-son' or 'teacher-student') as conceptual truths that demand external symmetry? Reflect on a specific relationship where you felt entitled to reciprocity. How can you realign your expectations to embrace the conceptual nature of that relationship without bitterness?
?
Consider a deeply held belief about priority—like 'success before happiness' or 'knowledge before action.' The lesson teaches that priority and posteriority are correlative concepts that coexist in the mind, not in external reality. Is there a priority you have rigidly imposed on your life that may be just a conceptual construct? What would change if you saw it as a mental tool rather than an external law?
?
The passage distinguishes between concepts and essences. When you think about your own identity (essence), do you conflate it with concepts others have of you? How can you live more fully grounded in your essence before Allah, rather than in the relational concepts that others project? What one step will you take today to detach from a conceptual label that has been weighing on your heart?

The Nature of Relation and the Equality of Relata
Definition of relation and equality of relata

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the category of relation is a relative with no essence other than being relative, and that the two relata are equal in determination, absoluteness, existence, non-existence, mental/external, and potential/actual.

Core Idea
Relation is purely conceptual; its two sides are always equal in all modes.

Before diving into specific theological arguments, Sanusi establishes the foundational principle of relation (al-idāfah). He clarifies that the category 'relation' refers only to that which has no essence but being relative—any composite thing that merely 'has' a relation is not itself the category. He then asserts a critical rule: the two relata are always equal in determination, absoluteness, existence, and other modes, both mentally and externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of relation
Read the Arabic passage from 'الحقيقي' to 'مقولة'. Notice how Sanusi distinguishes the pure relative from a composite that has a relation.
Key phrase: 'المضاف الذي لا ماهية له سوى كونه مضافا'.
2
Identify the equality rule
Find the phrase 'ويتكافأ الطرفان' and the list of four pairs: determination/absoluteness, existence/non-existence, mental/external, potential/actual.
These pairs cover all logical modes.
3
Study the examples
Read the example of double and half. See how the equality holds for both absolute and determinate cases.
The example shows the rule in concrete terms.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why must the two sides be equal? The commentary explains that relation is a single mental consideration that applies equally to both relata.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحقيقي، وأما المجموع المركب من اللحوق والإضافة كالكيف الموافق فإنما هو شيء ذو إضافة لا إضافة، ولهذا اتفقوا على أن المقولة هي الأمر الذي يعارض له التقيد واللحوق ، أعني المضاف الذي لا ماهية له سوى كونه مضافاً المجموع المركب وإلا لما انحصرت المقولات بل كان كل مشتق من لا العرض مقولة .
(قال: (ويتكافأ الطرفان)
في التحصيل والإطلاق والوجود والعدم ذهناً وخارجاً وقوة وفعلاً، والمتضايفان من المتقدم والمتأخر هما المفهومان، وهما معاً في الذهن، وإنما الإنفكاك بين (المعروضين.
يعني أن الإضافة إذا كانت في أحد الطرفين محصلة كانت في الطرف الآخر كذلك، وإذا كانت مطلقة فمطلقة. مثلاً الضعف العددي على الإطلاق بازاء النصف العددي على الإطلاق، والضعف الذي هو هذا العدد كالأربعة مثلاً بازاء نصفه كالاثنين، وكذا إذا كانت في أحد الطرفين موجوداً أو معدوماً بالقوة أو بالفعل بحسب الذهن أو بحسب الخارج كان في الطرف الآخر كذلك .
فإن قيل : المتقدم والمتأخر متضايفان مع أنهما لا يوجدان معاً .
قلنا : التضايف إنما هو بين مفهوميهما لاذايتهما بل بين مفهومي التقدم والتأخر، وهما معاً في الذهن، وإنما الافتراق بين الذاتين وذاتا المضافين قد يوجد كل منهما بدون الآخر كالأب والابن، وقد يوجد أحدهما بدون الآخر من غير عكس كالعالم والعلم ، وقد يمتنع كل بدون الآخر كالعلة مع معلولها الخاص .
As for the composite combination of attachment and relation like the conforming quality, it is something having a relation, not the relation itself. That is why they agreed that the category is that which is opposed by restriction and attachment, namely the relative which has no essence other than being relative—the composite combination. Otherwise, the categories would not be limited, but rather every derivative from 'not' would be a category. (He said: “The two sides are equal” in determination, absoluteness, existence, non-existence, mentally and externally, potentially and actually. The two correlatives among the prior and posterior are the two concepts, and they are together in the mind; separation is only between the two subjects. It means that if relation is determined in one of the two sides, it is so in the other; if it is absolute, it is absolute. For example, the numerical double absolutely corresponds to the numerical half absolutely, and the double which is this number, like four, corresponds to its half, like two. Likewise, if it exists or is non-existent potentially or actually mentally or externally in one side, it is so in the other. If it is said: The prior and posterior are correlatives, yet they do not exist together. We say: The correlation is only between their two concepts, not between their essences, but between the concepts of priority and posteriority, and they are together in the mind; separation is only between the essences. The essences of the two relatives: each may exist without the other, like father and son; one may exist without the other without reverse, like the knower and knowledge; each may be impossible without the other, like the cause and its specific effect.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of relation (al-idafah) and establishes the principle of equality between the two relata. It begins by distinguishing between a composite thing that has a relation versus the relation itself, noting that the category (al-maqūlah) is that which is opposed by restriction and attachment—the relative (al-muḍāf) whose essence is nothing but being relative. If that were not the case, categories would not be limited. The author then cites the statement 'The two sides are equal' in four pairs: determination/absoluteness, existence/non-existence, mental/external, potential/actual. The correlatives (al-mutadāʿifān) of priority and posteriority are concepts that coexist in the mind; separation occurs only in the extra-mental subjects. The explanation illustrates with examples: a determined double corresponds to a determined half, an absolute double to an absolute half, and existence/non-existence in one side entails the same in the other. An objection is raised: prior and posterior are correlatives, yet they do not coexist (e.g., prior exists before posterior). The answer is that the correlation is between the concepts, not the essences. The concepts of priority and posteriority are simultaneous in the mind; separation is between the actual entities. Three cases of relatives are then given: (1) each can exist without the other (father/son), (2) one can exist without the other non-reciprocally (knower/knowledge), (3) neither can exist without the other (cause/effect). This clarifies that relation is a conceptual matter, not a real external tie.

Key Terms

متضايفان
تحصيل
إطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a relative exists as a real external property inherent in one subject independent of the other.
Correction: The passage establishes that the category of relation has no essence other than being relative; it is a purely conceptual tie that equally involves both relata. The relata are equal in all modes—determined/absolute, existent/non-existent, etc.—and the relation is not a real attribute in one side alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'the two sides are equal' mean?
A) They are equal in essence.
B) They are equal in all four pairs of modes.
C) They are equal only in existence.
D) They are equal in the mind but not outside.

Key Takeaway
Relation is a conceptual correlate that exists equally in both relata across all logical modes, not a real property in one subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If relation is purely conceptual and both sides are equal in all modes, what does that imply about a relationship where you feel you are the primary determiner? How does this challenge your sense of control or superiority in that relationship?
?
Consider a time you viewed someone as merely 'related' to you without realizing that you are equally defined by that relation. How does the principle of equality of relata call you to re-evaluate that person's importance and your own dependence?
?
The passage says the two correlatives exist together in the mind, but the subjects may be separate. In your relationship with Allah, are you more focused on your own separate subject or on the conceptual correlate of being His servant? How does this distinction affect the sincerity of your worship?

Objection on Priority and Posteriority and Threefold Division of Relatives
Answering objection on priority/posteriority and classifying relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that priority and posteriority are correlatives only at the conceptual level, and that relatives can be classified into three cases based on the coexistence of their referents.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are concepts that coexist in the mind, not in external reality.

A potential objection arises: if prior and posterior are correlatives, they should coexist, but in reality they do not (e.g., the prior exists before the posterior). Sanusi resolves this by clarifying that the correlation is between the concepts, not the actual entities. He then provides a threefold classification of relatives based on whether the essences can exist without each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic from 'فإن قيل' to the end. Identify the objection: that prior and posterior are correlatives yet do not exist together.
The objection seems to challenge the earlier principle of equality.
2
Understand the answer
Sanusi answers that the correlation is between the concepts (mafhūmayn) of priority and posteriority, not between their essences (dhātiyyayn). The concepts are simultaneous in the mind.
Key distinction: concepts vs. essences.
3
Study the three cases
Find the three cases: (1) each can exist without the other (father/son), (2) one without the other non-reciprocally (knower/knowledge), (3) neither without the other (cause/effect).
This shows how relata can relate in extra-mental existence.

Majority Position and the Infinite Regress Argument
Sanusi states the majority view and gives the infinite regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority position that relation has no external reality and the first argument based on infinite regress.

Core Idea
If relation were real, each inherence would require another relation, leading to an infinite chain.

The theologians argue that relation cannot exist externally because it would cause an infinite regress. In this lesson, we examine this first proof as well as the initial statement of the majority view and a possible rejoinder.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial statement
Read the first part of the passage where the majority position is stated and the three objections are summarized.
Notice that the objections are immediately followed by a rejoinder.
2
Understand the rejoinder
The text provides a possible answer: negating all particular relations does not negate the universal relation. Reflect on this response.
3
Identify the first formal proof
The first proof is introduced by 'الأول'. Read it carefully and see how it leads to infinite regress.
The key is that inherence itself is a relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجمهور على ان لا تحقق للاضافة في الخارج وإلا لزم التسلسل، لأن الحلول في المحل أيضاً إضافة لها حلول، والدور أيضاً، لأن الاتصاف بالوجود إضافة يتوقف وجودها على كون مطلق الإضافة محالة وجود، وأيضاً يلزم عدم تناهي أوصاف كل عدد بحسب ماله من الإضافة إلى ما عداه . وقد يجاب بأن غاية ذلك امتناع أن يوجد كل إضافة وسلب الكل لا يقتضي سلب الكلي، ويستدل بأنا تقطع بفوقية السماء وتحتية الأرض، وبأبوة زيد وبنوة عمرو، وان لم يوجد اعتبار العقل وقد مر مثله .
يعني أن ما ذكر وإن كان مشعراً بأن الإضافة قد توجد في الخارج، لكن جمهور المتكلمين، وبعض الحكماء على أنه لا تحقق للإضافة في الخارج تمسكاً بوجوه .
الأول : أنها لو كانت موجودة في الخارج لكانت في محل، وحولها في المحل إضافة بينها وبين المحل مغايرة لها فيها، فينقل الكلام إليه ويلزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة .
الثاني : أنها لو كانت موجودة أي متصفة بالوجود واتصافها بالوجود إضافة خاصة يتوقف وجودها على وجود مطلق الإضافة لزم الدور، ولا حاجة إلى ما يقال من أنها لو كانت موجودة لكانت مشاركة لسائر الموجودات في الوجود، وممتازة عنها بخصوصيتها، وما لم تتصف تلك الخصوصية بالوجود لم تكن الإضافة موجودة لكن الاتصاف إضافة مخصوصة يتوقف وجودها على وجود مطلق الإضافة ، فيلزم تقدمه على نفسه .
الثالث: أنه يلزم أن يوجد لكل عدد صفات لا نهاية لها بحسب ما لها من
And the majority holds that there is no reality for relation in the external world, otherwise there would be infinite regress, because the inherence in a substrate is also a relation having inherence, and also circularity, because the qualification by existence is a relation whose existence depends on absolute relation being impossible in existence, and also it would entail the non-finiteness of the attributes of every number according to its relation to what is other than it. And it may be answered that the utmost of that is the impossibility of every particular relation existing, and the negation of all does not entail the negation of the universal, and one argues by our certain judgment of the sky's being above and the earth's being below, and of Zayd's fatherhood and Amr's sonship, even if the mind does not consider it, and similar has passed. It means that what was mentioned, although it suggests that relation may exist externally, the majority of theologians (al-mutakallimūn) and some philosophers (al-ḥukamāʾ) hold that there is no reality for relation in the external world, adhering to proofs. The first: that if it existed externally, it would be in a substrate, and its inherence in the substrate is a relation between it and the substrate different from it, so the discussion is transferred to it, and infinite regress in existing things would follow. The second: that if it existed, i.e., qualified by existence, and its being qualified by existence is a special relation whose existence depends on the existence of absolute relation, then circularity would follow, and there is no need for what is said that if it existed, it would share with other existents in existence and be distinguished by its particularity, and as long as that particularity is not qualified by existence, the relation would not exist, but the qualification is a special relation whose existence depends on the existence of absolute relation, so its priority over itself would follow. The third: that it would entail that for every number there are infinite attributes according to what it has of
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage presents the majority view (al-jumhūr) of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) and some philosophers (al-ḥukamāʾ) that relation (al-iḍāfah) has no reality in the external world. It begins by stating that if relation were real, it would entail three problems: infinite regress (al-tasalsul) because inherence in a substrate is itself a relation; circularity (al-dawr) because being qualified by existence is a relation dependent on absolute relation; and infinite attributes (ʿadam tanāhī al-awṣāf) for numbers. A possible rejoinder is noted: the impossibility of every particular relation does not negate the universal, and common sense confirms relations like above/below or fatherhood/sonship. However, the author explains that despite this appearance, the majority holds the opposite, providing three formal arguments. The first argument: if relation existed externally, it would require a substrate; its inherence in that substrate would be another relation between it and the substrate, leading to infinite regress. The second argument: if relation existed, it would be qualified by existence; that qualification is a specific relation whose existence depends on the existence of absolute relation, resulting in circularity (the specific relation presupposes the absolute, which itself presupposes the specific). The third argument (incomplete): for every number, there would be infinite attributes due to its relations to all other numbers. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the intended argument is that each number would have infinitely many relational attributes, which is impossible.

Key Terms
تحقق
الخارج
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relational attributes like fatherhood are real entities existing in the external world.
Correction: The kalam position denies that relations have external reality; they are mental considerations.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument given against the reality of relation in the external world?
A) Circularity due to the attribute of existence.
B) Infinite regress due to the relation of inherence.
C) Infinite attributes for numbers.
D) Common sense contradicts it.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against the reality of relation is that it would lead to an infinite regress, because inherence itself is a relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat relationships and connections as if they are fundamental realities, or do you recognize that all relations are contingent and mind-dependent? How does this affect your view of your own identity?
?
If relations are not real in themselves, what does that say about your attachment to social status, family ties, or worldly positions? Are you basing your sense of self on something that is not ultimately real?
?
Can you think of a relationship in your life that you consider essential? In light of this lesson, how does the view that relations are not externally real challenge you to reevaluate your priorities?
