The category of 'when' includes reference to the now—the present moment. Are you conscious of how you spend each 'now' in obedience or heedlessness? What concrete step can you take to ensure the next 'now' is spent in a way that pleases Allah?

Proof that Correlatives Cannot Be Opposites
A proof that correlatives cannot be opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why correlative terms like 'hotter' and 'colder' cannot be true opposites, and how the argument distinguishes between the relation of opposition and correlativity.

Core Idea
Correlatives are not opposites because each is defined by the other.

This passage presents a logical demonstration that correlative terms (such as 'hotter' and 'colder') cannot be opposites, even in a secondary sense. The argument hinges on the difference between the relation of opposition (tadadd) and the relation of correlativity (tadayuf). The author shows that if something is correlative, it lacks the independent nature required for true opposition. As you read, pay attention to the objection and its rebuttal, which solidify the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the main claim and initial argument
Begin by reading the first sentence of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author states that 'hotter' is opposite to 'colder' only if the relation is independent of its subject.
The key phrase is 'إذا لم تكن الإضافة تابعة لمعروضها'.
2
Follow the core proof
Read the second part where the author explains why correlatives cannot be opposites. The argument uses the fact that opposition and correlativity are different relations, and in any pair of opposites there must be a non-correlative element.
The description of opposition itself is correlative, so the non-correlative element must be the subject.
3
Understand the summary and objection
The author summarizes that 'hotter' and 'colder' do not meet the definition of opposites because each is only conceived by reference to the other. Then consider the objection: what about the bodies that are subject to these qualities?
The objection tries to locate non-correlativity in the substances.
4
Analyze the rebuttal
The author rejects the objection by clarifying that opposition and correlativity apply to the qualities themselves (heat, cold, hotter-ness), not to the substances. Substances like fire and water can be conceived without relation.
This reinforces that correlatives are not opposites at any level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستقلال بدليل أنه قال : كما أن الحار ضد للبارد، وكذا الأحر للأبرد إذ لم تكن الإضافة تابعة لمعروضها في هذا الحكم لكانت مستقلة فيه لكن احتجاجه بأن تقابل التضاد غير تقابل التضايف، فيجب أن يوجد في المتضادين شيء ليس بمتضايف، لكن وصف التضاد متضايف فلم يبق إلا موضوع التضاد فلزم أن يكون غير متضايف يدل على أن المتضايفين لا يتضادان لا تبعاً ولا استقلالاً، وحاصله أنه لا يصدق على مثل الأحر والأبرد حد الضدين، إذ لا يعقل كل منهما إلا بالقياس إلى الآخر، لا يقال الشيء الذي لا تضايف فيه هو موضوع الأحر والأبرد أعني الجسمين لأنا نقول التضاد أو التضايف إنما تعتبر فيما يرد على الموضوع كالحرارة والبرودة والأحرية والأبردية فتكون هي موضوع وصف التضاد أو التضايف لا موضوعاتها من النار والماء وغير ذلك مما يمكن تعقل كل منهما بدون الآخر، ولو في التضايف .
Independence by his saying: Just as hot is opposite to cold, so also hotter is opposite to colder. If the relation were not dependent on its subject in this judgment, it would be independent in it. But his argument that the opposition relation is different from the correlativity relation necessitates that there must exist in the two opposites something that is not correlative. However, the description of opposition is correlative, so nothing remains except the subject of opposition, so it follows that it is non-correlative. This indicates that correlatives are not opposites, neither as a consequence nor independently. The summary is that the definition of opposites does not apply to such things as hotter and colder, since each is only conceived by reference to the other. One cannot say: the thing that has no correlativity is the subject of hotter and colder, namely the two bodies, because we say: opposition or correlativity is only considered in what inheres in the subject, like heat and cold and hotter-ness and colder-ness. So these are the subject of the description of opposition or correlativity, not their subjects like fire and water and other things each of which can be conceived without the other, even in correlativity.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a proof that correlative terms (such as 'hotter' and 'colder') cannot be true opposites (such as 'hot' and 'cold'). The author argues that if the relation were independent of its subject, then 'hotter' and 'colder' would be independent opposites. But his opponent claims that the relation of opposition is different from the relation of correlativity. The author replies that in any pair of opposites, there must be a non-correlative element. The description of opposition itself is correlative (since the opposite of something is defined relative to it), so the only remaining non-correlative element is the subject in which the opposites inhere. Therefore, correlatives cannot be opposites even in a derivative sense. The summary is that 'hotter' and 'colder' do not meet the definition of opposites because each is only intelligible by reference to the other. A possible objection is that the non-correlative subject is the two bodies (e.g., fire and water). The author rejects this, explaining that opposition and correlativity apply to the qualities themselves (heat, cold, hotter-ness, colder-ness), not to the substances that possess them, since substances can be conceived without the relation.

تضايف
متضادان
متضايفان
إضافة
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The passage shows that each is only understood in relation to the other, making them correlatives, not opposites.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are not found in the same subject
B) Because each is only understood in relation to the other
C) Because they are accidental qualities
D) Because they are substances

Key Takeaway
Correlative terms like 'hotter' and 'colder' are not opposites because each is defined by its relation to the other, and true opposites require an independent concept.

Principle: Variety of Subjects Does Not Necessitate Variety of Relations
Explanation of the principle that different subjects do not create different types of relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that the variety of subjects does not necessitate a change in the type of accidental relations, and see how it reconciles two seemingly contradictory statements.

Core Idea
The same relation type can apply across different subjects.

In this passage, the author explains a key principle regarding accidental relations: just because two subjects belong to different species does not mean the relation between them is of a different type. Using the example of two humans and two horses both agreeing in whiteness, he illustrates that the relation of 'agreement in whiteness' is the same type in both cases. This principle helps reconcile statements about relations following subjects while also being independent of subject variation. Read on to grasp this subtle but important logical point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage aloud if possible, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the argument.
2
Identify the principle and example
Find the statement of the principle: 'variety of subjects does not necessitate variety of accidental relations'. Then locate the example with humans and horses agreeing in whiteness.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The passage mentions that this principle reconciles two claims: that relations follow their subjects, and that variety of subjects doesn't cause variety. Consider how the example shows both are true—the relation type remains constant despite different subjects.
Note the subtle remark about taking the subject as either the humans or the whiteness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : (وما تقرر) من أن تنوع المعروضات لا يوجب تنوع الإضافات العارضة، فمعناه أن موافقة الإنسانين في البياض مثلاً ليس نوعاً مخالفاً لموافقة الفرسين فيه) . إشارة إلى وجه التوفيق بين قولهم إن الإضافات في نوعيتها تبع لمعروضاتها . وقولهم إن تنوع المعروضات لا يوجب تنوع العوارض ، لكن لايخفي ما فيه من أخذ المعروض في موافقة الإنسانين في البياض ، تارة الإنسان وتارة البياض .
He said: (And what is established) that the variety of subjects does not necessitate the variety of accidental relations. Its meaning is that the agreement of two humans in whiteness, for example, is not a different type from the agreement of two horses in it. This is a reference to a reconciliation between their statement that relations in their type follow their subjects, and their statement that the variety of subjects does not necessitate the variety of accidents. But it is not hidden that there is in it taking the subject in the agreement of the two humans in whiteness — sometimes the human and sometimes the whiteness.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage explains a principle: that the variety of subjects (e.g., two humans vs. two horses) does not necessitate a variety in the accidental relations that qualify them. For example, the relation of 'agreement in whiteness' between two humans is the same type as that between two horses. This is meant to reconcile two statements: (1) that relations follow their subjects in type, and (2) that variety of subjects does not necessitate variety of accidents. The author notes a subtlety: in the case of 'agreement in whiteness', the subject taken could be either the humans (the subjects) or the whiteness (the accident), and this may affect the analysis.

معروضات
إضافات

موافقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relations differ in type when the subjects are from different species.
Correction: The principle states that the type of relation (like agreement in whiteness) is the same even when subjects are of different kinds (humans vs horses).

Checkpoint

C) Variety of subjects does not necessitate variety of accidental relations

Key Takeaway
The type of an accidental relation is not determined by the species of its subject; the same relation can occur among different subjects.

Definition of the Category 'When' (متى) in Logic
Definition and classification of 'when' as a relation to time or instant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the logical category 'when' (متى) as a relation to time or to an instant, and its classification into gradual occurrence, instantaneous continuous occurrence, and boundary instant occurrence.

Core Idea
'When' is not just about time; it's about how a thing relates to time or to a moment.

The logical category 'when' (متى) is one of the categories used to analyze how things exist in time. This passage defines 'when' as a relation of a thing either to time itself (in two ways) or to an instant (the boundary of time). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise reasoning about temporal existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: first a definition, then an analogy to 'where', then a detailed breakdown of the types of temporal relation.
2
Identify the three main types
The passage describes three ways a thing can relate to time: gradual occurrence, instantaneous but continuous, and relation to an instant. Find each one in the text.
3
Examine the subdivision
The second type (instantaneous but continuous) is further divided into 'existence' and 'intermediateness'. Understand the difference based on whether the thing exists at the bounding instant or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : (ومنها المتى وهو نسبة الشيء إما إلى الزمان لوقوعه على التدريج كالحركة وما يتبعها أو دفعة لكن على استمرار الآنات كالكون والتوسط وإما إلى الآن لعدم تحصله إلا في طرف من الزمان كالوصول إلى المنتصف أو المنتهى) . كما أن الأين هو النسبة إلى المكان نفسه كذلك المتى هو النسبة إلى الزمان إلا أنها قد تكون بوقوع الشيء فيه، وقد تكون بوقوعه في طرفه الذي هو الآن، فإن كثيراً ما يسأل عنه بمتى قد يقع في الآن كالوصول إلى منتصف المسافة ميلاً، والوقوع في الزمان قد يكون بأن يكون للشيء هو اتصالية ينطبق على الزمان ولا يمكن أن يتحصل إلا فيه، وهو معنى الحصول على التدريج، وذلك كالحركات وما يتبعها كالأصوات، وقد يكون بمعنى أنه لا يوجد في ذلك الزمان أن إلا ويكون ذلك الشيء حاصلاً فيه فيكون حصوله دفعة لكن على استمرار الآنات، وينقسم إلى ما لا يكون حاصلاً في الآن الذي هو طرف حصوله الكون وإلى ما لا يكون حاصلاً في ذلك كالتوسط أعني كون المتحرك على مسافة فيما بين طرفيها
He said: (And among them is 'when', which is the relation of a thing either to time, due to its occurrence gradually, like motion and what follows it; or instantaneously but with continuity of moments, like existence and intermediateness; or to the instant, because it is not realized except at a boundary of time, like reaching the midpoint or the endpoint). Just as 'where' is the relation to place itself, similarly 'when' is the relation to time, except that it may be by the thing occurring in it, or it may be by its occurring in its boundary which is the instant. For often one asks about it with 'when' and it occurs at the instant, like reaching the midpoint of a distance of a mile. Occurrence in time may be by the thing having a continuity that matches time and cannot be realized except in it, which is the meaning of gradual occurrence, such as motions and what follows them like sounds; and it may be in the sense that there is no moment in that time except that the thing exists in it, so its occurrence is instantaneous but with continuity of moments. It is divided into what is not existent at the instant which is the boundary of its occurrence 'existence', and what is not existent at that, like intermediateness, i.e., the moving thing being at a distance between its two endpoints.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage defines the category 'when' (متى) as a relation of a thing to time or to an instant. The author explains that 'when' can be a relation to time in three ways: (1) gradual occurrence like motion and its concomitants, (2) instantaneous occurrence but with continuity of moments (like being in a location or being between), or (3) relation to an instant (the boundary of time) for events that only occur at a moment, like reaching a midpoint or endpoint. He then draws an analogy: just as 'where' is the relation to space, 'when' is the relation to time. The latter can be either by the thing occurring in time or by its occurring at the boundary (the instant). He notes that the question 'when?' often applies to instants, e.g., reaching the midpoint at mile. Occurrence in time is further classified: gradual occurrence (motions, sounds) that require time, and 'instantaneous but continuous' occurrence where something exists at every moment of a period (like staying in place). Finally, he divides the second type: what is not present at the boundary instant (e.g., 'existence' is not at the boundary because existence starts at that instant?) and what is not present at that boundary like 'intermediateness' which is being between endpoints.

Correction: The passage clarifies that 'when' can be a relation to an instant, not just to continuous time.

The Paradox of the Instant (Now)
Discussion of the existence of the instant and responses to objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objections to the real existence of the instant (now) in temporal reasoning and why common responses fail to resolve them.

Core Idea
The instant cannot exist as a real atom of time without causing infinite regress.

In this passage, the author examines a classic problem in kalām: whether the 'instant' or 'now' can be considered a genuine existent. Some assert its existence as a boundary of time analogous to a point on a line, but this leads to logical puzzles about its own non-existence and infinite succession of instants. The author presents these objections and shows that common responses do not remove the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the full English translation. Let the author's argument flow without interruption.
2
Identify the two main objections
Find the two places where the author raises problems: first about the non-existence of the instant requiring another instant, and second about the instant's own passing away also requiring another instant.
The objections are sequential.
3
Examine the failed replies
Notice the two replies that are offered and then refuted: (1) the instant is in time but not gradually, (2) non-existence occurs in all time after existence. Understand why each fails.
The refutations hinge on the 'when' of non-existence.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider what this means for the nature of time and change. The instant cannot be a genuine existent; it is only a conceptual boundary.
This connects to later discussions in kalām about temporal atoms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (وهذا تصريح) منهم بوجود الآن مع أنه لا يتصور إلا بانقطاع الزمان على أنه لو وجد فحدوث عدمه لا يكون في آن ويلزم تتالي الآنين، ولا ينفع ما يقال إنه في الزمان لكن لا على التدريج. لاخفاء يريد أن ما ذكروا من وقوع بعض الأشياء في الآن الذي هو طرف للزمان بمنزلة النقطة للخط يدل على أنه موجود لامتناع وقوع الشيء فيما لا وجود له، لكن في أنه لا تحقق لطرف الشيء في الخارج إلا بعد انقطاعه، وانقسامه بالفعل والزمان إنما ينقسم بالوهم والفرض فقط، وأيضاً لو وجد الآن ولا شك أنه على الانقضاء دون البقاء وحدوث عدمه لا يكون إلا في أن يلزم تتالي الآنين . وجوابهم بأن عدمه يكون في جميع الزمان الذي بعد الوجود لكن لا على التدريج ليصير الآن زمانياً بل بمعنى أنه يوجد في ذلك الزمان آن إلا وذلك العدم حاصل فيه على ما مر لا يدفع الإشكال لأن الكلام في حدوث العدم وهو أني ، وكون هذا الآن مغايراً الآن الوجود ضروري .
He said: (And this is an explicit statement) from them of the existence of the instant, even though it is not conceivable except by the cessation of time, and if it existed, then the occurrence of its non-existence would not be in an instant, and infinite succession of instants would follow; and it does not avail what is said that it is in time but not gradually. It is not hidden: He intends that what they mentioned—that some things occur at the instant which is the boundary of time, analogous to the point for the line—indicates that it exists, because it is impossible for something to occur in what has no existence. But the truth is that a boundary of a thing has no reality in external existence except after its cessation and its actual division, and time is only divided by estimation and assumption. Also, if the instant exists—and there is no doubt that it passes away without endurance—then the occurrence of its non-existence can only be in an instant, which necessitates infinite succession of instants. Their answer—that its non-existence occurs in all the time after its existence, but gradually, lest the instant become temporal; rather, in the sense that there is no instant in that time except that non-existence is present in it, as mentioned—does not remove the problem, because the discussion is about the occurrence of non-existence, which is 'when?' And that this instant is different from the instant of existence is necessary.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage discusses the existence of the instant (الآن) as a boundary of time. The author reports that some assert its existence, but it is only conceivable by the cessation of time. If the instant exists, its non-existence would not occur in an instant, leading to an infinite succession of instants. The common reply that it is in time but not gradually does not help. The author then explains: the argument that events occur at an instant (like reaching a point) implies the instant exists because something cannot occur in a non-existent. However, the truth is that a boundary of a thing has no external reality except after the thing ceases and is actually divided, and time is only divisible in estimation. Moreover, if the instant exists, it passes away, and its non-existence must occur at an instant, leading to infinite regress. The answer that non-existence occurs in all time after existence, not gradually, does not solve the problem because the issue is the occurrence of non-existence, which involves a temporal 'when'. The instant of non-existence must be different from the instant of existence, necessitating multiple instants.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The instant exists as a real temporal atom similar to a point on a line.
Correction: The instant, as a boundary, has no reality in external existence except as an estimation, and treating it as real leads to paradoxes like infinite regress.

Checkpoint

B) The non-existence of the instant would require another instant, leading to infinite succession

D) The instant is not a boundary

Key Takeaway
The instant as a temporal boundary cannot exist as a real entity without generating logical paradoxes, particularly infinite regress.

?
The infinite regress argument reveals that every moment passes away and yet cannot be captured. In light of this, how should you live knowing that each instant is slipping away without any enduring substance? What change in your priorities does this necessitate today?

The Existence of the 'Now' and the Problem of Successive Now's
Sanusi examines the existence of the 'now' and refutes an objection regarding successive now's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for the existence of the 'now' (al-an) as a boundary of time, the objection that its existence leads to a succession of now's, and the author's resolution.

Core Idea
The 'now' must exist, but its non-existence would require another now, causing a succession.

This passage tackles a subtle issue in the philosophy of time: does the 'now' (al-an) actually exist as a boundary between past and future? Sanusi examines an objection that if the now exists, its ceasing to exist would have to take place in another now, leading to an infinite regress. He then shows why this objection fails, affirming the reality of the now as a necessary concept.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two ways something can occur in time
The author distinguishes between gradual occurrence (like movement) and instantaneous occurrence spanning the continuity of now's. Read this carefully.
Notice that gradual occurrence requires continuity, while instantaneous occurs at each now.
2
Identify the division of not-present-in-now
The passage divides things that are not present in the extreme now, like the midpoint of a distance.
This division sets up the need for the now as an endpoint.
3
Grasp the objection to the existence of the now
If the now exists, its non-existence would have to occur in another now, leading to tatali al-anayn (succession of two now's).
This is the core problem raised by opponents.
4
Examine the response and its refutation
The response that non-existence occurs in all subsequent time 'not gradually' does not resolve the problem because the discussion is about the coming-to-be of non-existence in a distinct instant.
The author argues that the response misses the point about origination (huduth).
5
Conclude that the now exists as a boundary
Despite the objection, the author maintains that the now must exist as a boundary, just as a point is necessary for a line.
This reinforces the reality of the now in kalam.

For often one asks about it with 'when' may occur in the now, like reaching the midpoint of a distance by a mile. And occurrence in time may be by the thing having a continuity that coincides with time and cannot be realized except in it, and that is the meaning of gradual attainment, such as movements and what follows them like sounds. And it may be in the sense that there is no 'now' in that time except that thing is present in it, so its attainment is instantaneous but upon the continuity of 'now's. And it divides into what is not present in the now which is the extremity of its attainment of being, and into what is not present in that like the middle, meaning the thing in motion being on a distance between its two extremities. He said: (This is a declaration) by them of the existence of the now, even though it cannot be conceived except by the interruption of time; for if it existed, the coming-to-be of its non-existence would not be in a now, and the succession of two now's would follow. And what is said that it is in time but not gradually does not benefit. It is clear. He means that what they mentioned of the occurrence of some things in the now which is the extremity of time, like a point for a line, indicates that it exists because it is impossible for a thing to occur in what has no existence. But (the issue) is that there is no realization of the extremity of a thing in the external except after its interruption and its division in actuality, and time is only divided by imagination and assumption only. Also, if the now existed, and there is no doubt that it is upon passing away, not remaining, then the coming-to-be of its non-existence would only be in a now, leading to the succession of two now's. And their answer that its non-existence is in all the time after existence, but not gradually, so that the now becomes temporal, but in the sense that there is no now in that time except that non-existence is present in it as passed, does not remove the difficulty, because the discussion is about the coming-to-be of non-existence, and that this now is different from the now of existence is necessary.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion about the existence of the 'now' (al-an) as a boundary of time. The author begins by noting that one often asks about when something occurs 'in the now', giving the example of reaching the midpoint of a distance. He then distinguishes two ways something can occur in time: gradual (like movements) and instantaneous but across a continuum of 'now's. He then presents a division of things that are not present in the 'now' which is the endpoint. Next, he reports a statement by some scholars declaring the existence of the now, and then raises an objection: if the now exists, its non-existence would have to come about in another now, leading to a succession of 'now's, which is problematic. The author then explains that the objection is based on the fact that the now cannot be conceived except by cutting time, and that if it existed, its non-existence would not occur in a 'now'. He mentions a response that the non-existence occurs in the whole subsequent time but not gradually, but he argues this does not resolve the problem because the discussion concerns the coming-to-be of non-existence in an instant distinct from the instant of existence. He concludes that it is necessary that this now is different from the now of existence, thereby reaffirming the existence of the now as a boundary.

Real and Non-Real 'When' (al-Mata)
The author defines real and non-real 'when' and notes that the real permits sharing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real and non-real 'when' and why the real 'when' permits multiple things to share the same time.

Core Idea
Real 'when' is precise and shared; non-real 'when' is broader and non-specific.

Just as 'where' (al-ayn) has real and non-real types, 'when' (al-mata) also falls into two categories. Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing temporal attributes in kalam. This passage clarifies how time can be specific or general, and why precise time can be shared by multiple events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the comparison with 'where' and the example of an eclipse.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries meaning.
2
Identify the two types of 'when'
Find the definitions of real and non-real 'when' and the example given for each.
Real 'when' is a specific hour; non-real is a day or month.
3
Understand the sharing rule
Note that real 'when' allows multiple things to happen at the same time, unlike 'where' which does not. Ask yourself why this is important for theology.
This relates to the possibility of multiple events co-existing in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ثم المتى كالأين حقيقي وغير حقيقي إلا أن الحقيقي منه لا يمنع اشتراك الكثير فيه . وهو كون الشيء في زمان لا يفضل عليه ككون الكسوف في ساعة معينة وغير حقيقي وهو بخلافه ككون الكسوف في يوم كذا أو شهر كذا إلا أن الحقيقي من التي يجوز فيه الاشتراك بأن يتصف أشياء كثيرة بالكون في زمان معين بخلاف الأين وهو ظاهر.
He said: Then 'when' (al-mata) is like 'where' (al-ayn): real and non-real, except that the real one does not prevent the sharing of many in it. It is the thing being in a time that does not exceed it, like the eclipse being in a specific hour. And non-real is its opposite, like the eclipse being on such and such a day or such and such a month. Except that the real one is that in which sharing is allowed, in that many things can be described as being in a specific time, unlike 'where', and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'when' (al-mata), comparing it to 'where' (al-ayn). The author states that 'when' is of two types: real (haqiqi) and non-real (ghayr haqiqi). The real 'when' is a specific time that does not exceed the thing, like an eclipse occurring at a precise hour. The non-real 'when' is more general, like the eclipse occurring on a particular day or month. Importantly, the real 'when' allows multiple things to share the same time, just as many events can occur at the same hour. This is different from 'where', which does not allow such sharing (presumably because two bodies cannot occupy the same place at the same time). The author concludes that this is obvious.

Key Terms
المتى
الأين
حقيقي
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'when' is only one type, or that all 'when' allows sharing equally.
Correction: The author distinguishes real and non-real 'when'. Only real 'when' (a precise time) permits multiple things to share it; non-real 'when' (a broader period) does not imply such precise sharing.

Checkpoint

B) Real 'when' is a specific time that does not exceed the thing, non-real is more general.

D) Real 'when' is a point, non-real is a duration.

Key Takeaway
Real 'when' is a precise time that can be shared by multiple events, unlike non-real 'when' which is more general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Position (al-Wad')
Definition of position with its types and properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'position' (al-wad') as a metaphysical category involving both internal and external relations, and its key properties such as potential/actual, natural/positional, and opposition.

Core Idea
Position is the relation of a body's parts to each other and to external things.

In this passage, the author introduces the category of 'position' (al-wad'), which is one of the nine accidents in Aristotelian metaphysics. He defines it not merely as spatial location, but as a relation that involves both the internal arrangement of a body's parts and their orientation relative to external objects. He then distinguishes various aspects of position, such as potential vs. actual, natural vs. unnatural, and notes that positions can be opposed to each other, like standing and inversion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the defining phrase: 'كون الجسم بحيث يكون لأجزائه نسبة فيما بينهما، وإلى الأمور الخارجة عنها'.
The definition has two parts: internal relation and external relation.
2
Understand the two kinds of relations
Identify that position involves both the relation of parts to each other and the relation to external things (surrounding, surrounded, etc.).
3
Examine the subtypes: potential/actual and natural/positional
The passage lists that position can be potential or actual, and each can be natural (like standing) or unnatural (like inversion).
4
See the example of standing and inversion
Standing and inversion are given as opposed positions. Consider how this illustrates the concept of opposition in positions.
5
Apply the concept to encompassing bodies
The passage distinguishes between the absolutely encompassing (sphere), absolutely encompassed, and both. Relate this to the earlier definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها الوضع وهو كون الجسم بحيث يكون لأجزائه نسبة فيما بينهما، وإلى الأمور الخارجة عنها محيطة أو محاطة أو غيرها، ويكون بالقوة وبالفعل، وطبعاً ووضعاً ويقبل التضاد كالقيام والانعكاس والاشتداد كالأشد انتصاباً. هيئة تعرض للجسم باعتبار نسبة أجزائه بعضها إلى البعض بحيث تتخالف الأجزاء لأجلها بالقياس إلى الجهات في الموازاة والانحراف ونسبته أجزائه إلى أشياء غير ذلك الجسم خارجة عنه أو داخلة فيه كالقيام فإنه هيئة للإنسان بحسب انتصابه، وهو نسبة فيما بين أجزائه، وبحسب كون رأسه من فوق، ورجله من تحت، ولهذا يصير الانتكاس وضعاً آخر، فالمحيط على الإطلاق يكون له الوضع بحسب الأمور الداخلة فقط، والمحاط على الإطلاق بالعكس، وما هو هو محيط ومحاط بالاعتبارين وحصول الوضع للجسم قد يكون بالقوة، وقد يكون بالفعل، وكل منهما قد يكون بالطبع كقيام الإنسان ولا بالطبع كانتكاسه، ويجري فيه التضاد فإن القيام والانتكاس وجوديان يتعاقبان على موضوع واحد بينهما
He said: And among them is position (al-wad'), which is the body being such that its parts have a relation between them and to external things, surrounding or surrounded or otherwise. And it is potential and actual, natural and positional, and it admits opposition like standing and inversion and intensification like being more erect. It is a state that occurs to the body by virtue of the relation of its parts to each other such that the parts differ because of it with respect to directions in parallelism and deviation, and its relating its parts to things other than that body, external to it or internal to it, like standing, for it is a state of the human in terms of his erectness, and it is a relation among his parts, and in terms of his head being above and his leg being below. For this reason, inversion becomes a different position. So the absolutely encompassing has position only in terms of internal matters, and the absolutely encompassed has the opposite, and what is encompassing and encompassed in both respects. And the occurrence of position for the body may be potential or actual, and each of them may be natural, like a human's standing, or not natural, like his inversion. And opposition applies to it, for standing and inversion are two existent states that succeed each other on one subject, between them (opposition).
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'position' (al-wad') in Aristotelian/Kalam metaphysics. The author begins by stating that position is the body being such that its parts have a relation (nusba) to each other and to external things, whether surrounding or surrounded. Position can be potential or actual, natural or positional (i.e., acquired). It also admits of opposition, such as standing vs. inversion, and intensification, like being more or less erect. He then elaborates: position is a state (hay'a) that occurs to the body due to the relation of its parts to each other, causing the parts to differ in orientation (e.g., parallel or deviant). It also involves the relation of parts to external or internal things, using the example of standing: it is a state of the human based on erectness, with head above and legs below. Inversion (e.g., handstand) is a different position. The author then notes that a wholly encompassing body (like a sphere) has position only with respect to internal parts, while a wholly encompassed body has position with respect to external surroundings. Something that is both encompassing and encompassed has position in both ways. Position can be potential (like a standing posture possible) or actual, and each can be natural (like standing upright) or unnatural (like inversion). Finally, opposition applies: standing and inversion are two existent states that alternate on the same subject, implying they are contraries.

Correction: Position involves both the internal arrangement of parts and their relation to external things, as defined.

The Category of When (Mata): Real and Non-Real Types
The author defines and explains the category of 'when' (mata) with its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real and non-real types of 'when' (mata) in Islamic philosophy and why real 'when' allows for multiple events to share the same moment.

Core Idea
Real 'when' is a specific moment that many events can share; non-real 'when' is a broader time range.

In this passage, the author continues the discussion of categories (maqulat), focusing on 'when' (mata). Just as 'where' (ayn) has real and non-real types, so does 'when'. The key insight is that real 'when'—like an exact hour—can be common to many events, unlike real 'where', which cannot be shared. Let's explore this distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the analogy to 'where' (al-ayn).
2
Identify the two types of 'when'
The passage presents real (haqiqi) and non-real (ghair haqiqi) 'when'. Find how each is defined and what example is given for each.
The real type is a specific moment like an hour; the non-real is broader like a day or month.
3
Compare with 'where'
The author contrasts real 'when' with real 'where'. Note that real 'when' allows sharing (ishtirak) but real 'where' does not. Reflect on why that might be.
Multiple events can occur at the same time, but two objects cannot occupy the same place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ثم المتى كالأين حقيقي. وغير حقيقي إلا أن الحقيقي منه لا يمنع اشتراك الكثير فيه . وهو كون الشيء في زمان لا يفضل عليه ككون الكسوف في ساعة معينة وغير حقيقي وهو بخلافة ككون الكسوف في يوم كذا أو شهر كذا إلا أن الحقيقي من التي يجوز فيه الاشتراك بأن يتصف أشياء كثيرة بالكون في زمان معين بخلاف الأين وهو ظاهر.
He said: Then al-mata (when) is like al-ayn (where) in being real and non-real, except that the real does not prevent multiple things sharing in it. It is the existence of a thing in a time that does not exceed it, like the occurrence of an eclipse at a specific hour. The non-real is its opposite, like the occurrence of an eclipse on such a day or such a month, except that the real kind allows sharing in that many things can be described as existing at a specific time, unlike al-ayn (where) and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'when' (mata) as one of the categories (maqulat). The author states that it, like 'where' (ayn), has both a real (haqiqi) and a non-real (ghair haqiqi) type. The real type does not prevent multiple things sharing in it—meaning multiple events can occur at the same exact time, for example an eclipse at a specific hour. The non-real type is the opposite, like an eclipse occurring on a certain day or month, which is a broader time frame. The key point is that the real 'when' allows sharing (ishtirak) in that many things can be described as being at the same specific time, unlike 'where' which does not allow multiple things to occupy the same place. The author ends by saying this is apparent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The real 'when' allows many events to share the same moment. But in your life, do you truly share your time with Allah, or do you crowd your moments with distractions? If you knew you had one hour left in this world, how differently would you spend it right now?
?
Just as the real 'where' cannot be shared, your death has a specific 'when' that is yours alone. Are you living as if that 'when' is already real and imminent? What is the one thing you would change if you knew it would happen in the next hour?

Defining Position (Wad') in Kalam
The author defines position (wad') and explains its aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of position (wad') as an accidental quality involving the relation of a body's parts to each other and to external things, and its various aspects: potential/actual, natural/unnatural, opposition, and intensity.

Core Idea
Position is not just location—it's the arrangement of parts relative to each other and external references.

In Aristotelian physics and kalam, position (wad') is one of the nine accidental categories. This passage from the Sharh al-Maqasid defines it precisely, distinguishing it from mere location. The author explains that position involves relations among a body's parts and to external things, and that it can be potential or actual, natural or unnatural, admits opposites like standing and inversion, and varies in intensity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words establish the definition before you interpret.
Notice the dense style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the core definition
Find the sentence that defines position (wad') as a state of a body with respect to the relation of its parts. Understand that it is not just location but a relational quality.
3
List the classifications
The passage presents several dichotomies: potential/actual, natural/unnatural, and the existence of opposition and intensity. Note each one and how they are exemplified.
Use the example of standing and inversion to understand opposition.
4
Reflect on the encompassing/encompassed distinction
Pay attention to the special case of bodies that encompass or are encompassed. This nuance shows that position can depend on internal or external relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها الوضع وهو (١) كون الجسم بحيث يكون لأجزائه نسبة فيما بينهما، وإلى الأمور الخارجة عنها محيطة أو محاطة أو غيرها، ويكون بالقوة وبالفعل، وطبعاً ووضعاً (۲) ويقبل التضاد كالقيام والانعكاس والاشتداد كالأشد انتصاباً) . هيئة تعرض للجسم باعتبار نسبة أجزائه بعضها إلى البعض بحيث تتخالف الأجزاء لأجلها بالقياس إلى الجهات في الموازاة والانحراف ونسبته أجزائه إلى أشياء (۳) غير ذلك الجسم خارجة عنه أو داخلة فيه كالقيام فإنه هيئة للإنسان بحسب انتصابه، وهو نسبة فيما بين أجزائه، وبحسب كون رأسه من فوق، ورجله من تحت، ولهذا يصير الانتكاس وضعاً (٤) آخر، فالمحيط على الإطلاق يكون له الوضع بحسب الأمور الداخلة فقط، والمحاط على الإطلاق بالعكس، وما هو هو محيط ومحاط بالاعتبارين وحصول الوضع للجسم قد يكون بالقوة، وقد يكون بالفعل، وكل منهما قد يكون بالطبع كقيام الإنسان ولا بالطبع كانتكاسه، ويجري فيه التضاد فإن القيام والانتكاس وجوديان يتعاقبان على موضوع واحد (٥) بينهما غاية الخلاف ويقبل الشدة والضعف على ما هو ظاهر في كل من الانتصاب والركوع .
He said: And among them is position (wad‘), which is the state of a body such that its parts have a relation among themselves and to external things surrounding or surrounded or other, and it is by potency and by act, by nature and by position, and it admits opposition like standing and inversion and intensity like more erect. It is an accidental quality that occurs to a body in consideration of the relation of its parts to each other such that the parts differ on account of it in relation to directions in parallelism and deviation, and the relation of its parts to things other than that body, external to it or internal to it, like standing: it is a state of a human in terms of his uprightness, and it is a relation among his parts, and in terms of his head being above and his leg below; because of this, inversion becomes another position. The absolutely encompassing has position only by internal matters; the absolutely encompassed is the opposite; and what is both encompassing and encompassed in both respects. The occurrence of position for a body may be by potency and may be by act, and each of them may be by nature like the standing of a human, or not by nature like his inversion. Opposition applies to it: standing and inversion are two existent states that succeed each other on one subject, between them maximal difference, and it admits intensity and weakness as is apparent in each of uprightness and bowing.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage defines the category of position (wad‘). The author first gives a concise definition: position is the state of a body such that its parts have a relation among themselves and to external things (surrounding or surrounded). It can be potential or actual, natural or positional. It admits opposition (like standing and inversion) and intensity (like being more erect). Then he expands: position is an accidental quality (hay’a) that occurs to a body based on the relation of its parts to each other — this causes the parts to differ in relation to directions like parallelism and deviation — and also based on the relation of its parts to things outside or inside the body. Example: standing is a state of a human due to uprightness, involving a relation among parts (head above, leg below). Inversion is a different position. He then notes that a body that is fully encompassing (like a container) has position only by internal matters; a body that is fully encompassed has position only by external; a body that is both is considered from both aspects. The occurrence of position can be potential (bil-quwwa) or actual (bil-fi‘l), and each can be natural (like standing) or not natural (like inversion). Opposition applies because standing and inversion are two existent states that alternate on the same subject, with maximal difference between them. Also, position admits intensity and weakness, as in uprightness and bowing.

Correction: Position (wad') is not just location; it is the arrangement of a body's parts relative to each other and to external references, involving relations and directionality.

Key Takeaway

The Category of Possession: Definition and Types
Al-Sanusi defines possession and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the category of possession (lahu) as a relation of a body to something containing it, and its distinction into essential and accidental possession.

Core Idea
Possession is a relation of containment, not ownership.

Before addressing Ibn Sina's critique, Al-Sanusi defines the category of possession (lahu) as a relation between a body and something that contains it. He distinguishes between essential possession, where the container is naturally attached, and accidental possession, where the container is separate. This foundational definition is crucial for understanding the metaphysical category.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of possession
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that possession is defined as the relation of a body to something containing it or part of it, moving with its movement.
The term 'lahu' is also called 'al-milk' and 'al-jidda'.
2
Distinguish essential vs accidental
Identify the two types: essential (like skin moves with animal) and accidental (like clothes move with human). Notice the examples.
Essential possession is inherent; accidental is temporary.
3
Note the polysemous uses
The term 'lahu' is also used for other relations, like powers to the soul or a horse to its owner. This polysemy is important for Ibn Sina's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها له ويسمى الملك والجدة وهو نسبة الجسم إلى حاصر له أو لبعضه ينتقل بانتقاله ذاتياً كالحيوان في إهابه أو عرضياً كالإنسان في ثيابه ، ويقال بالاشتراك بمثل نسبة القوى إلى النفس والفرس إلى زيد وتردد ابن سينا في كون هذه المقولة جنساً برأسه). ويفسر بالنسبة الحاصلة للجسم إلى أمر حاصر له أو لبعضه فينتقل بانتقاله، كالتقمص والتختم (١) ، ويكون ذاتياً كنسبة الهرة (۲) إلى إهابها، وعرضياً كنسبة الإنسان إلى قميصه . وقد يقال بحسب الاشتراك لنسبة الشيء إلى الشيء واختصاص له به من جهة استعماله إياه، وتصرفه فيه، ككون القوى للنفس، والفرس لزيد . وقال ابن سينا : أما أنا فلا أعرف هذه المقولة حق المعرفة، لأن قولنا له كم أوله كيف أوله مضاف كقولك له أبن أوله جوهر حاصر كله كما في له ثوب أو لبعضه كما في له (۳) خاتم أو محصور فيه، كما في قولنا للدن (٤) شراب يقع عليها لفظة له لا بالتواطؤ لكن بالتشابه والتشكيك وإن احتيل حتى يقال إن مقولة له يدل على نسبة الجسم إلى شامل إياه ينتقل بانتقاله كالتقمص والتسلح والتنعل، لم يكن لهذا المعنى من القدر في عداد المقولات وإن كان التشكيك يزول.
He said: And among them is possession (lahu), also called property (al-milk) and having (al-jidda), which is the relation of a body to something containing it or part of it, moving with its movement, either essentially like an animal in its skin, or accidentally like a human in his clothes. It is said by polysemy like the relation of powers to the soul and a horse to Zayd. Ibn Sina was hesitant about whether this category is a genus by itself. It is interpreted as the relation occurring to a body to something containing it or part of it, moving with its movement, like wearing a shirt and putting on a ring. It may be essential like the relation of a cat to its skin, or accidental like the relation of a human to his shirt. It may be said by polysemy for the relation of a thing to a thing and its special connection by virtue of its use and disposal, like the powers being for the soul, and the horse for Zayd. Ibn Sina said: As for me, I do not know this category truly, because our saying 'for him' is like 'how' and 'why' and 'where' — it is a relation, as you say 'for him is a house' — it is a substance containing all of it, as in 'he has a garment', or part of it, as in 'he has a ring', or something contained in it, as in our saying 'the jar has wine'. The word 'lahu' applies not by univocity but by similarity and equivocality. Even if one contrives to say that the category of possession indicates the relation of a body to something encompassing it that moves with its movement like wearing a shirt, arming, and putting on shoes, this meaning would not have sufficient standing among the categories, even if the equivocality is removed.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage discusses the category of possession (lahu), also called property (al-milk) and having (al-jidda). The author defines it as the relation of a body to something containing it or part of it, where the container moves with the body's movement. This can be essential (like an animal's skin) or accidental (like a human's clothes). The term is also used by polysemy (ishtirak) for relations like powers to the soul or a horse to its owner. The author then provides an expanded interpretation: it is a relation to something containing the body or part of it, moving with it (e.g., wearing a shirt, putting on a ring). It can be essential (cat's skin) or accidental (human's shirt). Then he quotes Ibn Sina, who expresses doubt about this category. Ibn Sina says he does not truly know this category because expressions like 'for him' (lahu) are like 'how' and 'why' and 'where'—they involve relation. Examples: 'he has a garment' (substance containing all), 'he has a ring' (part), 'the jar has wine' (contained). The word 'lahu' applies not by univocity but by similarity and equivocality (tashbih wa tashkik). Even if one tries to define it strictly as a relation to something encompassing that moves with the body (like wearing, arming, shoeing), this meaning would not have enough weight to be counted among the categories, even if the equivocality is removed. Thus, Ibn Sina is skeptical about the status of this category.

Key Terms
له
الملك
الجدة
الإضافة
الاشتراك
ابن سينا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of essential possession according to the passage?
A) A man in his shirt
B) An animal in its skin
C) A horse owned by Zayd
D) A person wearing a ring

Key Takeaway
Possession (lahu) is a metaphysical relation of a body to something that contains it and moves with it, divided into essential and accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the things that 'contain' you—your body in its clothes, your soul in your body. Which of these containments are essential (like skin) and which are accidental (like a garment)? What does that distinction reveal about what you truly are?
?
The passage speaks of possession as a relation of use and disposal (like a horse to Zayd). What do you own that you treat as merely a tool for your use? Has that relation ever deceived you into thinking that use is the same as possession in the essential sense?
?
Ibn Sina hesitated about whether possession is its own category. What does that hesitation say about how we often take simple concepts for granted? Where in your own life have you assumed something is a fundamental category when it might be derivative?

Ibn Sina's Doubt About the Category of Possession
Ibn Sina expresses skepticism about possession as a category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's critique that the category of possession is not a genuine category because the term 'lahu' is equivocal and even if defined strictly, it lacks sufficient significance.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina doubted possession because it is relational and equivocal.

After presenting the definition of possession, Al-Sanusi quotes Ibn Sina's famous doubt. Ibn Sina argues that the word 'lahu' is used in many ways and does not have a clear univocal meaning. Even if one restricts it to the relation of a containing body moving with the body, the concept is not significant enough to stand as a separate category. This critique challenges the very status of possession among the categories.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's statement
Read the Arabic passage where Ibn Sina expresses his doubt. Notice his opening: 'As for me, I do not know this category truly.'
Ibn Sina is skeptical, not denying outright.
2
Understand his argument on equivocality
Ibn Sina points out that 'lahu' is like relational words such as 'how', 'why', 'where', and it applies to different types of relations (garment, ring, jar). He says it is used by similarity and equivocality, not univocity.
Equivocality (tashkik) means the term is applied in varying senses.
3
Analyze the conclusion
Even if one restricts the meaning to a body encompassing container moving with it, Ibn Sina says this meaning is not weighty enough to be a separate category. Thus, he doubts the category's legitimacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها له ويسمى الملك والجدة وهو نسبة الجسم إلى حاصر له أو لبعضه ينتقل بانتقاله ذاتياً كالحيوان في إهابه أو عرضياً كالإنسان في ثيابه ، ويقال بالاشتراك بمثل نسبة القوى إلى النفس والفرس إلى زيد وتردد ابن سينا في كون هذه المقولة جنساً برأسه). ويفسر بالنسبة الحاصلة للجسم إلى أمر حاصر له أو لبعضه فينتقل بانتقاله، كالتقمص والتختم (١) ، ويكون ذاتياً كنسبة الهرة (۲) إلى إهابها، وعرضياً كنسبة الإنسان إلى قميصه . وقد يقال بحسب الاشتراك لنسبة الشيء إلى الشيء واختصاص له به من جهة استعماله إياه، وتصرفه فيه، ككون القوى للنفس، والفرس لزيد . وقال ابن سينا : أما أنا فلا أعرف هذه المقولة حق المعرفة، لأن قولنا له كم أوله كيف أوله مضاف كقولك له أبن أوله جوهر حاصر كله كما في له ثوب أو لبعضه كما في له (۳) خاتم أو محصور فيه، كما في قولنا للدن (٤) شراب يقع عليها لفظة له لا بالتواطؤ لكن بالتشابه والتشكيك وإن احتيل حتى يقال إن مقولة له يدل على نسبة الجسم إلى شامل إياه ينتقل بانتقاله كالتقمص والتسلح والتنعل، لم يكن لهذا المعنى من القدر في عداد المقولات وإن كان التشكيك يزول.
He said: And among them is possession (lahu), also called property (al-milk) and having (al-jidda), which is the relation of a body to something containing it or part of it, moving with its movement, either essentially like an animal in its skin, or accidentally like a human in his clothes. It is said by polysemy like the relation of powers to the soul and a horse to Zayd. Ibn Sina was hesitant about whether this category is a genus by itself. It is interpreted as the relation occurring to a body to something containing it or part of it, moving with its movement, like wearing a shirt and putting on a ring. It may be essential like the relation of a cat to its skin, or accidental like the relation of a human to his shirt. It may be said by polysemy for the relation of a thing to a thing and its special connection by virtue of its use and disposal, like the powers being for the soul, and the horse for Zayd. Ibn Sina said: As for me, I do not know this category truly, because our saying 'for him' is like 'how' and 'why' and 'where' — it is a relation, as you say 'for him is a house' — it is a substance containing all of it, as in 'he has a garment', or part of it, as in 'he has a ring', or something contained in it, as in our saying 'the jar has wine'. The word 'lahu' applies not by univocity but by similarity and equivocality. Even if one contrives to say that the category of possession indicates the relation of a body to something encompassing it that moves with its movement like wearing a shirt, arming, and putting on shoes, this meaning would not have sufficient standing among the categories, even if the equivocality is removed.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage discusses the category of possession (lahu), also called property (al-milk) and having (al-jidda). The author defines it as the relation of a body to something containing it or part of it, where the container moves with the body's movement. This can be essential (like an animal's skin) or accidental (like a human's clothes). The term is also used by polysemy (ishtirak) for relations like powers to the soul or a horse to its owner. The author then provides an expanded interpretation: it is a relation to something containing the body or part of it, moving with it (e.g., wearing a shirt, putting on a ring). It can be essential (cat's skin) or accidental (human's shirt). Then he quotes Ibn Sina, who expresses doubt about this category. Ibn Sina says he does not truly know this category because expressions like 'for him' (lahu) are like 'how' and 'why' and 'where'—they involve relation. Examples: 'he has a garment' (substance containing all), 'he has a ring' (part), 'the jar has wine' (contained). The word 'lahu' applies not by univocity but by similarity and equivocality (tashbih wa tashkik). Even if one tries to define it strictly as a relation to something encompassing that moves with the body (like wearing, arming, shoeing), this meaning would not have enough weight to be counted among the categories, even if the equivocality is removed. Thus, Ibn Sina is skeptical about the status of this category.

Understanding Action and Passion in Kalam
Definition of action and passion as relational influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam distinction between action (an yaf'ala) and passion (an yanfa'ila) as ongoing relational influence, not merely the resulting state.

Core Idea
Action and passion are dynamic connections, not static results.

In kalam theology, understanding the categories of action and passion is crucial for discussing divine attributes and causality. This passage from the author briefly defines these terms, emphasizing that they refer to the ongoing process of influence as long as the connection lasts, not the state that follows. Let's dive into the author's precise wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms and the example of the heater.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the Arabic expressions 'an yaf'ala' and 'an yanfa'ila'. Understand that they denote active and passive influence respectively.
These are technical terms in kalam.
3
Understand the distinction
The author contrasts the ongoing process (as long as connection lasts) with the resulting state ('al-hal al-hasila'). Reflect on why this distinction matters.
The heater example illustrates the point.
4
Apply the concept
Think of other examples of action and passion in everyday life, such as teaching and learning, where the process is distinct from the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها أن يفعل وأن ينفعل وهو تأثير شيء في شيء، وتأثير شيء عن شيء ما دام سالكاً على اتصال كالذي للمسخن والمتسخن ما دام يسخن ويتسخن ، لا الحال الحاصله)
He said: And among them is that it acts and that it is acted upon, which is the effect of a thing on a thing, and the effect of a thing from a thing as long as it continues in connection, like that of the heater and the heated as long as it heats and is heated, not the acquired state.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage briefly defines the two categories of action (an yaf'ala) and passion (an yanfa'ila). The author says they are the effect (ta'thir) of one thing on another, and the effect of one thing from another, as long as the connection (ittisal) continues. The example given is the heater (musakhkhin) and the heated (mutasakhkhin) as long as the heating is occurring. The focus is on the ongoing process, not the resulting state (al-hal al-hasila). So action and passion are dynamic relations while the agent and patient are in contact.

Definition of the Category of Possession and Ibn Sina's Doubt
Defines category of possession and presents Ibn Sina's doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the category of possession (lahu) and why Ibn Sina questioned whether it is a genuine category.

Core Idea
Lahu is a complex category that may be ambiguous, not a simple univocal concept.

In this passage, the author defines one of the ten Aristotelian categories: possession (lahu), also called ownership. He distinguishes essential vs. accidental possession and notes that the term is used by homonymy. Then he presents Ibn Sina's critical doubt: the expression 'he has' is ambiguous and does not form a single clear category. This lesson explores both the definition and the skeptical challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Notice the technical terms like 'lahu', 'milk', 'jidda'.
2
Identify the two types of possession
Find the distinction between essential possession (like animal in its skin) and accidental possession (like a person in clothes). Understand the difference.
Essential means the covering is inherent; accidental means it is acquired.
3
Understand the homonymous use
The passage says 'lahu' is also used by homonymy for relations like powers to the soul or a horse to Zayd. This shows the term has multiple meanings.
4
Analyze Ibn Sina's doubt
Read Ibn Sina's statement carefully. He says he doesn't truly know this category because 'lahu' can be used in many ways ('how much', 'how', etc.) and is applied by resemblance and equivocity, not univocity.
This challenges whether 'lahu' deserves to be a category at all.
5
Consider the resolution attempt
The passage ends with an attempt to narrow the definition but Ibn Sina argues even that meaning lacks sufficient status among categories. Reflect on the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها له ويسمى الملك والجدة وهو نسبة الجسم إلى حاصر له أو لبعضه ينتقل بانتقاله ذاتياً كالحيوان في إهابه أو عرضياً كالإنسان في ثيابه ، ويقال بالاشتراك بمثل نسبة القوى إلى النفس والفرس إلى زيد وتردد ابن سينا في كون هذه المقولة جنساً برأسه). ويفسر بالنسبة الحاصلة للجسم إلى أمر حاصر له أو لبعضه فينتقل بانتقاله، كالتقمص والتختم ، ويكون ذاتياً كنسبة الهرة إلى إهابها، وعرضياً كنسبة الإنسان إلى قميصه . وقد يقال بحسب الاشتراك لنسبة الشيء إلى الشيء واختصاص له به من جهة استعماله إياه، وتصرفه فيه، ككون القوى للنفس، والفرس لزيد . وقال ابن سينا : أما أنا فلا أعرف هذه المقولة حق المعرفة، لأن قولنا له كم أوله كيف أوله مضاف كقولك له أبن أوله جوهر حاصر كله كما في له ثوب أو لبعضه كما في له خاتم أو محصور فيه، كما في قولنا للدن شراب يقع عليها لفظة له لا بالتواطؤ لكن بالتشابه والتشكيك وإن احتيل حتى يقال إن مقولة له يدل على نسبة الجسم إلى شامل إياه ينتقل بانتقاله كالتقمص والتسلح والتنعل، لم يكن لهذا المعنى من القدر في عداد المقولات وإن كان التشكيك يزول.
He said: Among them is 'having' (lahu), also called 'ownership' (al-milk) and 'possession' (al-jidda). It is the relation of a body to something that encloses it or part of it, moving with its movement, either essentially (dhātiyyan) like the animal in its skin, or accidentally (ʿaraḍiyyan) like a human in his clothes. It is said by homonymy (ishtirāk) like the relation of powers to the soul and a horse to Zayd. Ibn Sina hesitated whether this category is a genus by itself. It is interpreted as the relation obtained for a body to something enclosing it or part of it, so that it moves with its movement, like wearing a shirt (taqammuṣ) and wearing a ring (takhattum). It is essential like the relation of a cat to its skin, and accidental like the relation of a human to his shirt. It may be said by homonymy for the relation of one thing to another and its being specific to it due to its use and disposal of it, like the powers belonging to the soul and the horse to Zayd. Ibn Sina said: As for me, I do not truly know this category, because our saying 'he has' is either 'how much' or 'how' or 'what is added' like your saying 'he has where is its first?' or 'he has a substance enclosing all of it' as in 'he has a garment' or 'part of it' as in 'he has a ring', or 'enclosed in it' as in our saying 'the wine container has a drink'. The word 'lahu' is applied to it not by univocity (tawāṭuʾ) but by resemblance (tashābuh) and equivocity (tashkīk). Even if one tries until it is said that the category 'having' indicates the relation of a body to what includes it, moving with its movement, like wearing a shirt, arming, and wearing sandals, this meaning would not have sufficient status among the categories, even though equivocity is removed.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage defines the category of possession (lahu), also called ownership (al-milk) and possession (al-jidda). It is one of the ten Aristotelian categories. The author explains that it is a relation of a body to something that encloses it (or part of it) and moves with it. This relation can be essential (dhāti), like the skin of an animal that moves with it, or accidental (ʿaraḍī), like a human's clothing that moves with the human. It is also used by homonymy (ishtirāk) to refer to other relations like the soul's powers or a horse's ownership. The passage then records Ibn Sina's hesitation about whether this is a true category. Ibn Sina argues that expressions like 'he has' are ambiguous and do not form a single unambiguous category; they are applied by resemblance and equivocity (tashkīk). He concludes that even if one tries to define it narrowly as a body's relation to something encompassing it that moves with it, this meaning does not deserve to be a category. The passage thus presents both the definition and a critical perspective on its status.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'lahu' is a simple, univocal category like substance or quantity.
Correction: The passage shows that 'lahu' is ambiguous; it can mean essential possession (skin), accidental possession (clothes), or homonymous relations (powers to soul). Ibn Sina argues it is not a true category due to equivocity.

Ibn Sina admitted he did not truly know this category. When was the last time you sincerely admitted doubt about something you thought you understood? ?

Definition and Characteristics of Action and Passion
Defines action and passion as continuous processes and discusses their characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the categories of action and passion refer to the continuous process of effecting and being affected, and they admit opposition and intensity.

Core Idea
Action and passion are about the ongoing doing and undergoing, not the static outcome.

Sanusi introduces two key categories used by philosophers and theologians: action (an yafʿala) and passion (an yanfaʿila). He explains they denote the dynamic relationship between agent and patient as long as the process continues. This clarification is essential to avoid confusing them with the resulting states. The passage also notes that these categories involve opposition and intensity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi defines action and passion as 'effect of something on something' while the connection persists.
Note the phrase 'ما دام سالكاً على اتصال' — as long as it continues in connection.
2
Distinguish from static results
The passage contrasts the ongoing process (e.g., heating) with the resulting state (e.g., heat). Understand that the latter belongs to other categories like quality.
Static states like height, heat, and standing are not action/passion.
3
Understand the rebuttal
Some argued that action/passion exist only mentally to avoid infinite regress. The author refutes this by clarifying that they refer to continuous process, not absolute causation.
This rebuttal secures the objective reality of these categories.
4
Note characteristics: opposition and intensity
The passage states that both categories admit opposition (heating vs. cooling) and intensity (fire heats more than a hot stone).
Think of examples from everyday experience.
5
Connect to al-Farabi's interpretation
Al-Farabi interpreted 'to act' as causing motion and 'to be acted upon' as being moved, reinforcing the process-oriented view.
This helps solidify the dynamic nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها أن يفعل وأن ينفعل وهو تأثير شيء في شيء، وتأثير شيء عن شيء ما دام سالكاً على اتصال كالذي للمسخن والمتسخن ما دام يسخن ويتسخن ، لا الحال الحاصله أي مثل بعد الاستقرار كطول الشجر ، وسخونة الماء، وقيام الإنسان، ويجري فيها التضاد والاشتداد . وما قيل إن ثبوتهما ذهني وإلا لزم التسلسل مدفوع بأن ليس المراد بهما مطلق التأثير والتأثر، بحيث يشمل الإبداع والحدوث الدفعي، بل الحال الذي يكون للفاعل والمنفعل حتى أن الفارابي فسر أن يفعل بالتحريك ، وأن ينفعل بالتحرك . هو تأثير الشيء في غيره على اتصال غير قار كالحال الذي للمتسخن ما دام يتسخن وإن ينفعل هو تأثير الشيء عن غيره كذلك الذي للمتسخن ما دام يتسخن وأما الحال الحاصل للمستكمل عند الاستقرار أي انقطاع الحركة عنه كالطول الحاصل للشجر، وكالسخونة الحاصلة للماء والاحتراق الحاصل للثوب ، والقعود أو القيام الحاصل للإنسان، فليس من هذا القبيل، وإن كان فقط أثراً أو انفعالاً، بل من الكم أو الكيف أو الوضع أو غير ذلك، وكذلك الحال الحاصل للفاعل قبل التأثير وبعده كقوة للنار تسمى احتراقاً، ويجري في كل من المقولتين التضاد فإن التسخين ضد التبريد والتسخن ضد التبرد، ويقبلان الشدة والضعف، فإن تسخين النار أشد من تسخين الحجر الحار، والاسوداد الذي هو الحركة إلى السواد منه ما هو أقرب إلى الإسوداد الذي هو الغاية في ذلك، وأسرع وصولاً إليه من اسوداد آخر إليه. وذهب الإمام وجمع من المحققين إلى أن ثبوت هاتين المقولتين إنما هو في الذهن، إذ لو وجدتا في الخارج لافتقر كل منهما إلى مؤثر له تأثير آخر ضرورة امتناع كون التأثير نفس الأثر على تقدير كونهما من الأعيان الخارجية، وحينئذ يلزم
He said: Among them are 'to act' (an yafʿala) and 'to be acted upon' (an yanfaʿila). They are the effect of something upon something, and the being-effected of something from something, as long as it continues in connection, like the state of the heater and the heated as long as it heats and is heated, not the state that results. That is, like after stability, like the height of a tree, the heat of water, and the standing of a human. Opposition and intensity occur in them. What was said that their existence is mental, otherwise infinite regress would follow, is refuted by the fact that what is meant by them is not absolute effectivity and passivity, such that it would include creation and instantaneous origination, but rather the state that belongs to the agent and patient, such that al-Farabi interpreted 'to act' as causing motion (taḥrīk) and 'to be acted upon' as being moved (taḥarruk). It is the effect of a thing upon another in continuous non-stationary connection, like the state of the heated as long as it is being heated. And 'to be acted upon' is the effect of a thing from another similarly, like that of the heated as long as it is being heated. As for the state obtained for the completed thing at stability, i.e., cessation of motion, like the height obtained for the tree, the heat obtained for water, the burning obtained for cloth, and the sitting or standing obtained for a human, it is not of this kind, even if it is only an effect or a passion; rather, it belongs to quantity, quality, position, or something else. Similarly the state obtained for the agent before and after the effect, like a power in fire called burning. In each of the two categories opposition occurs, for heating is opposite to cooling, and being heated is opposite to being cooled. They accept intensity and weakness, for the heating of fire is more intense than the heating of a hot stone; and blackening, which is the movement toward blackness, includes that which is closer to the blackness that is the goal thereof, and faster in reaching it, than another blackening toward it. The Imām (al-Ghazali) and a group of verifiers held that the existence of these two categories is only in the mind, because if they existed externally, each of them would need an effecter having another effect, due to the impossibility of the effect being the same as the effect itself assuming they are external entities; and then an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage defines the categories of action (an yafʿala) and passion (an yanfaʿila). It explains that they refer to the process of causing and receiving an effect while the action is ongoing, not the static state after completion. For example, heating and being heated are action and passion, but the resulting heat is a quality, not an action. The passage notes that these categories admit opposition (heating vs. cooling) and intensity (e.g., fire heats more than a hot stone). It then addresses an objection: some argued that these categories are only mental because if they were real, they would require an infinite regress of effects. The author refutes this by clarifying that the intended meaning is not absolute effectivity (which would include creation) but rather continuous process. Al-Farabi interpreted action as causing motion and passion as being moved. The passage then contrasts the ongoing state with the static state after completion (e.g., tree height, water heat, human standing), which belong to other categories. Finally, it reports that al-Ghazali (the Imām) and many verifiers held that these two categories exist only in the mind, not in external reality, because if they were external, each would need another effect, leading to infinite regress. The argument is cut off at 'then an infinite regress would follow' (يلزم).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Action and passion are the same as the resulting static states (e.g., heat is an action).
Correction: Action/passion refer to the ongoing process of causing/receiving an effect, not the state after stability.

Action and passion are categories that describe the continuous dynamic of causing and receiving an effect, not the resulting state.

?

Or has it become a set of finished results—prayers done, fasting completed? What will you do this week to turn one static duty into a dynamic process?
?
