The Mental Existence of Action and Passion
Presents al-Ghazali's argument that action and passion exist only mentally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that action and passion exist only in the mind, as held by al-Ghazali and others, and the infinite regress problem.

Core Idea
If action and passion were external, each would need another effect, leading to infinite regress.

After defining the categories, the passage presents a significant view held by al-Imam al-Ghazali and many verifiers: that the categories of action and passion exist only mentally. Their reasoning is that if these categories were external entities, each action would require a further effect, causing an infinite chain. This lesson examines that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim
Read the passage where al-Ghazali and verifiers assert that action and passion exist only in the mind (fi al-dhihn).
This is a different position from the definition previously given.
2
Understand the reasoning
The argument is: if they existed externally, each would need an effecter with another effect, since the effect cannot be the same as the effect itself. This leads to infinite regress.
Consider why this regress is problematic for real external existence.
3
Reflect on the consequence
The passage cuts off, but the implication is that infinite regress is impossible, so the categories must be mental.
Think about how this view contrasts with the earlier rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها أن يفعل وأن ينفعل وهو تأثير شيء في شيء، وتأثير شيء عن شيء ما دام سالكاً على اتصال كالذي للمسخن والمتسخن ما دام يسخن ويتسخن ، لا الحال الحاصله أي مثل بعد الاستقرار كطول الشجر ، وسخونة الماء، وقيام الإنسان، ويجري فيها التضاد والاشتداد . وما قيل إن ثبوتهما ذهني وإلا لزم التسلسل مدفوع بأن ليس المراد بهما مطلق التأثير والتأثر، بحيث يشمل الإبداع والحدوث الدفعي، بل الحال الذي يكون للفاعل والمنفعل حتى أن الفارابي فسر أن يفعل بالتحريك ، وأن ينفعل بالتحرك . هو تأثير الشيء في غيره على اتصال غير قار كالحال الذي للمتسخن ما دام يتسخن وإن ينفعل هو تأثير الشيء عن غيره كذلك الذي للمتسخن ما دام يتسخن وأما الحال الحاصل للمستكمل عند الاستقرار أي انقطاع الحركة عنه كالطول الحاصل للشجر، وكالسخونة الحاصلة للماء والاحتراق الحاصل للثوب ، والقعود أو القيام الحاصل للإنسان، فليس من هذا القبيل، وإن كان فقط أثراً أو انفعالاً، بل من الكم أو الكيف أو الوضع أو غير ذلك، وكذلك الحال الحاصل للفاعل قبل التأثير وبعده كقوة للنار تسمى احتراقاً، ويجري في كل من المقولتين التضاد فإن التسخين ضد التبريد والتسخن ضد التبرد، ويقبلان الشدة والضعف، فإن تسخين النار أشد من تسخين الحجر الحار، والاسوداد الذي هو الحركة إلى السواد منه ما هو أقرب إلى الإسوداد الذي هو الغاية في ذلك، وأسرع وصولاً إليه من اسوداد آخر إليه. وذهب الإمام وجمع من المحققين إلى أن ثبوت هاتين المقولتين إنما هو في الذهن، إذ لو وجدتا في الخارج لافتقر كل منهما إلى مؤثر له تأثير آخر ضرورة امتناع كون التأثير نفس الأثر على تقدير كونهما من الأعيان الخارجية، وحينئذ يلزم
He said: Among them are 'to act' (an yafʿala) and 'to be acted upon' (an yanfaʿila). They are the effect of something upon something, and the being-effected of something from something, as long as it continues in connection, like the state of the heater and the heated as long as it heats and is heated, not the state that results. That is, like after stability, like the height of a tree, the heat of water, and the standing of a human. Opposition and intensity occur in them. What was said that their existence is mental, otherwise infinite regress would follow, is refuted by the fact that what is meant by them is not absolute effectivity and passivity, such that it would include creation and instantaneous origination, but rather the state that belongs to the agent and patient, such that al-Farabi interpreted 'to act' as causing motion (taḥrīk) and 'to be acted upon' as being moved (taḥarruk). It is the effect of a thing upon another in continuous non-stationary connection, like the state of the heated as long as it is being heated. And 'to be acted upon' is the effect of a thing from another similarly, like that of the heated as long as it is being heated. As for the state obtained for the completed thing at stability, i.e., cessation of motion, like the height obtained for the tree, the heat obtained for water, the burning obtained for cloth, and the sitting or standing obtained for a human, it is not of this kind, even if it is only an effect or a passion; rather, it belongs to quantity, quality, position, or something else. Similarly the state obtained for the agent before and after the effect, like a power in fire called burning. In each of the two categories opposition occurs, for heating is opposite to cooling, and being heated is opposite to being cooled. They accept intensity and weakness, for the heating of fire is more intense than the heating of a hot stone; and blackening, which is the movement toward blackness, includes that which is closer to the blackness that is the goal thereof, and faster in reaching it, than another blackening toward it. The Imām (al-Ghazali) and a group of verifiers held that the existence of these two categories is only in the mind, because if they existed externally, each of them would need an effecter having another effect, due to the impossibility of the effect being the same as the effect itself assuming they are external entities; and then an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage defines the categories of action (an yafʿala) and passion (an yanfaʿila). It explains that they refer to the process of causing and receiving an effect while the action is ongoing, not the static state after completion. For example, heating and being heated are action and passion, but the resulting heat is a quality, not an action. The passage notes that these categories admit opposition (heating vs. cooling) and intensity (e.g., fire heats more than a hot stone). It then addresses an objection: some argued that these categories are only mental because if they were real, they would require an infinite regress of effects. The author refutes this by clarifying that the intended meaning is not absolute effectivity (which would include creation) but rather continuous process. Al-Farabi interpreted action as causing motion and passion as being moved. The passage then contrasts the ongoing state with the static state after completion (e.g., tree height, water heat, human standing), which belong to other categories. Finally, it reports that al-Ghazali (the Imām) and many verifiers held that these two categories exist only in the mind, not in external reality, because if they were external, each would need another effect, leading to infinite regress. The argument is cut off at 'then an infinite regress would follow' (يلزم).

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali and some verifiers held that action and passion exist only in the mind to avoid the infinite regress that would arise if they were real external entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage argues that action and passion are only mental constructs to avoid infinite regress. Have you ever considered that your daily actions—like the warmth of the sun or a kind word you spoke—might not be 'real' in the same way as physical objects? How does that challenge your instinctive belief in the independent reality of your deeds? What does that mean for your sense of responsibility before Allah?
?
Al-Ghazali's reasoning removes action and passion from external reality. If your 'actions' are just mental categories, does that make you less accountable for them? Or does it drive you to realize that every effect ultimately traces back to the Creator alone? Which thought settles your heart and makes you more mindful of His power?
?
The infinite regress argument demands that we stop the chain of causes somewhere. Where does your mind stop? Do you ever feel anxious about the causes behind your actions, or do you entrust them to Allah? What would it take for you to truly rest in the knowledge that He is the ultimate Cause?

Defining 'To Act' and 'To Be Acted Upon' as Non-Stationary States
Defines the two categories and distinguishes them from stable states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of 'to act' and 'to be acted upon' as non-stationary causal processes, how they differ from stationary states and instantaneous creation, and their properties of contrast and intensity.

Core Idea
To act and to be acted upon are not static; they are continuous processes of change.

This passage defines the two fundamental Aristotelian categories of action and passion as continuous, non-stationary processes. The author clarifies their scope, refutes an objection that they are merely mental, and distinguishes them from stable states and instantaneous events. Finally, he discusses how these categories admit contrast and intensity.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overall sense of the author's argument. Notice the examples of heating and blackening.
Pay attention to the phrase 'على اتصال غير قار' (non-stationary continuity).
2
Identify the key definitions
Locate the sentences defining 'to act' and 'to be acted upon'. Understand that they involve continuous effect, not instantaneous events.
The definition uses the example of something being heated while still heating.
3
Understand the objection and reply
The text addresses a potential objection: that these categories exist only in the mind. Note the author's refutation: they refer to specific states of agent and patient during change.
Al-Farabi is cited to support the interpretation.
4
Distinguish from stationary states
Compare the definitions with examples of stationary states (length, heat, standing). See why these are not considered 'to act' or 'to be acted upon' but belong to other categories.
Stationary states are results, not processes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي مثل بعد الاستقرار كطول الشجر، وسخونة الماء، وقيام الإنسان، ويجري فيها التضاد والاشداد. وما قيل إن ثبوتهما ذهني وإلا لزم التسلسل مدفوع بأن ليس المراد بهما مطلق التأثير والتأثر، بحيث يشمل الإبداع والحدوث الدفعي، بل الحال الذي يكون للفاعل والمنفعل حتى أن الفارابي فسر أن يفعل بالتحريك، وأن ينفعل بالتحرك. هو تأثير الشيء في غيره على اتصال غير قار كالحال الذي للمتسخن ما دام يتسخن وإن ينفعل هو تأثير الشيء عن غيره كذلك الذي للمتسخن ما دام يتسخن. وأما الحال الحاصل للمستكمل عند الاستقرار أي انقطاع الحركة عنه كالطول الحاصل للشجر، وكالسخونة الحاصلة للماء والاحتراق الحاصل للثوب، والقعود أو القيام الحاصل للإنسان، فليس من هذا القبيل، وإن كان فقط أثراً أو انفعالاً، بل من الكم أو الكيف أو الوضع أو غير ذلك، وكذلك الحال الحاصل للفاعل قبل التأثير وبعده كقوة للنار تسمى احتراقاً. ويجري في كل من المقولتين التضاد فإن التسخين ضد التبريد والتسخن ضد التبرد، ويقبلان الشدة والضعف، فإن تسخين النار أشد من تسخين الحجر الحار، والاسوداد الذي هو الحركة إلى السواد منه ما هو أقرب إلى الإسوداد الذي هو الغاية في ذلك، وأسرع وصولاً إليه من اسوداد آخر إليه
Namely, like after stability, like the length of the tree, the heat of water, and the standing of a person, and contrast and intensity occur in them. And what was said that their existence is mental, otherwise infinite regress would follow, is refuted by the fact that what is meant by them is not absolute affecting and being affected, such that it includes creation and instantaneous occurrence, but rather the state that belongs to the agent and patient, to the extent that al-Farabi interpreted 'to act' as causing motion, and 'to be acted upon' as being moved. It (i.e., 'to act') is the effect of one thing upon another in a non-stationary continuous manner, like the state of something being heated as long as it is being heated. And 'to be acted upon' is the effect of one thing from another likewise, like that of something being heated as long as it is being heated. As for the state obtained by the completed thing at stability, i.e., cessation of motion from it, like the length obtained for the tree, and the heat obtained for water, and the burning obtained for cloth, and the sitting or standing obtained for a person, it is not of this kind, even if it is only an effect or passion; rather, it belongs to quantity or quality or position or other. Similarly, the state obtained for the agent before and after the effect, like a power for fire called burning. And in each of the two categories, contrast occurs: for heating is contrary to cooling, and being heated is contrary to being cooled. And they accept intensity and weakness: for the heating of fire is more intense than the heating of hot stone, and blackening (al-iswidad) which is the movement to blackness, among it there is what is closer to the blackness that is the goal in that, and faster in reaching it than another blackening to it.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage defines the two Aristotelian categories of 'doing' (أن يفعل) and 'undergoing' (أن ينفعل) as non-stationary causal processes. It begins by giving examples of stable states (like length, heat, standing) that are not these categories, but clarifies that contrast and intensity apply to them. Then it refutes an objection that the categories are mental, stating that the categories refer specifically to the state of the agent and patient during a continuous change, not to absolute creation or instantaneous occurrence, citing al-Farabi's interpretation. It then gives the precise definition: 'to act' is the effect of one thing on another in a non-stationary continuous manner, like the state of something being heated while it is still heating; 'to be acted upon' is the effect from another likewise. It contrasts these with stable states obtained after the process is complete, such as the length of a tree after growth, which belong to other categories (quantity, quality, etc.). It also notes the state of the agent before and after, like fire's burning power. Finally, it discusses the contrast and intensity in these categories: heating is contrary to cooling, and they can be more or less intense, giving examples.

Misconception: The categories of 'to act' and 'to be acted upon' are merely mental constructs or include instantaneous creation.
Correction: They refer to real, continuous non-stationary processes that occur in the agent and patient during change, not to stable states or instantaneous events.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you experience a moment of spiritual 'heat' (e.g., in prayer or dhikr), do you let it cool into a fixed state of satisfaction, or do you strive to maintain it as a continuous 'being heated' that intensifies? What concrete step will you take to keep that process alive tomorrow?
?
The lesson distinguishes between a power before acting (like fire's burning potential) and the act itself. Are you harboring untapped spiritual capacities that you keep as mere potentials? What is one good deed you have postponed, and how will you move it from potential to continuous action this week?

Objection: Categories are Purely Mental
Al-Ghazali's objection against external existence of categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's objection that the categories of acting and being acted upon must be mental constructs to avoid infinite regress.

Core Idea
If acting were external, each act would need another act, leading to infinite regress.

Al-Ghazali and a group of investigators raise a powerful objection against assigning external reality to the categories of acting and being acted upon. They argue that making these categories external forces an infinite chain of causes, which is rationally impossible. This passage challenges us to reconsider the ontological status of fundamental theological categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms like 'ذهن' (mind) and 'خارج' (external).
Pay attention to the logical structure: if... then... leads to...
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Identify the conditional argument: if external existence, then infinite regress. Then understand why infinite regress is impossible (muhal).
The conclusion is that the categories are mental constructs.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for kalam. If categories are mental, then actions and effects are not independent entities but conceptual relations.
This connects to later discussions on divine attributes and causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الإمام وجمع من المحققين إلى أن ثبوت هاتين المقولتين إنما هو في الذهن، إذ لو وجدتا في الخارج لافتقر كل منهما إلى مؤثر له تأثير آخر ضرورة امتناع كون التأثير نفس الأثر على تقدير كونهما من الأعيان الخارجية، وحينئذ يلزم التسلسل المحال وترتب أمور لا نهاية لها مع كونها غير محصورة بين حاصرين
And the Imam (al-Ghazali) and a group of investigators held that the existence of these two categories is only mental, for if they existed externally, each of them would be in need of an agent having another effect, due to the impossibility that the effect be the same as the effect, on the assumption that they are among external entities. And then an impossible infinite regress would follow and the succession of things with no end, while they are not confined between two boundaries.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Imam al-Ghazali and a group of investigators against the external reality of the categories 'to act' and 'to be acted upon'. Their argument is that if these categories existed as external entities, every instance of acting would require another agent with a separate act, and every instance of being acted upon would require another patient with a separate passion. This is because it is impossible for the act itself to be identical to the effect if both are considered external objects. Consequently, an infinite regress of acts and passions would arise, which is impossible (muhal). Therefore, these categories must exist only in the mind (fi al-dhihn) as mental concepts, not in external reality.

Key Terms
الإمام
محققون
ثبوت
ذهن
خارج
تسلسل
محصور
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the objection applies only to causal relations in the physical world, not to theological categories.
Correction: The objection is specifically about the logical status of the categories of acting and being acted upon, which are used in kalam to discuss divine attributes and causation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's objection, why can the two categories not exist externally?
A) Because they are purely mental concepts.
B) Because they would require an infinite regress of agents and effects.
C) Because they are not real.
D) Because they are only in the mind of the observer.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's objection forces us to view the categories of acting and being acted upon as mental constructs to avoid the impossibility of an infinite causal chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ghazali argues that if 'acting' existed externally, it would require an infinite chain of agents. Since you believe yourself to be an agent capable of initiating actions, does this argument challenge your assumption that your will is truly independent? Will you spend time contemplating the mental nature of your actions and rely on Allah as the sole agent?
?
The lesson concludes that categories are only mental. How does this affect your understanding of praise and blame? When you are praised for a good deed, do you think of the deed as an external reality that increases your worth, or as a mental concept that Allah created? Will you correct yourself when you feel proud by reminding yourself that 'acting' is a mental construct?
?
Consider your relationships: you often see yourself as the one who acts upon others (by helping or hurting) or the one who is acted upon (by receiving kindness or injustice). Al-Ghazali's proof shows that such categories cannot be external without absurdity. Does this insight help you detach from a victim mentality or from pride in your influence? Will you commit to viewing these interactions as mental constructs that point you back to Allah?

The Response: Distinguishing Instantaneous from Gradual Causation
Sanusi refutes the infinite regress objection by distinguishing instantaneous from gradual causation, citing al-Farabi on motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response refutes the infinite regress objection by distinguishing instantaneous creation from gradual change, supported by al-Farabi's analysis of 'to act' and 'to be acted upon'.

Core Idea
Not all causation is gradual; instantaneous creation escapes the categories that lead to regress.

The objection claimed that 'to act' and 'to be acted upon' are separate entities leading to infinite regress. Here, Sanusi provides the answer by clarifying that these categories only apply to continuous, gradual change, not to instantaneous creation. He supports this with al-Farabi's classification of motion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the response
Notice the conditional opening: 'That would only follow if...'. This sets up the refutation by showing the objection's assumption is false.
The response begins with a hypothetical conditional to frame the distinction.
2
Examine the distinction between instantaneous and gradual causation
Identify the key contrast between 'creative' (ibdā‘ī) and 'instantaneous' (daf‘ī) causation versus continuous change. The categories only apply to the latter.
This distinction is the core of the refutation.
3
Analyze al-Farabi's equation of 'to act' with change and motion
Al-Farabi defines 'to act' as changing/causing motion, and 'to be acted upon' as being changed/moved. They are not separate entities but the very process of change.
This supports the idea that categories are real only within gradual change.
4
See the application through examples and species of motion
The passage lists four species of motion (generation/corruption, growth/decay, alteration, translation) and gives examples like house construction and heating. Understand that each 'to be acted upon' has a corresponding 'to act'.
This shows the symmetry and prevents infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب: إن ذلك إنما يلزم لو كان كل تأثير وإيجاد حتى الإبداعي الذي لا يفتقر إلى زمان من قبيل أن يفعل وكل تأثر وحصول حتى الدفعي من قبيل أن ينفعل وليس كذلك بل إذا كان الفاعل يغير المنفعل من حال إلى حال على الاتصال والاستمرار فحال الفاعل هو أن يفعل وحال المنفعل أن ينفعل حتى فسر الفارابي أن يفعل بالتغيير والتحريك، وأن ينفعل بالتغير والتحرك. وقال لا فرق بين قولنا ينفعل وبين قولنا يتغير ويتحرك وأنواع هذا الجنس هي أنواع الحركة، ففي الجوهر الكون والفساد، وفي الكم النمو والاضمحلال وفي الكيف الاستحالة، وفي الأين النقلة. وحقيقة أن ينفعل هو تصير الجوهر من شيء إلى شيء، وتغيره من أمر إلى أمر ما دام سالكاً بين الأمرين على الاتصال، فالتكون كابتناء البيت قليلا قليلا وشيئاً شيئاً، وجزءاً جزءاً على اتصال إلى أن يحصل البيت. وعلى هذا قياس البواقي. وأن يفعل هو أن ينتقل الفاعل باتصال الفعل على النسب التي له أجزاء ما يحدثه في المنفعل حين ما ينفعل، فالمسخن حين ما يسخن له نسبته إلى جزء جزء من الحرارة التي تحدث فيما يتسخن ينتقل من نسبته إلى جزء من الحرارة إلى نسبته إلى جزء آخر على الاتصال، وأنواعه على عدد أنواع أن ينفعل فان كل تغير وحركة يقابله تغير وتحريك كالتكوين للتكون، والإفساد للفساد وكذا أنواع الأنواع كالبناء للابتناء والهدم للانهدام وعلى هذا قياس
And the answer: That would only follow if every effect and bringing into existence, even the creative (ibdā‘ī) which does not require time, were of the type of 'to act', and every being affected and occurrence, even the instantaneous (daf‘ī), were of the type of 'to be acted upon'. But it is not so. Rather, when the agent changes the patient from state to state continuously and perpetually, then the state of the agent is 'to act' and the state of the patient is 'to be acted upon', to the extent that al-Farabi interpreted 'to act' as changing and causing motion, and 'to be acted upon' as being changed and being moved. And he said: There is no difference between our saying 'it is acted upon' and our saying 'it changes and moves', and the species of this genus are the species of motion: in substance it is generation and corruption, in quantity growth and decay, in quality alteration, in place translation. And the reality of 'to be acted upon' is the becoming of the substance from something to something, and its change from one state to another as long as it is proceeding between the two states continuously. So 'becoming' (takawn) is like the construction of a house little by little, thing by thing, part by part, continuously until the house is obtained. And upon this is the measurement of the rest. And 'to act' is that the agent moves by the continuity of the action in the relations that it has to the parts of what it produces in the patient at the time when the patient is acted upon. So the heater, when it heats, has a relation to each part of the heat that is produced in what is being heated; it moves from its relation to one part of heat to its relation to another part continuously. And its species are equal to the number of species of 'to be acted upon', for every change and motion is faced with a change and causing motion, like 'making be' (takwīn) for 'becoming' (takawwun), and 'corrupting' (ifsād) for 'corruption' (fasād), and likewise the species of species, like building (binā') for being built (ibtinā'), and demolition (hadm) for being demolished (inhidām). And upon this is the measurement.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the previous objection. It distinguishes between two types of causation: creative (ibdā‘ī) and instantaneous (daf‘ī) which do not involve time, and gradual change which involves continuity and temporal extension. The objection would only apply if all acts and passions were of the continuous, gradual type. But not all are; instantaneous creation is not of the type 'to act' as defined. The passage then explains that 'to act' and 'to be acted upon' refer specifically to the states of agent and patient during a continuous change. It then cites al-Farabi who equates 'to act' with changing/causing motion, and 'to be acted upon' with being changed/moved, identifying these categories with motion and its species (generation/corruption, growth/decay, alteration, translation). It elaborates on the reality of 'to be acted upon' as the gradual becoming of a substance, and 'to act' as the agent's continuous relating to parts of the effect. This response shows that the categories are real and not subject to infinite regress because they are not about external entities separate from the change itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The response denies the reality of 'to act' and 'to be acted upon' categories entirely.
Correction: The response affirms these categories but limits them to gradual change; it denies that they apply to instantaneous creation.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the objection's infinite regress not apply?
A) Because the categories exist only mentally.
B) Because not all causation is gradual; instantaneous creation is not covered.
C) Because the categories are not real.
D) Because the objection was made by al-Farabi.

Key Takeaway
The response saves the categories by limiting them to gradual change, where they represent the agent's continuous action and patient's continuous reception, thus avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you believe that your spiritual transformation must happen gradually, or can God grant you an instantaneous turning point (ibdā‘) right now? What is one area of your life where you have been waiting for a slow change, when God is offering you an immediate reset?
?
The passage describes the heater producing heat part by part. In your relationship with God, do you tend to see His blessings as earned in pieces, or do you recognize that some gifts—like guidance or repentance—are given instantly? How does that change your sense of gratitude and urgency?
?
When you read that 'to act' and 'to be acted upon' are just two sides of the same continuous process, what does that reveal about your own passivity? Are you waiting to be 'acted upon' (yanfa‘il) without actively seeking to be changed? What will you do today to move from being a passive receiver to an active participant in your own transformation?

Logic and Technical Terms

Contrariety in the Categories of Action and Passion
Enumeration of examples of contraries in action and passion categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how contrariety applies to both the passive and active forms of the categories of action and passion.

Core Idea
Contraries exist in both what is done and what is undergone.

This passage begins the discussion of categories of action and passion by illustrating the concept of contrariety through concrete examples. These examples show that contrariety is found in both the passive aspect (e.g., being built vs. being destroyed) and the active aspect (e.g., building vs. destroying). Mastering this foundation is essential before moving to Ibn Sina's terminological precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the contrariety examples
Read the Arabic sentence that lists the examples of contraries. Notice the patterns: 'yanhadim' vs. 'yabtani' and 'yahdim' vs. 'yabni' etc.
2
Identify the pairs
List each pair of contraries: being destroyed vs. being built, being heated vs. being cooled, destroying vs. building, heating vs. cooling.
Notice the symmetry between passive and active forms.
3
Generalize the principle
The author states 'and on this is the analogy of the rest,' meaning all contrary pairs in these categories follow this pattern.
This shows that contrariety is a universal feature of these categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التضاد، فكما أن ينهدم مضاد لأن يبتني، وأن يتسخن لأن يتبرد، وكذلك أن يهدم مضاد لأن يبتني، وأن يسخن لأن يبرد، وعلى هذا قياس البواقي.
وقال ابن سينا: إنما أؤثر لفظ أن ينفعل وأن يفعل على الانفعال والفعل لأنهما قد يقالان للحاصل بعد انقطاع الحركة، وإنما المقولة ما كان توجها إلى غاية من وضع أو كيف أو غير ذلك مستقر من حيث هو [كلك؟]. ولفظ أن ينفعل وأن يفعل مخصوص بذلك.
Contrariety: just as that it is destroyed (yanhadim) is contrary to that it is built (yabtani), and that it becomes hot (yatasakhkhan) is contrary to that it becomes cold (yatabarrud), likewise that it destroys (yahdim) is contrary to that it builds (yabni), and that it heats (yaskhun) is contrary to that it cools (yabrud); and on this is the analogy of the rest.
Ibn Sina said: I prefer the expression 'that it is acted upon' (an yanfa'il) and 'that it acts' (an yaf'al) over 'passion' (al-infial) and 'action' (al-fi'l) because they (the latter) are sometimes said of the result after the cessation of motion, while the category (al-maqula) is that which is a direction towards a goal of position or quality or other such, being established as such [?]. And the expression 'that it is acted upon' and 'that it acts' is specifically for that.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents the tail end of a discussion on contraries within the categories of action and passion, followed by Ibn Sina's terminological refinement. First, the author gives examples of contraries in these categories: destruction is contrary to construction, heating contrary to cooling, both in the passive sense (being destroyed vs. being built) and the active sense (destroying vs. building). This establishes that contrariety applies to both the passive and active forms. Then Ibn Sina is quoted to clarify the correct linguistic expression for these categories. He states that he prefers to use the subjunctive forms 'an yanfa'il' (that it be acted upon) and 'an yaf'al' (that it acts) over the masdar (verbal noun) forms 'al-infial' (the being acted upon) and 'al-fi'l' (the acting). The reason is that the latter (the masdars) may refer to the resulting state after the motion (of change) has ceased, whereas the category (al-maqula) itself is defined as a direction or tendency towards a goal—whether of position, quality, or something else—that is stable as such. Therefore, the forms with 'an' specifically indicate the ongoing process or orientation, making them more appropriate for naming the categories. The passage thereby emphasizes the importance of precise terminology in philosophical discussions of categories.

Key Terms
تضاد
ينهدم
يبتني
يتسخن
يتبّرد
يهدم
يبني
يسخن
يبرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contrariety only applies to the active form of an action, not the passive.
Correction: The examples show contrariety applies equally to both the passive (e.g., being destroyed vs. being built) and the active (e.g., destroying vs. building) forms.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of contrariety in the active sense given in the passage?
A) Being built and being destroyed
B) Building and destroying
C) Being heated and being cooled
D) Becoming hot and becoming cold

Key Takeaway
Contrariety is a fundamental feature of both the passive and active dimensions of the categories of action and passion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship in your life that is currently being 'destroyed' (yanhadim) and one that you are actively 'destroying' (yahdim). Which of these are you responsible for, and how does recognizing the contrariety between building and destroying compel you to change your actions starting today?
?
You experience both being 'heated' (yatasakhkhan) by anger and 'cooling' (yabrud) in forgiveness. In which of these states do you usually reside? How does the lesson's demonstration of contrariety in both active and passive forms challenge you to take responsibility for your emotional states?

Ibn Sina's Preference for 'An Yaf'al' and 'An Yanfa'il'
Ibn Sina's argument for preferring verbal noun forms over masdars for categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina favors the subjunctive verbal forms over the masdars for naming the categories of action and passion.

Core Idea
The category is a direction, not a finished result.

After illustrating contrariety, Ibn Sina offers a crucial terminological refinement. He argues that the correct terms for the categories of action and passion should indicate an ongoing process or direction toward a goal, rather than a completed state. This distinction highlights the philosophical depth behind what might seem like a linguistic choice.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's statement
Read the Arabic sentence from 'Wa qala Ibn Sina...' to the end. Notice his preference for 'an yaf'al' and 'an yanfa'il'.
2
Understand the reason
Ibn Sina gives a reason: the masdars can refer to the result after motion stops, while the category is a direction towards a goal.
Think of 'al-fi'l' as the act done, finished, and 'an yaf'al' as the act in progress.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows that the categories are about tendencies, not static outcomes.
This precision prevents confusion in philosophical discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التضاد، فكما أن ينهدم مضاد لأن يبتني، وأن يتسخن لأن يتبرد، وكذلك أن يهدم مضاد لأن يبتني، وأن يسخن لأن يبرد، وعلى هذا قياس البواقي.
وقال ابن سينا: إنما أؤثر لفظ أن ينفعل وأن يفعل على الانفعال والفعل لأنهما قد يقالان للحاصل بعد انقطاع الحركة، وإنما المقولة ما كان توجها إلى غاية من وضع أو كيف أو غير ذلك مستقر من حيث هو [كلك؟]. ولفظ أن ينفعل وأن يفعل مخصوص بذلك.
Contrariety: just as that it is destroyed (yanhadim) is contrary to that it is built (yabtani), and that it becomes hot (yatasakhkhan) is contrary to that it becomes cold (yatabarrud), likewise that it destroys (yahdim) is contrary to that it builds (yabni), and that it heats (yaskhun) is contrary to that it cools (yabrud); and on this is the analogy of the rest.

And the expression 'that it is acted upon' and 'that it acts' is specifically for that.

Commentary

Therefore, the forms with 'an' specifically indicate the ongoing process or orientation, making them more appropriate for naming the categories. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn Sina's preference is merely a grammatical preference without deeper significance.
Correction: His choice is based on the philosophical distinction between a process (ongoing direction) and the product (completed result).

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why does he prefer 'an yaf'al' and 'an yanfa'il' over the masdars?
A) Because the masdars are grammatically incorrect.
B) Because the masdars refer to the result after motion ceases, while the category involves direction toward a goal.
C) Because the subjunctive forms are shorter.
D) Because the masdars are ambiguous in number.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's terminological precision reminds us that category names should reflect the dynamic orientation of actions, not their static results.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily actions, do you focus more on the completed result (like the masdar) or on the ongoing direction and intention (like 'an yaf'al')? Which one does your heart actually consider as the category of your action?
?
When you evaluate your character, do you look at the static state of how you appear, or at the dynamic orientation of your will towards good? Ibn Sina's distinction challenges us: are you more concerned with having arrived or with the direction you are moving?
?
Is there a specific habit or relationship where you treat the 'category' as a finished product rather than a continuous striving? What would change today if you started seeing it as a direction towards a goal?
